Received 
      3rd March 2019
    , Accepted 25th April 2019First published on 25th April 2019AbstractSemiconductor polymeric graphitic carbon nitride (g-C3N4) photocatalysts have attracted dramatically growing attention in the field of the visible-light-induced hydrogen evolution reaction (HER) because of their facile synthesis, easy functionalization, attractive electronic band structure, high physicochemical stability and photocatalytic activity. This review article presents a panorama of the latest advancements in the rational design and development of g-C3N4 and g-C3N4-based composite photocatalysts for HER application. Concretely, the review starts with the development history, synthetic strategy, electronic structure and physicochemical characteristics of g-C3N4 materials, followed by the rational design and engineering of various nanostructured g-C3N4 (e.g. thinner, highly crystalline, doped, and porous g-C3N4) photocatalysts for HER application. Then a series of highly efficient g-C3N4 (e.g., metal/g-C3N4, semiconductor/g-C3N4, metal organic framework/g-C3N4, carbon/g-C3N4, conducting polymer/g-C3N4, sensitizer/g-C3N4) composite photocatalysts are exemplified. Lastly, this review provides a comprehensive summary and outlook on the major challenges, opportunities, and inspiring perspectives for future research in this hot area on the basis of pioneering works. It is believed that the emerging g-C3N4-based photocatalysts will act as the “holy grail” for highly efficient photocatalytic HER under visible-light irradiation.
Guangfu Liao
Guangfu Liao received his MSc in Polymer Materials and Engineering from Hubei University in 2017. He then joined Prof. Haiyang Gao's group at Sun Yat-sen University to pursue PhD in Materials Science. His primary research is focused on Ag-based catalysts, nanocomposites, biomaterials, and coordination polymerization. He has more than 6 years of research experience in the fields of catalysis, environment and biomaterials. He has published over 20 SCI papers, and also received some awards including the Graduate Student National Scholarship, Hubei University First-class Academic Scholarship, Hubei University Outstanding Graduate & Excellent Graduation Thesis, and Sun Yat-sen University Second-class Academic Scholarship.

Currently, she is working in Prof. Da Zhan’ research group at Xiamen University to pursue her master's degree in Materials Science. Her primary research is focused on the development of high performance graphite-like C3N4 nanosheets for visible-light-driven photocatalysis applications.

His research interests are devoted to designing and fabricating various organic/inorganic hybrid nanomaterials, and investigating their applications especially in photo-induced nanomedicine.

He then worked as a JSPS research fellow (Japan), Lise Meitner scientist (Austria), and Research Professor (Korea), and is a Senior Scientist at the University of British Columbia (Canada). Broader context
Exploitation of clean and renewable energy is a crucial strategy to satisfy the growing global energy demands and solve the environmental problems caused by the over-consumption of fossil fuels. Photocatalytic hydrogen production from water splitting by solar energy has been widely recognized as a promising avenue for sustainable development. Semiconductor polymeric graphitic carbon nitride (g-C3N4) photocatalysts have attracted dramatically growing interest in the research area of the visible-light-induced hydrogen evolution reaction (HER) because of their facile synthesis, easy functionalization, attractive electronic band structure, high physicochemical stability and particularly unique visible-light responsiveness, which are believed to be the “holy grail” for photocatalytic HER. To effectively reduce the recombination rate of charge carriers and simultaneously enhance visible-light absorption above 460 nm, a series of nanostructured g-C3N4 photocatalysts (e.g., thinner, highly crystalline, doped, and porous g-C3N4) and highly efficient g-C3N4 composite photocatalysts (e.g., metal/g-C3N4, semiconductor/g-C3N4, metal organic framework/g-C3N4, carbon/g-C3N4, etc.) have been explored. They can effectively modify the electronic band structure of g-C3N4, widen the light absorption range and facilitate the separation of photo-generated electron–hole pairs, leading to considerably reduced recombination rate of charge carriers, enlarging visible light harvesting and utilization, and thus significantly improving HER activity under visible-light irradiation.

1. Introduction
The exploitation of clean and renewable energy is a crucial strategy to satisfy the growing global energy demands and to solve the energy shortage problem resulting from the over-consumption of fossil fuels.1–4 With the development of science and technology, photocatalytic HER in water by solar energy has been generally regarded as a promising and sustainable strategy to generate hydrogen (H2) because solar energy and water are inexhaustible on the earth.5–10 In the past few decades, various semiconductor materials, e.g., TiO2,11 ZnO,12 Fe2O3,13 SnO2,14 ZrO2,15 Ag3PO4,16 Ag3VO4,17 Bi2WO6,18 BiVO4,19 CdS,20 NiS,21etc., have been reported for photocatalytic HER under UV and visible-light illumination. Thus far, developing highly efficient semiconductor photocatalysts for HER under UV particularly visible-light irradiation has become a hot research topic. Unfortunately, metal oxide semiconductors possess a wide band gap (e.g., TiO2 ∼ 3.2 eV, ZnO ∼ 3.4 eV, WO3 ∼ 3.2 eV, and BiVO4 ∼ 2.9 eV) (Table 1), which severely limits their photocatalytic applications because of the low utilization of solar energy. Metal sulfides, metal phosphides, and metal nitrides (e.g., CdS ∼ 2.4 eV, SnS2 ∼ 1.7 eV, Ni2P ∼ 1.0 eV, InP ∼ 1.4 eV, InN ∼ 1.1 eV, etc.) (Table 1) have also been taken into account since they possess a narrow band gap with suitable band levels for photocatalytic HER; however, photocorrosion and self-oxidation make them generally deleterious and volatile which limit their further application.22 In the development of robust visible-light-active photocatalysts for the HER, g-C3N4, a polymeric semiconductor, has gradually opened up a new field of vision in photocatalytic HER since the pioneering study in 2009 by Wang and co-workers23 on the visible-light irradiated photocatalytic HER over g-C3N4, which is regarded as a visible-light-active metal-free polymeric semiconductor with a narrow band gap (ca. 2.7 eV), an appropriate optical wavelength (ca. 460 nm), and a suitable band structure for photocatalytic HER. Moreover, it also possesses excellent thermal stability (>600 °C in air) and chemical stability (against acid, alkali, and organic solvents).24 Furthermore, g-C3N4 can be simply generated by direct pyrolysis of earth-abundant N-rich precursors, e.g., melamine,25 dicyandiamide,26 cyanamide,27 urea,28 thiourea,29 and ammonium thiocyanate,30 and it can be readily functionalized via chemical exfoliation.31 In addition, it can also be employed to construct various highly tailorable composite photocatalysts with adjustable compositions, sizes, thicknesses, pore structures, size distributions, and morphologies.32 In view of its unique advantages including facile synthesis, fascinating electronic band structure, excellent physicochemical stability, “earth-abundant” characteristic, and easy functionalization,33–35 g-C3N4 is believed to be the “holy grail” as the next generation photocatalysts for HER application. However, its practical application for photocatalytic HER is still impeded due to the existing obstacles and shortcomings including high recombination rate of charge carriers, low electrical conductivity, and the lack of visible-light absorption above 460 nm.36 Therefore, the development of modified g-C3N4-based photocatalysts with enhanced electrical conductivity, enlarged photoharvesting, reduced recombination rate and improved photocatalytic properties is essential to achieve target-specific applications. Strategies such as synthesizing thinner, highly crystalline, doped, and porous g-C3N4 based materials have been applied to improve the photocatalytic properties of g-C3N4 photocatalysts.37–39 In addition to the rapid development of these advanced techniques for intrinsic g-C3N4, the preparation of g-C3N4 composite heterojunctions can also improve the photocatalytic properties of g-C3N4-based photocatalysts.40,41

Over the past few years, researchers have witnessed a booming trend in the exploration of g-C3N4-based photocatalysts for HER application. There is a blowout growth in the number of published papers after the first landmark report on g-C3N4 photocatalysts for H2 production in 2009, and there have been much more publications on g-C3N4 photocatalysts for HER application during the last two years, which implies the fundamentality and attractiveness of this emerging frontier. More importantly, novel g-C3N4-based photocatalysts with high photocatalytic performance are becoming a new family of next generation visible-light-tailored photocatalysts for HER application.
Despite the dramatic growth in the number of publications on g-C3N4-based photocatalysts, it still remains a question about how to fully explore the unusual features of g-C3N4 for the fabrication of highly efficient g-C3N4-based photocatalysts. Thus, it is particularly important to take full advantages of the fascinating features of g-C3N4, especially the visible-light responsiveness, which can effectively improve the solar utilization efficiency. The attractive characteristics of g-C3N4 allow us to fully explore novel highly efficient visible-light-active photocatalysts, but to this end, an illustrated overview of the recent advancements and future research efforts in g-C3N4-based HER photocatalysts is highly desired.
With such a fleetly increasing number of publications in g-C3N4-based photocatalysts for HER application, especially in the past two years, it is an opportune moment to offer a comprehensive review on g-C3N4-based HER photocatalysts particularly investigated under visible-light irradiation. Up to now, although there have been many inspiring reviews on g-C3N4-based photocatalysts, a comprehensive review focusing on g-C3N4-based photocatalysts for HER is absent, particularly there is no review focusing on photocatalytic HER under visible-light irridation.22,61–71 Rational design and engineering of highly efficient g-C3N4-based HER photocatalysts are very important but were rarely addressed in these reviews. In consideration of the rapid increase in the number of papers on g-C3N4-based HER photocatalysts since 2016 and their fascinating properties particularly the high visible-light response capability, a comprehensive and updated review is highly worth considering. This review focuses mainly on the rational design and engineering of highly efficient g-C3N4-based photocatalysts for HER application. Specifically, the development history, synthesis approaches, electronic structures and physicochemical characteristics of g-C3N4-based nanostructured materials will be introduced first. Then a diverse range of design and configuration strategies for nanostructured g-C3N4 (e.g., thinner, highly crystalline, doped, and porous g-C3N4) photocatalysts for HER application are elaborated. Later, a series of representative g-C3N4 (e.g., metal/g-C3N4, semiconductor/-g-C3N4, metal organic framework/g-C3N4, carbon/g-C3N4, conducting polymer/g-C3N4, sensitizer/g-C3N4) composite HER photocatalysts are exemplified. Last but not least, this review will provide advanced insights into the major challenges, opportunities, and inspiring perspectives for future researches in this hot area.
2. g-C3N4 as highly efficient photocatalysts for HER application
2.1. A brief development history of g-C3N4
The development history of g-C3N4 and its precursors could be dated back to 1834 when Liebig found melamine, melam, melem, and melon for the first time, which were deemed to be heptazine- and triazine-based molecular compounds (Scheme 1). Encouraged by this report, a yellow amorphous and undissolved compound, namely melon, was prepared via the direct pyrolysis of ammonium chloride (NH4Cl) with potassium thiocyanate (KSCN). However, melon and the other molecular compounds could not be fully characterized in that era. In 1835, Gmelin discovered potassium hydromelonate (K3C6N7NCN) by the direct pyrolysis of potassium ferricyanide (K3[Fe(CN)6]) and sulfur in a crucible.72 Unfortunately, the compositions and structures of C3N4 and K3C6N7NCN were still indistinct because of lack of advanced analytical methods and technologies. An acoplanar tri-s-triazine unit was an elementary structural unit of all polymeric derivatives, as shown by an X-ray crystallography study, which was first proposed by Pauling and Sturdivant in 1937.73 Three years later, Redemann and Lucas74 found that 2,5,8 triamino-tris-s-triazine (C126H21N175) could be an oligomeric product. However, combined with the discoveries of the predecessors, they boldly speculated that this structure did not belong to melon, because it consisted of multifarious nanostructures and sizes. In 1982, the first single crystal structure of cyameluric derivatives (C6N7H3) was proposed by Leonard and co-workers,75 which was prepared through a bottom-up assembly strategy using 2,4-diamino-1,3,5-triazine and methyl N-cyanomethanimidate as the raw materials. They confirmed a coplanar arrangement as Pauling and Sturdivant reported.
 Scheme 1  Molecular structures of C- and N-containing materials such as melamine, melam, melem, and melon synthesized from the pyrolysis of mercury(II) thiocyanate as described by Liebig. 
Although C3N4 was discovered very early, it had been forgotten for a long time because of its chemical inertness and poor solubility. Surprisingly, the research enthusiasm about C3N4 was reactivated and reinspired due to a landmark discovery by Teter and Hemley in 1996, that is the compact phase of sp3-hybridized C3N4 (namely β-C3N4) showed ultrahigh bulk rigidity and modulus, which could be as good as or even greater than that of diamond.76 Teter et al.76 found that C3N4 possessed seven phases, which were α-C3N4, β-C3N4, cubic C3N4, pseudocubic C3N4, g-h-triazine, g-otriazine, and g-h-heptazine with a band gap of ca. 5.49, 4.85, 4.30, 4.13, 2.97, 0.93, and 2.88 eV, respectively. The basic structural units to construct allotropes of g-C3N4 were confirmed to be triazine (C3N3) or tri-s-triazine (C6N7) rings (Scheme 2).24,77–81 Many studies showed that the thermal polymerization of melamine, cyanamide, dicyandiamide, or urea produced a melon polymer from the melem units, that is to say, tri-s-triazine was usually deemed to be a building unit for the construction of g-C3N4. Under such conditions, the different sizes of nitride pores and the different electronic environments of N atoms help to form various energetic phases. Among these seven phases, tri-s-triazine based g-C3N4 was energetically advantageous and it was the most robust phase of C3N4, and thus it has been widely studied in many areas, especially in the field of photocatalysis. In 2009, the landmark discovery of g-C3N4 as a metal-free polymeric semiconductor photocatalyst for HER was first proposed by Wang and co-workers.23,37 Since then the research on g-C3N4 as a highly efficient metal-free photocatalyst has attracted increasing attention.
 Scheme 2  Triazine (a) and tri-s-triazine (b) structures of g-C3N4. 
In spite of the fact that g-C3N4 has been proverbially and deeply researched, it is still not easy to engineer faultless crystalline g-C3N4 from a material structural point of view. Only a small number of studies involved highly crystalline g-C3N4, and their results are still controversial. For example, Komatsu et al.82,83 reported highly crystalline g-C3N4 and considered it to be “high-molecular-weight” melon. Schnick and co-workers84,85 portended that the chemical fabrication of high crystal packing of g-C3N4 had a great possibility for isolating and identifying the crystal structures of heptazine derivatives, melem and melam. McMillan and co-workers86 reported the conversion of dicyandiamide to a crystalline g-C3N4 imide phase under high-pressure and high-temperature conditions. Thomas's group87 prepared highly crystalline g-C3N4 through a facile self-polymerization of dicyandiamide in a salt melt of LiCl and KCl. Very recently, Xue and co-workers88 prepared highly crystalline g-C3N4 by a general and rapid microwave-assisted heating method. Hu and co-workers89 fabricated highly crystalline g-C3N4 nanosheets through an “alternated cooling and heating” strategy. However, in the present stage, we still cannot obtain faultless crystalline g-C3N4 because the defect-containing polymeric g-C3N4 is much more reactive as confirmed by many studies. For most heterogeneous catalysts, crystalline perfection only helps to promote bulk properties, e.g., the graphitic structure, high thermal and chemical stability, and semiconductor electronic structure, etc. More importantly, in our opinion, deeply understanding how to prepare polymeric g-C3N4 by using various synthesis strategies, e.g., supramolecular pre-assembly strategy, microwave-assisted strategy, template-mediated strategy, etc., which can produce large pore volume and specific surface area, or even highly crystalline g-C3N4, is much more important than the exploration for single crystalline perfection.
2.2. Synthesis methods, electronic structures and physicochemical properties of g-C3N4

2.2.1. Synthesis methods. 
Since Wang and co-workers23,37 first reported g-C3N4 as a promising visible-light-tailored photocatalyst for HER in 2009, massive efforts have been made to prepare g-C3N4 mainly via pyrolysis of N-rich precursors, e.g., urea, thiourea, melamine, cyanamide, dicyandiamide, etc.90–96 For example, Chen and co-workers97 synthesized porous g-C3N4 through direct pyrolysis of urea. Li and co-workers98 synthesized a g-C3N4 photocatalyst by direct thermal treatment of low-cost melamine. Wang and co-workers99 synthesized g-C3N4 semiconductors via direct thermal polycondensation of thiourea. Melamine and melam could be polymerized via solid state reactions at high temperature and the in-planar alignment of CN heterocycles therefore resulted in 2D atomic layers that were stacked with each other by the intermolecular interaction to form a 3D architecture.100–102 Liquid precursors, cyanamide and dicyandiamide, could also be adopted to construct g-C3N4 in two steps: first condensed to melamine at 240 °C and then underwent polycondensation to yield g-C3N4.103–105 Deficiently, the photocatalytic properties of the g-C3N4 fabricated via direct pyrolysis of N-rich precursors are weak due to the fast recombination of the photogenerated electron–hole pairs.62,106,107 Therefore, new synthesis methods must be pursued to tackle this issue. Detailed descriptions about the various strategies for the construction of g-C3N4 are given below, and a comparison about the advantages and disadvantages of different methods is summarized in Table 2.
Table 2 Comparison between different methods for the construction of g-C3N4




Synthesis methods
Advantages
Disadvantages




Supramolecular pre-assembly strategy
Produced g-C3N4 with an ordered texture and controllable morphology
(i) Low efficiency, time- and cost-consuming, and sophisticated


(ii) Produced g-C3N4 with a relatively low crystallinity


Microwave-assisted heating strategy
(i) Facile, time-saving, and highly efficient
Produced g-C3N4 without an ordered texture, controllable morphology and porous structure


(ii) Produced g-C3N4 with a relatively high crystallinity, thinner layer, and decreased structural imperfections


Template-assisted strategy
Produced g-C3N4 with porous structure and high crystallinity
Time- and cost-consuming and complicated preparation process


Molten salt strategy
Produced g-C3N4 with high crystallinity
Time- and cost-consuming and complicated preparation process


Ionic liquid strategy
Produced g-C3N4 with porous structure, thinner layer, and high crystallinity

(i) Facile, time-saving, and highly efficient

(ii) Produced g-C3N4 without an ordered texture, controllable morphology and porous structure

(i) Facile, eco-friendly, and highly efficient
Complicated preparation process


(ii) Produced g-C3N4 with a relatively high crystallinity, and thinner layer


Fast flash frozen strategy
(i) Fast, high effect, and eco-friendly
Complicated preparation process and expansive equipment


(ii) Produced g-C3N4 with a relatively high crystallinity




2.2.1.1. Supramolecular pre-assembly strategy for g-C3N4 preparation. 
As mentioned above, g-C3N4 is usually synthesized via direct pyrolysis of the N-rich precursors, e.g., dicyanadiamide, cyanamide, melamine, urea, and thiourea at 500–600 °C in an air or inert atmosphere.61 At a high temperature, these N-rich precursors can be directly pyrolyzed into g-C3N4 accompanied by various intermediates. However, these intermediates cannot be completely dislodged from the resultant g-C3N4 systems because of incomplete polycondensation, leading to a disordered g-C3N4 structure.108,109 Therefore, it is necessary to explore new strategies to synthesize well-organized g-C3N4.
Supramolecular pre-assembly is a procedure in which molecules are arranged into stable aggregates via non-covalent bond interactions under equilibrium conditions.110,111 Among these non-covalent bond interactions hydrogen bonding is one of the most important interactions in arranging the structure of supramolecular aggregates because of its directionality and specificity.112,113 It is noteworthy that the melamine precursor can connect with triazine derivatives (e.g., cyanuric acid) to form supramolecular aggregates via hydrogen bond interactions. The obtained melamine–cyanuric acid complex can employ different linkages mainly relying on the solvent, forming a melamine–cyanuric acid complex with different morphologies.114 The adoption of melamine–cyanuric acid complexes as starting materials for the preparation of g-C3N4 was first proposed by Thomas's group115 and Shalom's group.116 Using melamine–cyanuric acid complexes as the start materials for the fabrication of g-C3N4 is much more advantageous than the traditional melamine precursor. Firstly, melamine encounters heavy distillation during the heat polycondensation process; the good news is that the organized structure of the melamine–cyanuric acid complex can reduce or even restrain the distillation process. Secondly, the supramolecular structure of the melamine–cyanuric acid complex is akin to the natural arrangement in g-C3N4 building blocks, which ensures the perfect arrangement of g-C3N4 in the self-assembly process. The above two merits provide an intact and ordered network in the obtained g-C3N4, resulting in good photocatalytic properties. Thirdly, this supramolecular pre-assembly approach is very simple and highly reproducible. The shape of the obtained melamine–cyanuric acid complex mainly depends on the used solvent, temperature, and condensation times due to the different surface energies of the hydrogen bond structures. Interestingly, g-C3N4 prepared through supramolecular aggregates can provide improved photocatalytic activity because of its enhanced structure intactness.
Although the supramolecular pre-assembly method can produce g-C3N4 with a relatively ordered structure, the solid-state reaction limits the flexibility of the reaction intermediates and causes an incomplete polycondensation with relatively low crystallinity. Therefore, it is very important to search for a new supramolecular pre-assembly approach for the rational design of g-C3N4 with enhanced crystallinity. 2,4,6-Triaminopyrimidine, as a member of the nucleobase family, is of particular interest to researchers because it possesses a higher carbon content than the commonly used g-C3N4 precursors along with unique structural characteristics. Introducing only a tiny bit of this pyrimidine moiety into the g-C3N4 structure will dramatically improve light harvesting in the visible light range. A very meaningful work was reported by Takanabe and co-workers117 to ameliorate the crystallinity of g-C3N4, who developed a two-step rearrangement strategy including supramolecular pre-assembly and ionic melt polymerization by adopting melamine and 2,4,6-triaminopyrimidine as a dopant to prepare crystalline triazine-based g-C3N4 (Fig. 1a). The prepared g-C3N4 possessed high crystallinity attributed to the incorporated pyrimidine units. Moreover, the H2 evolution rate of the prepared g-C3N4 could reach up to 8160 μmol h−1 g−1, which was 14-fold higher than that of pristine g-C3N4 (Fig. 1b). In addition, the apparent quantum efficiency was even up to 7% at 420 nm, which is the highest value for previously reported triazine-based g-C3N4 photocatalysts (Fig. 1c).117 Inspired by this idea, the 2,4-diamino-6-phenyl-1,3,5-triazine molecules were also introduced into the melamine–cyanuric acid supramolecular network (Fig. 1d). The morphology of the obtained g-C3N4 could be regulated via changing the molar ratio of cyanuric acid, melamine, and 2,4-diamino-6-phenyl-1,3,5-triazine (Fig. 1e) and the photocatalytic properties were prominently improved (Fig. 1f). The introduction of phenyl groups into the g-C3N4 structure significantly improves light harvesting in the visible light range and results in the generation of a new charge-migration path.118
 Fig. 1  (a) Scheme for the construction of the triazine-based g-C3N4 samples. (b) Photocatalytic H2 evolution rate of the g-C3N4 samples prepared using different methods. (c) Wavelength-dependent apparent quantum efficiency of the prepared g-C3N4 samples. (d) Hydrogen-bonded supramolecular cyanuric acid–melamine–2,4-diamino-6-phenyl-1,3,5-triazine (C–M–Mp) complex and the proposed g-C3N4 structure after calcination. (e) SEM images of the structured C–M–Mp g-C3N4 with different molar ratios after condensation at 550 °C for 4 h. (f) Plots of (C/C0) versus reaction time for the C–M–Mp g-C3N4 samples and bulk g-C3N4 sample. Here, bulk g-C3N4 was constructed by the condensation of melamine at 550 °C for 4 h. This figure has been reproduced from ref. 118 with permission from John Wiley and Sons, and from ref. 119 with permission from the American Chemical Society. 
Based on the supramolecular pre-assembly approach, some other supramolecular networks such as Cu–melamine supramolecular networks were also employed as the precursors for the preparation of g-C3N4.119 The Cu–melamine network can form a 2D supramolecular structure via hydrogen bonds and coordination bonds, which was used as a sacrificial template as well as a precursor to synthesize Cu-doped g-C3N4. The obtained Cu-doped g-C3N4 exhibited strong optical absorption from the visible-light region to the near-infrared region. In addition, it also revealed high photocatalytic activity and stability. Thus, these kinds of metal–melamine supramolecular networks have the ability to produce g-C3N4 hybrids with controllable morphologies and electronic structures, providing great potential for heterogeneous photocatalytic applications. An ameliorative work based on Cu–melamine networks was reported by Gao and co-workers,120 who presented a new and simple synthetic method for the construction of porous nanorod-type g-C3N4/CuO composites through employing a Cu–melamine supramolecular framework as a precursor, which was obtained as a blue solid by treating Cu(OAc)2 with melamine in a methanol solution. The Cu–melamine supramolecular framework can form a 3D porous structure with hexagonal pores linked by N–H⋯N self-complementary hydrogen bonds as reported by Chen et al.121 Consequently, the prepared g-C3N4/CuO composites showed excellent optical and photocatalytic properties.
2.2.1.2. Microwave-assisted heating strategy for g-C3N4 preparation. 
As we know, g-C3N4 is usually obtained via pyrolyzing the N-rich precursors at high temperature for several hours or even several days, which is time-consuming and labor-intensive.122 Moreover, the reaction atmosphere, polymerization time, and heating rate greatly influence its physicochemical and photocatalytic performance. Inspired by the microwave-assisted method for the preparation of graphene,123 a similar strategy has also been recently reported for the fabrication of g-C3N4 to overcome the defects mentioned above. The first report using the microwave-assisted heating strategy for g-C3N4 preparation was presented by Du and co-coworkers88 in 2014. They developed a rapid and facile synthetic method named “microwave-assisted heating synthesis”, which allowed the highly crystalline g-C3N4 photocatalyst to be obtained within minutes. Interestingly, the g-C3N4 prepared by this method showed ca. 1.5-fold higher photocatalytic HER activity than the g-C3N4 produced by the thermal polymerization process (550 °C for 6 h). This improved photocatalytic property could be because of the enlarged specific surface area and the efficient separation of the photogenerated electrons and holes caused by the improved crystalline quality of the C3N4.
To obtain a robust and reliable synthesis condition under microwave radiation, a liquid reaction system should be given the priority because the microwave radiation is more stable and efficient in liquid media than the common solid-state reaction system.124 Thus, multifaceted liquid phases such as aqueous solutions,125 polyols,126 and ionic liquids127 have been widely employed as reaction media during the microwave-assisted reaction to enhance the stability of microwave radiation. Inspired by this idea, Chen and co-workers128 developed a microwave-assisted molten-salt strategy to prepare an isotype heterojunction of triazine/heptazine based g-C3N4 photocatalyst using readily accessible melamine as the precursor. This synthetic strategy combined the merits of fast heating of microwave radiation and the features of KCl/LiCl molten salt, achieving large-scale and rapid fabrication of g-C3N4 photocatalysts (Fig. 2). The photocatalytic properties of the prepared isotype triazine/heptazine-based g-C3N4 heterojunctions were higher than those of the conventional pyrolysis products, and the typical apparent quantum yield was up to 10.7% at 420 nm. The remarkable enhancement of photocatalytic properties was owing to the appropriate configuration in the electronic band structures of isotype triazine/heptazine-based g-C3N4 heterojunctions, which provided effective transfer and separation of the photo-generated electron–hole pairs. The synergistic effects of microwave heating and molten-salt liquid polycondensation offer a robust and reliable platform for large-scale and rapid production of g-C3N4-based photocatalysts. Enlightened by this conception, combining multiple methods to achieve large-scale production of g-C3N4-based photocatalysts with high catalytic activity is particularly important.
 Fig. 2  (a) Fabrication routes for g-C3N4 photocatalysts from melamine (mw-ms = microwave-assisted molten-salt process, er-ms = electric-resistance molten-salt process, er = electric-resistance heating, and mw = microwave-heating process). Comparisons of (b) polymerization times and (c) yields for various strategies. This figure has been reproduced from ref. 128 with permission from Elsevier. 
2.2.1.3. Template-assisted strategy for g-C3N4 preparation. 
Mesoporous g-C3N4 possesses unique surface characteristics and physicochemical properties, e.g., opened crystalline pore wall and large specific surface area, rapid charge transfer and multiple scattering effects, which could improve the light harvesting ability and thus enhance its photocatalytic properties.37 For this purpose, organic or inorganic materials should be considered as hard templates to prepare porous g-C3N4. In 2009, Wang's group37 used SiO2 nanoparticles as the hard template to prepare porous g-C3N4 by direct pyrolysis of cyanamide. The prepared mesoporous g-C3N4 showed varied specific surface area from 8 to 373 m2 g−1 and a 3D interconnected framework with a semiconductor band gap of 2.7 eV. Zhao's group129 presented a facile nanocasting method for the fabrication of highly ordered mesoporous g-C3N4 nanorods with ultrahigh specific surface area (971–1124 m2 g−1), ultralarge pore volume (1.31–1.79 cm3 g−1), and ultrahigh N content (9.3–23 wt%) employing SBA-15 as the template and hexamethylenetetramine as the g-C3N4 precursor. Notably, the specific surface area, pore volume and N content depended mainly on the selected precursor and pyrolysis temperature. In addition to inorganic materials, organic materials could also be used as templates to prepare porous g-C3N4. Yan et al.130 prepared g-C3N4 with worm-like pores and a narrow pore size distribution adopting Pluronic P123 as the soft-template and melamine as the precursor. Pluronic 123 enabled an adequately steady self-assembly within the matrixes.131 The prepared mesoporous g-C3N4 not only possessed large specific surface area (up to 90 m2 g−1) but also ensured sufficient light absorption towards the maximum of solar spectrum (up to 800 nm).
In addition to the hard-template method mentioned above, the soft-template method is also highly worth considering since it is more facile and could avoid the incorporation of impurities.132,133 Zhang and co-workers131 reported a simple one-step strategy for the preparation of g-C3N4 by adopting block copolymers, nonionic surfactants, and ionic liquids as a soft template. The pore volume and specific surface area of the obtained g-C3N4 could be regulated through the template content and processing conditions and these ionic liquids endowed g-C3N4 with accessible pore networks and relatively high N contents. Chen and co-workers28 reported a convenient method to prepare g-C3N4 from urea with self-supported gas. The gas bubbles produced in the reaction process could be used as the soft-templates to help g-C3N4 form porous micro-structures. The prepared g-C3N4 could be used as a highly efficient photocatalyst, showing excellent photocatalytic properties and recyclability under visible light.
2.2.1.4. Molten salt strategy for g-C3N4 preparation. 
Inspired by the molten salt method of graphene,134 recently, a molten salt strategy has also been reported extensively for the preparation of g-C3N4 due to the extraordinary features including high electrical conductivity, good ionic mobility, negligible vapor pressure, and outstanding chemical and thermal stability.135 Usually, molten salt can act as a solvent for the preparation of various high-temperature materials, and as a “soft template” to regulate micro- and meso-porosity of the final products. In an early report, Thomas and co-workers87 presented highly crystalline g-C3N4 prepared from polymerization of dicyandiamide adopting LiCl/KCl molten salts at 380–600 °C. The resultant product from the molten salt method was found to be g-C3N4 with hexagonally arranged s-heptazine units. A similar work was reported by Lotsch and co-workers,136 who developed a novel 2D triazine-based g-C3N4via a simple one-step ionothermal synthesis approach involving LiCl/KCl molten salts and using 4-amino-2,6-dihydroxypyrimidine as the dopant due to its structural similarity to melamine and high C and O content. The prepared 2D triazine-based g-C3N4 showed substantial visible-light-induced H2 production ability, and the quantum efficiency was up to 3.4%, which was 6-fold higher than that of g-C3N4 prepared by polycondensation of precursors without molten salt.
To make full development of the molten-salt method, researchers utilized templates,137,138 various precursors139 and secondary semiconductors140 to prepare g-C3N4 based photocatalysts. Takanabe and co-workers137 reported triazine-based g-C3N4 materials with different C/N ratios obtained via adjusting the monomer composition ratio between melamine and 2,4,6-triaminopyrimidine. The preparation of the triazine-based g-C3N4 materials contained two steps: (i) solution-state dispersion of the monomers through hydrogen-bonded supramolecular aggregates, and (ii) molten-salt high-temperature polymerization. To further enhance the photocatalytic properties of the triazine-based g-C3N4, KCC-1 silica was employed as a support for the flux-assisted preparation of g-C3N4, which provided improved crystallinity and light harvesting ability. Dontsova and co-workers139,140 employed various precursors, e.g., 3,5-diamino-1,2,4-triazole and 3-amino-1,2,4-triazole-5-thiol, and secondary semiconductors, e.g., MoS2 and Co2O3, to produce g-C3N4 and g-C3N4 composites through the molten salt strategy. Both the synthesized g-C3N4 and g-C3N4 composites showed improved photocatalytic properties and visible-light absorption ability.
2.2.1.5. Ionic liquid strategy for g-C3N4 preparation. 
Different from the high melting point and viscosity of molten salts, ionic liquids are considered to be an organic salt with a melting point of below 100 °C and have relatively low viscosity.141 In addition, ionic liquids have many unique inherent advantages such as outstanding chemical and thermal stability, small vapor pressure, and the intrinsically liquid feature at room temperature, which make them ideal solvents in many research fields such as catalysis, electrochemistry, biology, petrochemical industry, etc.142–146 The development of an ionic liquid strategy for g-C3N4 preparation dates back to 2010 by Wang and co-workers.147 They adopted 1-butyl-3-methylimidazolium tetrafluoroborate (BmimBF4) ambient ionic liquid as a unique soft template and dicyandiamide (DCDA) as a precursor to prepare B- and F-containing mesoporous g-C3N4. Fascinatingly, the ratio of BmimBF4/DCDA has an important impact on the crystallinity and specific surface area of the obtained g-C3N4. The obtained g-C3N4 possessed high surface area (ca. 440 m2 g−1) and large pore volume (ca. 0.32 m3 g−1). Moreover, it also possessed high N, B, and F contents, local graphitic order, and excellent photocatalytic properties under visible light. Furthermore, g-C3N4 hybrids can also be fabricated by the ionic liquid strategy. Li and co-workers148 presented an ionic liquid system containing Fe3+ for the preparation of g-C3N4/α-Fe2O3 hollow microspheres via a green solvothermal strategy. Interestingly, ionic liquid 1-butyl-3-methylimidazolium tetrachlorideferrate(III) acted as the dispersing medium as well as template, which was greatly important for the construction of the g-C3N4/α-Fe2O3 hollow microspheres. The g-C3N4 could be tightly immobilized onto the α-Fe2O3 surface because of the excellent dispersibility of the ionic liquid, which ensured the g-C3N4/α-Fe2O3 hollow microspheres to have a large specific capacitance, outstanding cycling properties and high coulombic efficiency. An analogous idea has also been adopted to synthesize Ag/AgBr/g-C3N4 hybrids.
Although ionic liquids have recently been widely used in the preparation of g-C3N4, ionic liquids containing organic cations must be kept below 350 °C, and decomposition will occur during high-temperature pyrolysis.149 In addition, the high-cost, multistep synthesis and complicated purification process of these ionic liquids also severely restrict their application.150 Watanabe and co-workers151 found that H2SO4-based protic ionic liquids and salts possessed great potential as single, low-cost, and versatile precursors for one-step facile synthesis of carbon materials in the absence of catalysts or prepolymerization. Unlike traditional ionic liquids, which are limited to only with cyano/nitrile moieties, this kind of H2SO4-based protic ionic liquids and salts can bear harsh carbonization, and they are cost-effective and easy to prepare. More importantly, direct carbonization of H2SO4-based protic ionic liquids and salts can produce intrinsic N-doped carbons with tunable N contents (up to 11.1%) and high conductivity. Inspired by these, they also reported a series of easily prepared protic ionic liquids and salts based on widely available N-containing bases and acids, which can be used as novel small-molecule precursors for the preparation of carbon materials via direct carbonization. Protonation enabled nearly all N-containing compounds to be directly carbonized to carbon materials without an additional catalyst, tedious synthesis, vacuum system, and etching step. When protic ionic liquids and salts possessed very high N contents, no C was obtained during heat treatment. Such protic ionic liquids and salts were first transformed into melon-type g-C3N4 above 500 °C. Thus, they could also be a suitable candidate for the construction of g-C3N4 by thermal polycondensation at moderate temperatures.149
2.2.1.6. Other strategies for g-C3N4 preparation. 
Despite the significant advance in liquid exfoliation of g-C3N4 prepared via adopting an ultrasound-assisted method as mentioned above, the exfoliation efficiency is still unsatisfactory. A large number of undispersed bulk g-C3N4 particles remain as sediments through centrifugation. From the practical application viewpoint, highly efficient fabrication of 2D g-C3N4 nanosheets with a unilamellar structure is highly desirable. Chemical exfoliation via redox reactions and intercalation or ion-exchange process is another widely used method for effective exfoliation of g-C3N4 owing to its unique advantages, e.g., the incorporation of guests into the interlayer gallery, functionalization on the nanosheet surface, the possibility of delamination into unilamellar nanosheets, and high exfoliation efficiency as well as the acceptable cost-effectiveness.96,152,153 Inspired by the idea of intercalating H2SO4 into the interplanar space to prepare graphite oxide,154,155 Zhu and co-workers153 prepared g-C3N4 nanosheets with a single atomic layer nanostructure via simple H2SO4 chemical exfoliation. Through an ultrasonic treatment and centrifugation, supernatant liquor containing g-C3N4 monolayer nanosheets was obtained. The prepared g-C3N4 nanosheets showed a single atomic thickness (ca. 0.4 nm) which approached the theoretical thickness of the g-C3N4 host layer (0.325 nm), indicating that single layered g-C3N4 nanosheets have been successfully prepared. More importantly, the photocatalytic H2 generation, pollutant degradation activity and photocurrent production of single layered g-C3N4 nanosheets were more outstanding when compared to the bulk g-C3N4. Motivated by H2SO4 chemical exfoliation, other common acids like HCl and HNO3, were also used for the effective exfoliation of g-C3N4. The HCl treatment activated g-C3N4 through a protonation effect that would enhance the photocatalytic properties. Moreover, HCl-treated g-C3N4 showed a higher dispersion in water than hydrothermal- or ultrasonic-treated g-C3N4.156 Exfoliation behaviour could also occur when refluxing g-C3N4 with HNO3, and the obtained g-C3N4 nanosheets showed a single atomic thickness (<1 nm).157
Sol–gel methods are also used to fabricate g-C3N4 and its nanocomposites because of their simplicity, environmental friendliness, low cost, high purity and homogeneity, and ambient processing temperature. Chang and co-workers158 reported a simple sol–gel strategy for the fabrication of g-C3N4–TiO2 nanocomposites by employing tetra-n-butyl titanate as the precursor, which underwent hydrolysis and a polycondensation reaction to form a colloid solution. The apparent rate constant of g-C3N4–TiO2 nanocomposites was as high as 1.2 × 10−2 min−1, which was 2.4-fold that of bare TiO2. The improvement could be mainly ascribed to the full-matched band gap structure with a heterojunction interface, large specific surface area, and favorable optical performance. A similar work was proposed by Sun and co-workers,159 who successfully synthesized g-C3N4/TiO2 hybrid photocatalysts by a modified sol–gel technique with varying mass ratios of g-C3N4 under facile conditions. They illustrated that the g-C3N4/TiO2 heterojunction was formed and the TiO2 nanoparticles were uniformly coated on g-C3N4 nanosheets. The g-C3N4/TiO2-80% hybrid photocatalyst showed improved visible-light photocatalytic property, which was ca. 3.5-fold compared to that of pure g-C3N4. The improvement could be ascribed to the relatively higher adsorption capacity and effective separation of the photogenerated electron–hole pairs.
As we know, ultrathin or monolayer g-C3N4 nanosheets show superior charge carrier separation and more abundant surface active sites compared to bulk g-C3N4.38,153 The preparation of ultrathin or monolayer g-C3N4 nanosheets commonly involves strong acid treatment or chemical exfoliation, and thus the preparation process is not eco-friendly. Moreover, ultrathin nanosheets suffer from serious agglomeration, leading to decreased specific surface area and a loss of reaction sites, both of them are generally harmful to photocatalytic properties. Thus, there is an urgent demand to explore an environment-friendly and highly efficient technology for the preparation of g-C3N4. Huang and co-workers160 reported a novel precursor reforming strategy for the synthesis of g-C3N4. A mild hydrothermal pretreatment of melamine in aqueous urea induced a phase transformation in melamine, yielding a novel precursor, which could prepare 3D ultrathin porous N self-doped g-C3N4 with improved photocatalytic absorption properties and excellent photocatalytic activity (Fig. 3a). In fact, the 3D ultrathin porous g-C3N4 nanostructure displays several attractive features, such as short diffusion paths of photo-induced charge carriers, improved visible light absorption, ample surface active sites for reactions, and accessible channels for reactant diffusion and adsorption. As a result, the prepared ultrathin nanosheets showed a thickness of ∼3 nm (7 or 8 layers) with mesopores, and possessed high specific surface area, narrowed band gap and more efficient charge separation when compared to pristine g-C3N4 prepared via direct melamine calcination (Fig. 3b and c). Here, an unprecedented breakthrough is highly worthy to be emphasized. A 23 times enhanced H2 evolution rate (ca. 3579 μmol h−1 g−1) (λ > 420 nm) and an apparent quantum efficiency of 27.8% at 420 ± 15 nm were obtained. The apparent quantum efficiency of 27.8% was much higher than those previously reported for ultrathin/porous/doped g-C3N4 photocatalysts (Fig. 3d–g). On the basis of the above results, it is believed that the combination of precursors’ phase transformation and the introduction of urea results in the formation of advanced 3D ultrathin porous g-C3N4-based photocatalysts, achieving highly active photocatalytic HER, which guide us to develop highly efficient photocatalysts via precursor modification.
 Fig. 3  (a) Diagrammatic sketch for the formation of 3D ultrathin porous N-doped g-C3N4. (b) AFM image and the height distribution profiles of the separated layer from UM3. (c) Nitrogen adsorption–desorption isotherms for g-C3N4 and UMx, and the corresponding pore size distribution for UM3 (inset). (d) H2 evolution curves. (e) H2 evolution rates over the pure g-C3N4 and the UMx samples in the presence of Pt, and (f) wavelength-dependent apparent quantum efficiency over UM3 in the presence of Pt as a cocatalyst. (g) H2 evolution rates over the pure g-C3N4 and the UMx samples in the absence of Pt. Here, UMx represents the obtained 3D ultrathin porous N-doped g-C3N4, with x = 0, 1, 3, 6 representing the molar ratio of urea: melamine. Pure g-C3N4 was fabricated by direct melamine calcination. This figure has been reproduced from ref. 160 with permission from Elsevier. 
Based on the knowledge that constructing porous g-C3N4 commonly involves either templates or post-treatment with bulk g-C3N4, a novel template-free precursor-surface-etching strategy was reported by Huang and co-workers161 for the preparation of porous g-C3N4 thin nanosheets. The addition of thiourea solution helped prepare porous g-C3N4 thin nanosheets, which could etch the melamine surface. In this way, the microstructure and porosity of the porous g-C3N4 thin nanosheets could be controlled by only regulating the thiourea amount. The prepared porous g-C3N4 nanosheets showed remarkably enhanced photocatalytic HER (3.3-fold enhancement) and NO removal activity (5.5-fold improvement) in contrast to the pristine bulk g-C3N4. Under visible-light illumination, the photogenerated electrons and holes were prone to recombine in the bulk g-C3N4, leading to low photocatalytic properties. But as for the porous g-C3N4 nanosheets, the photogenerated electrons and holes could easily transfer to their surface, and then participated in the redox reactions, giving rise to high photocatalytic performance.

Table 2 summarizes the merits and demerits of different methods for the construction of g-C3N4. The microwave-assisted and chemical exfoliation strategies are considered to be a class of highly efficient methods for fabricating uniform single-layered or few layered g-C3N4 nanosheets, which are promising for large scale and rapid fabrication of g-C3N4 photocatalysts. But unfortunately, there is still a long way to go before we can really achieve the single-layered g-C3N4 nanosheets because of their easily agglomerating nature. Moreover, these methods involve commonly aggressive acid treatment or chemical exfoliation, and the treatment processes are not environmentally benign. In addition, in order to produce g-C3N4 with an ordered texture, controllable morphology and high crystallinity, supramolecular pre-assembly, template-assisted, ionic liquid, and hydrothermal pretreatment strategies are highly worth considering. But they seem to be time- and cost-consuming and relatively complicated preparation processes. All in all, every method has its own advantages and disadvantages, and thus choosing a suitable approach or combining multiple methods to tailor g-C3N4 is particularly important.


2.2.2. Electronic structure. 
Commonly, g-C3N4 possesses a graphitic structure.162 However, the ground state structure of g-C3N4 is still not very clear, and clarification of the exact ground state structure remains a challenge for researchers. Generally speaking, the thermal polycondensation approach is commonly employed to synthesize g-C3N4 and X-ray diffraction patterns are applied to analyze the crystal structure of g-C3N4. However, two typical X-ray diffraction peaks at 2θ = 13.04 and 27.25° only can provide inadequate information due to low crystallinity of g-C3N4. Many researchers believe that these two peaks correspond to an in-plane ordering with a repeated distance of about 6.788 Å and graphitic interlayer distance of 3.273 Å, respectively.24,63,87 However, exact stacking positions of C and N atoms and the adjacent layers are still not clear, and thus further study is needed to clarify the stacking order and geometry of g-C3N4. Wang and co-workers163 used ab initio evolutionary searching to predict the stable crystal structure of g-C3N4 obtained via thermal polycondensation and salt-melt synthesis (SMS) method. For the g-C3N4 prepared by thermal polycondensation, off-plane distortion of heptazine units was a characteristic of the most stable structure (phases 1–3), as shown in Fig. 4a. In comparison with other layered structures, distorted phases in heptazine-based g-C3N4 were the most robust. Phase 1 had a direct band gap of 2.87 eV whereas phases 2 and 3 had indirect band gaps of 3.14 and 2.27 eV, respectively. The peripheral N atoms, Nper, and the C atoms C2 and C3 were shifted around an axis passing through the atoms Nlink, Ncen, and C1 (Fig. 4b). Its each layer consisted of heptazine units and pores, which was significantly different from typical honeycomb layered structures (e.g., graphite164 and h-BN165). Thus, g-C3N4 is more susceptible to distortion. For the g-C3N4 prepared by the SMS method, they clarified that the controversial structure of the previously reported high-crystallinity SMS phase was C6N9H3·LiCl instead of g-C3N4 (Fig. 4c and d), and C6N9H3·LiCl was a more stable high-crystallinity phase than C6N9H3·Li3Cl. Based on the predicted stable structures, a very promising reaction pathway to synthesize highly crystalline g-C3N4 from C6N9H3·LiCl was also proposed (Fig. 4e). The first step was to remove Li ions from C6N9H3·LiCl due to the small size of Li ions (∼0.76 Å). The next one was the removal of Cl− ions, which could be slightly more difficult due to their relatively larger size (∼1.81 Å). Finally, highly crystalline g-C3N4 can be formed via the heat treatment of C2N3H in an oxidizing environment accompanied by NH3 removal. A deep understanding about the crystal structure of g-C3N4 is beneficial for researchers to explore highly crystalline g-C3N4 and achieve optimal photocatalytic properties, which inspires us to make great efforts towards theoretical calculations and experimental validation.
 Fig. 4  (a) Prediction of heptazine-based g-C3N4: (i) predicted most stable configuration, phase 1; (ii) second stable distorted configuration, phase 2; (iii) most stable planar configuration, phase 3. The atoms in the middle layer of the lattices were labeled with yellow crosses. (b) Specific structure of a distorted heptazine unit (i) and probable rearrangement route from phase 2 to phase 1 (ii). The red arrows indicate the directions of movement of the atoms. (c) Predicted structures corresponding to the SMS condition: C6N9H3–LiCl. The small pink, green, and green balls represent H, Li, and Cl atoms, respectively. The atoms in the lower layer of the lattices were labeled with yellow crosses. (d) Comparison of the simulated XRD patterns of C6N9H3–LiCl with the experimental XRD patterns of the highly crystalline samples synthesized by SMS and modified SMS. (e) Proposed reaction pathway to synthesize highly crystalline g-C3N4 starting from C6N9H3·LiCl. This figure has been reproduced from ref. 163 with permission from the American Chemical Society. 
Other research groups also researched the electronic structure of g-C3N4. In g-C3N4, lone pair electrons of N are mainly responsible for the band structure and formation of the valence band. The reduction and oxidation potentials are related to positioning of valence and conduction bands with regard to the standard reduction potential scale of H2. As early as 2008, Wang and co-workers23 presented a DFT calculation to research the electronic structure of g-C3N4 and provided evidence that g-C3N4 possessed the potential to function as a photocatalyst for H2 production. They found that the highest occupied molecular orbital/lowest unoccupied molecular orbital band gap of a melem molecule was 3.5 eV, which was decreased to 2.6 eV for polymeric melon,166 and finally decreased to 2.1 eV for forming well-condensed g-C3N4. The wavefunction investigation showed that the valence and conduction bands were mainly driven by the N Pz orbitals and C Pz orbitals, respectively, where the photogenerated electrons and holes were separated, indicating that the oxidation and reduction sites for splitting water could take place solely in the N atom and C atom. The reaction energies were computed by ab initio thermodynamics, and the results showed that the reduction level for H+ was fully located in the centre of the bandgap, and the oxidation level was positioned slightly above the top of the valence band. This meant that the reduction process was energetically probable and the oxidation process would admit transfer of electrons and holes, but presumably with a low driving force. The electron–hole pair separation suggested independent oxidation and reduction sites for water splitting in N and C atoms.167 These calculations provided evidence that g-C3N4 had the potential to function as a photocatalyst for H2 production. Jacob and coworkers168 used the many-body Green's function method to research the electronic and optical properties of g-C3N4. The result showed that the electronic band gap of g-C3N4 was 4.24 eV, but the electronic band gap of tri-s-triazine based g-C3N4 was 5.22 eV (direct) and 4.15 eV (indirect). They also confirmed that the electronic structures of g-C3N4 in the valence and conduction bands were formed by the overlap of the 2p orbital of the N atom and the 2p orbital of the C atom together.
All in all, the 2D structure of g-C3N4 can be considered as an N atom substituted graphene framework, which possesses conjugated graphitic planes with sp2 hybridization of C and N atoms in an aromatic ring. The lone pair electron of N can form the lone pair valence band which is stabilized by the π electronic state. The mid-way band gap (2.7 eV) ensures it to absorb visible-light in a wider range, while appropriate positioning of the reduction level of the conduction band (−1.3 eV) and the oxidation level of the valence (+1.4 eV) band initiates the photocatalytic reactions.63,169


2.2.3. Physicochemical properties. 
g-C3N4, as one of the most stable allotropes in various carbon nitride structures, has been proven stable up to 600 °C or even higher by thermal gravity analysis.66 A strong endothermal peak appeared at 630 °C because of the degradation of g-C3N4, which was ca. 30 °C higher than the starting degradation temperature of the obtained g-C3N4 (600 °C). Complete decomposition of g-C3N4 occurred at 750 °C and led to no residue of g-C3N4 (Fig. 5a),98 reflecting that the g-C3N4 possessed very good thermal stability. In addition, no detectable solubility or reactivity of g-C3N4 was found in conventional solvents, e.g., H2O, C2H5OH, DMF, THF, (CH3CH2)2O, and toluene.170 In order to study the stability and durability of g-C3N4 in various solvents, g-C3N4 powder was dispersed in H2O, (CH3)2CO, C2H5OH, pyridine, acetonitrile, CH2Cl2, DMF, CH3COOH, and 0.1 M NaOH aqueous solution for 30 days. These dispersed g-C3N4 samples were subsequently dried at 80 °C for 10 h. The FTIR spectra of the immersed g-C3N4 samples were basically unaltered in contrast to those of g-C3N4 dispersions (Fig. 5b). These results confirmed that g-C3N4 showed excellent chemical stability and good durability.
 Fig. 5  (a) TG–DSC thermograms of g-C3N4 formed via heating melamine at 520 °C. (b) FT-IR of melem (prepared by heating dicyandiamide at 400 °C), melamine, and g-C3N4 before and after the protonation in 37% HCl. (c) Diffuse reflectance absorption spectrum and photoluminescence spectrum (inset) under 420 nm excitation, and (d) time-resolved photoluminescence spectrum monitored at 525 nm under 420 nm excitation at 298 K for bulk g-C3N4 (black) and mesoporous g-C3N4 (red). (e) Periodic on/off photocurrent Iph response of the mesoporous g-C3N4 electrode in 0.5 M Na2SO4 under zero bias in a standard two electrode photoelectrochemical cell. This figure has been reproduced from ref. 37, 98 and 173 with permission from the American Chemical Society. 
Optical properties of g-C3N4 are also very important. Usually, the optical properties of g-C3N4 are tested by UV/vis absorption and photoluminescence tests. The theoretical calculation confirmed that g-C3N4 was a typical semiconductor with a bandgap of >5 eV, mainly relying on structural or anatomic diversities.171 In fact, traditional g-C3N4 showed the typical absorption pattern of an organic semiconductor with an intensively enlarged bandgap adsorption at ∼420 nm (Fig. 5c). This result indicated that the mesoporous structure can improve the light harvesting ability of the material.37 This is because of its large specific surface area and multiple scattering effects. Time-resolved photoluminescence experiments were performed to measure the photoinduced charge separation of the recombined species, showing a lifetime of ∼5 ns even at 298 K, which provided circumstantial evidence to prove the excellent photocatalytic properties of g-C3N4 (Fig. 5d). The photoluminescence signal of the mesoporous g-C3N4 was greatly suppressed. This was evidence for electron relocalization on surface terminal sites, which can promote catalytic redox functions.172 Visible-light illumination of mesoporous g-C3N4 layers decorated on fluoride–tin oxide glass plates revealed a short circuit photocurrent (Fig. 5e), but it was still rather low, mainly because of the poor contact between the substrate and the organic powder featuring grain boundaries. All of these results confirmed that g-C3N4 displayed excellent stability, good durability, and high photocatalytic activity, indicating that it can be used as promising highly efficient photocatalysts even under harsh and dreadful conditions.


2.2.4. Photocatalytic HER mechanism. 
As indicated above, g-C3N4 shows a narrow band gap energy (ca. 2.7 eV), which is considered as a promising visible-light-active metal-free polymeric semiconductor HER photocatalyst. To understand its outstanding photocatalytic performance in depth, a photocatalytic HER mechanism of g-C3N4 is fundamental and needs to be presented clearly, which will act as a strategic guide for researchers to further improve its photocatalytic properties effectively. A succinct photocatalytic HER mechanism of g-C3N4 and the corresponding photoexcited electron–hole pairs with probable decay paths are shown in Fig. 6. After the g-C3N4 photocatalyst is illuminated by visible-light, photoinduced electrons and holes are expeditiously formed. Once the photoinduced electron and hole are spatially separated, the charge carriers are in an excited state, which are subsequently transferred fleetly to the g-C3N4 surface, initiating photocatalytic HER.174 Generally, the reactive sites may be located either on the g-C3N4 or cocatalyst surface.175 Under these circumstances, the g-C3N4 plays a pivotal role in absorbing visible-light, generating photoinduced electron–hole pairs and then transferring them to the g-C3N4 or cocatalyst surface. Nevertheless, g-C3N4 is chemically active only when the photoinduced electron–hole pair is consumed simultaneously before the recombination occurs in a fraction of nanoseconds. The excitation of band gap upon light absorption and the formation of electron–hole pairs can also be fleetly accompanied by a few de-excitation pathways. The successful transfer of the photoinduced electron and hole to the reactant molecules leads to the reduction and oxidation reactions (paths 1 and 3). Unfortunately, two disadvantageous recombination processes, e.g., volume recombination (path 2) and surface recombination (path 4) may take place, which will strongly weaken its photocatalytic activity.176 In detail, when the recombination processes take place, the excited electrons and holes are reverted to the VB and CB, respectively, abreacting the energy without reacting with the adsorbed reactant molecules. Therefore, the key factor of limiting g-C3N4 catalytic performance is the recombination of the photogenerated electron–hole pairs. Besides, its band gap and morphology also have enormous impacts on the catalytic performance. Up to now, tremendous attempts have been made to promote its photocatalytic efficiency by modifying its band gap and morphology or accelerating the separation of the photogenerated electrons and holes.177 A comprehensive understanding of the photocatalytic reaction mechanism is necessary for further amelioration and improvement in the photocatalytic performance, which is advantageous for guiding us to rationally design and develop highly efficient g-C3N4-based photocatalysts.
 Fig. 6  Photocatalytic HER mechanism of g-C3N4 and the corresponding photoexcited electron–hole pairs with probable decay paths. Here, CB represents the conduction band, VB represents the valence band, and Eg represents the band gap energy. 


2.2.5. Challenges and solutions. 
As the photocatalytic mechanism explained above, g-C3N4 firstly absorbs visible light, generating electron–hole pairs which then transfer to the g-C3N4 surface or to a cocatalyst to initiate the reduction and oxidation processes for the photocatalytic conversion of reactant molecules. As for a g-C3N4 photocatalyst, there are still some challenging problems, mainly including lower photocatalytic efficiency and visible-light utilization.61 In order to meet the practical application requirements, a significant enhancement of its photocatalytic performance and broader visible-light absorption are highly desired. To prepare highly active g-C3N4-based photocatalysts, several important principles are highly worth emphasizing: (i) accelerating the charge migration and separation to restrain the recombination rate, (ii) improved exposure of visible-light absorption, (iii) more active sites and enhanced reactant adsorption on the g-C3N4 surface, and (iv) enhanced photochemical stability of the photocatalysts for prolonged duration.
The design strategies for various nanostructures such as thinner, highly crystalline, porous, and element-doped g-C3N4 are constructively employed to enhance the photocatalytic properties of intrinsic g-C3N4. The large specific surface area can enhance mass transfer ability and provide more active sites, which can further enhance the photocatalytic activity of g-C3N4.178 Because of the stacking of polymeric g-C3N4, its specific surface area is customarily less than 10 m2 g−1. But in theory, its specific surface area can reach 2500 m2 g−1 for ideal monolayered g-C3N4. An ultrathin thickness (ca. 2 nm, which was about 9 stacked layers) and superhigh specific surface area (ca. 384 m2 g−1) g-C3N4 nanosheet was reported in 2013,179 which achieved a H2 evolution rate of 1860 μmol h−1 g−1. Just as obviously, the formation of the porous structure is also beneficial for improving the specific surface area of g-C3N4. In addition, the existence of structural defects and intralayer hydrogen bonds in the g-C3N4 nanostructure also severely restricts its photocatalytic properties. In view of this, developing highly crystalline g-C3N4 is a reasonable protocol, which can decrease the number of structural defects and further enhance the photocatalytic properties. Heteroatom doping can also effectively ameliorate the structural defects and dominate the electronic band structure of g-C3N4 to enlarge the visible-light absorption and regulate the redox potentials to further enhance the photocatalytic properties in the visible-light region.22
Besides, the formation of g-C3N4 heterojunction composites (e.g., metal/g-C3N4, semiconductor/g-C3N4, metal organic framework/g-C3N4, carbon/g-C3N4, conducting polymer/g-C3N4, sensitizer/g-C3N4) is a compelling and feasible strategy to overcome its lower photocatalytic efficiency and visible-light utilization. This is mainly to be achieved by a built-in electric field within a space charge region to spatially separate and availably transfer the photogenerated electrons and holes.180 A common strategy is semiconductor hybridization, which is based on the band alignment between g-C3N4 and the other semiconductors, driving the photogenerated electrons and holes to transfer in the opposite directions, and further restraining the charge recombination to obtain enhanced photocatalytic activity.181 The conductive component (such as Au, Ag, graphene, and conductive polymer, etc.) can act as electronic conductive channels to efficiently separate the photogenerated electron–hole pairs and further improve the visible-light photocatalytic H2 generation properties of g-C3N4.182 An effective tactic for populating the conduction band of g-C3N4 with abundant electrons can be achieved by combining with suitable organic dyes, which enables the energy conversion of visible light to take place at longer wavelengths.183 Integration of MOFs with g-C3N4 can significantly improve the photocatalytic activity because of their structural and species diversities, controllable cavities and porosity, and high specific surface areas.184
In brief, in view of the lower photocatalytic efficiency and visible-light utilization of bulk g-C3N4, both intrinsic g-C3N4 (e.g., thinner, highly crystalline, doped, and porous g-C3N4) and g-C3N4 composite heterojunctions (e.g., metal/g-C3N4, semiconductor/g-C3N4, metal organic framework/g-C3N4, carbon/g-C3N4, conducting polymer/g-C3N4, sensitizer/g-C3N4) can effectively improve its photocatalytic activity. In the following sections, we will introduce and discuss in detail these highly efficient intrinsic g-C3N4 photocatalysts and g-C3N4 composite heterojunctions for the HER.

3. Design of various nanostructures of g-C3N4 for HER application
The photocatalytic HER takes place at the g-C3N4/H2O interface, and thus the photocatalytic activity strongly relies on its size, shape, nanostructure, and defects of g-C3N4.22 The regulation of the g-C3N4 mirco/nanostructure endows it with high specific surface area, affluent surface active sites, and enlarged light harvesting, all of them favor photocatalytic H2 production. The different nanostructures of g-C3N4 such as quantum dots,185 nanofibers,186 and nanosheets187 have a vital impact on its photocatalytic properties. Thus, there is still a plenty of room for improving the photocatalytic properties of g-C3N4 by enlarging the specific surface area. Here, four nanostructured intrinsic g-C3N4 materials designed by nanostructure engineering including thin g-C3N4 nanosheets, highly crystalline g-C3N4, doped g-C3N4, porous g-C3N4 for HER application are elaborated in detail, and their performance comparison is summarized in Table 3.
