3.1. Thinner g-C3N4 nanosheets for HER application
Up to now, there has already been many works highlighting the benefits for HER application by reducing the thickness of g-C3N4. Owing to the stacking of polymeric g-C3N4, its specific surface area is usually less than 10 m2 g−1. However, its theoretical specific surface area can reach 2500 m2 g−1 for ideal monolayered g-C3N4. Commonly, thinner g-C3N4 nanosheets are fabricated via an exfoliative strategy such as chemical exfoliation, ultrasonic exfoliation, liquid-phase exfoliation, chemical-oxidation-induced exfoliation, thermal exfoliation, and alkaline metal ion intercalation.96 A thinner free-standing g-C3N4 nanosheet was prepared by Yang and co-workers179 in 2013 by using a facile liquid-phase exfoliation strategy. Notably, the g-C3N4 nanosheets were exfoliated via ultrasonic treatment in 2-propanol, which possessed ultrathin thickness (ca. 2 nm), superhigh specific surface area (ca. 384 m2 g−1), and large aspect ratios, providing ample active sites and thus promoting the charge migration. Moreover, the photocatalytic H2 evolution rate of the prepared g-C3N4 nanosheets was as high as 1860 μmol h−1 g−1, which was much higher than that of bulk g-C3N4 (200 μmol h−1 g−1) and even higher than reported for highly-ordered porous g-C3N4 and g-C3N4 nanosheets synthesized via direct thermal exfoliation.188–190
It is worth emphasizing that the hydrothermal treatment can create vertical channels for directional electron transfer and generate ultrathin holey g-C3N4 nanosheets with remarkable hierarchical porosity and good hydrophilicity. Recently, a significant breakthrough was achieved by Qu and co-workers.38 A single atomic layered g-C3N4 nanosheet (a height of less than 0.5 nm) (Fig. 7b and c) was constructed through solvothermal exfoliation of mesoporous g-C3N4 synthesized by pyrolysis of a freeze-drying assembled dicyandiamide nanostructure precursor (Fig. 7a). The specific surface area of the monolayer mesoporous g-C3N4 nanosheets was about 331 m2 g−1, which was 6-fold higher compared to the bulk counterpart (∼54 m2 g−1) and 33-fold higher compared to the traditional bulk g-C3N4 (ca. 10 m2 g−1), and the pore size was mainly less than 20 nm. Such a remarkable enhancement of the specific surface area of the monolayer mesoporous g-C3N4 nanosheets could offer greater exposure of the interfacial contact and edge sites to the reactant, which was really beneficial to improve photocatalytic efficiency. In addition, the monolayer mesoporous g-C3N4 nanosheets showed a remarkably enhanced light-harvesting ability (Fig. 7d).191 The obtained monolayer mesoporous g-C3N4 nanosheets showed an obvious enhancement of photocurrent with respect to the bulk samples (Fig. 7e), indicating an improved charge separation. There was a large shift in the conduction band and valence band of the monolayer mesoporous g-C3N4 nanosheets, which resulted in a superior thermodynamic driving force for photocatalytic HER (Fig. 7f). As a result, the H2 evolution rate of the monolayer mesoporous g-C3N4 nanosheets reached 8510 μmol h−1 g−1, which was higher than 2D g-C3N4 nanostructures reported previously,10,37,179,192–199 namely, 5.5-fold that of the bulk counterpart (1560 μmol h−1 g−1) and 24.3-fold that of the traditional bulk g-C3N4 (350 μmol h−1 g−1) (Fig. 7g). To the best of our knowledge, it is the highest H2 evolution rate of all the metal-free intrinsic g-C3N4 nanosheet photocatalysts reported so far. Notably, the g-C3N4 nanosheets with a specific surface area of 331 m2 g−1 can achieve ultrahigh H2 evolution rate of 8510 μmol h−1 g−1, which inspires us to develop some new methods to further increase its specific surface area. Nowadays, there is still plenty of room for us to increase its specific surface area because its theoretical specific surface area can reach 2500 m2 g−1 for ideal monolayered g-C3N4.
 Fig. 7  (a) Fabrication process of monolayer mesoporous g-C3N4 nanosheets. (b) AFM image of the synthesized mesoporous g-C3N4 nanosheets; the inset shows the enlarged view of the rectangle area. (c) Height profiles along the lines in (b). (d) UV-vis diffuse reflectance spectra of the monolayer mesoporous g-C3N4 nanosheets and the bulk counterpart. The inset is the corresponding Tauc plots. (e) Fluorescence emission spectra of the monolayer mesoporous g-C3N4 nanosheets and the bulk counterpart with an excitation wavelength of 400 nm. The inset shows transient photocurrents under visible-light radiation (λ > 420 nm) of the monolayer mesoporous g-C3N4 nanosheets and the bulk counterpart obtained in 0.2 M Na2SO4 aqueous solution (pH = 6.8). (f) The valence band spectra and electronic band structure of the monolayer mesoporous g-C3N4 nanosheets and the bulk counterpart. (g) Photocatalytic activity of (i) the monolayer mesoporous g-C3N4 nanomesh, (ii) the bulk counterpart, (iii) the traditional g-C3N4 bulk under irradiation with visible light (λ > 420 nm). This figure has been reproduced from ref. 38 with permission from the American Chemical Society. 
As known, g-C3N4 with a layered structure is deemed to have the most potential for metal-free photocatalysts. Shi and co-workers200 reported a direct and green strategy to fabricate few-layered holey g-C3N4 nanosheets via simply introducing a piece of nickel foam over the precursors during the heating process. The prepared g-C3N4 nanosheets with unique structural advantages exhibited excellent photocatalytic HER activity, with a H2 evolution rate (ca. 1871.09 μmol h−1 g−1), which was 31-fold higher than that of bulk g-C3N4 under visible light. This outstanding photocatalytic property originated from the high specific surface area (240.34 m2 g−1) and mesoporous structure, which endowed g-C3N4 nanosheets with more active sites, efficient exciton dissociation, and prolonged charge carrier lifetime. Fang and co-workers201 presented a simple method for the construction of holey g-C3N4 nanosheets with a high crystallization network and enlarged optical absorption region through using the hydrothermal and microwave processes. The hydrothermal treatment of dicyandiamide produced the melamine–cyanaurate complex which would evolve into ultrathin g-C3N4 nanosheets with the abundant porous structure. And the microwave-assisted thermolysis process ensured that the prepared g-C3N4 nanosheets possess high crystallinity and the enhanced optical absorption region. The obtained g-C3N4 nanosheets showed an improved photocatalytic performance when compared to the pristine bulk g-C3N4.
As mentioned above, thinner g-C3N4 nanosheets are a thinner graphite-like 2D layered structure material with moderate conduction band and valence band levels, having great potential for HER application. Similarly, ultrathin g-C3N4 nanoplatelets would seem likely to have excellent photocatalytic properties for H2 production. Qu and coworkers202 prepared ultrathin g-C3N4 nanoplatelets via a facile, green, and highly efficient ball milling strategy. The prepared ultrathin g-C3N4 nanoplatelets with a small size (2–6 nm) and thin thickness (0.35–0.7 nm) had a higher specific surface area than bulk g-C3N4. Moreover, the obtained ultrathin g-C3N4 nanoplatelets were highly active in photocatalytic HER, with a H2 evolution rate of 1365 μmol h−1 g−1, which was 13.7 times higher than those of their bulk counterparts. The enhancement was mainly attributed to the synergistic effects resulting from the improved specific surface area, decreased thickness, and a negative shift in the conduction band accompanied by the exfoliation of bulk g-C3N4 into ultrathin g-C3N4 nanoplatelets. A variety of in-plane holes were distributed in the entire g-C3N4 layer which would generate new exposed active sites and the resulting cross-plane diffusion channels might further improve the electron transport and charge separation efficiency. As a consequence of these merits, the photocatalytic property of the prepared ultrathin g-C3N4 nanosheets was almost 22-fold higher than that of bulk g-C3N4 for H2 generation.
In summary, the fabrication of thinner g-C3N4 is mainly via exfoliation strategies, either microwave-assisted exfoliation or chemical exfoliation, but ideal single-layered g-C3N4 nanosheets still cannot be perfectly prepared at current levels of synthesis. Based on the fact that the few-layered g-C3N4 nanosheets display a relatively high H2 evolution rate of 8510 μmol h−1 g−1 (Table 3), we can imagine that ideal single-layered g-C3N4 nanosheets will display considerably improved photocatalytic properties. Thus, there is clearly a great need for the exploration of some advanced technologies for the synthesis of ideal single-layered g-C3N4 nanosheets.
3.2. Highly crystalline g-C3N4 for HER application
The surface characterization of g-C3N4 indicated the existence of structural defects because of inadequately reacted intermediates, containing amino and/or cyano groups.61,67 The photocatalytic properties are easily influenced by these structural defects since they commonly act as charge trap sites in photocatalytic reactions. To solve this problem, an effective method for the preparation of highly crystalline g-C3N4 to decrease the number of structural defects and intralayer hydrogen bonds is highly desired.203 The crystalline quality was found to have an important impact on photocatalytic performance.204 However, it is noteworthy that an effective technique for the construction of highly crystalline g-C3N4 is yet challenging. Melamine–cyanuric acid supramolecular aggregates were used for the construction of highly crystalline g-C3N4 with a significantly decreased number of defects through a rapid, microwave-assisted thermolysis technique. In consequence, the photocatalytic H2 evolution rate of the prepared highly crystalline g-C3N4 sample was 2-fold higher than that of the g-C3N4 sample prepared via direct pyrolysis of melamine–cyanuric acid supramolecular aggregates.106 In contrast to melon-type g-C3N4, poly(triazine imide) is a highly crystalline species and represents the only structurally characterized 2D g-C3N4 network, which ensures itself as an excellent model system to explore both structural effects and photocatalytic activity as a function of the exfoliation state.87,205–207 The layers consisted of imide-linked triazine units (Fig. 8a) and were stacked in an ABA-type fashion, with Li+ and Cl− situated in channels running along the stacking direction (Fig. 8b). Lotsch and co-workers prepared highly crystalline 2D g-C3N4 nanosheets via an ionothermal strategy of the layered bulk poly(triazine imide) materials and one-step liquid exfoliation (Fig. 8c). The obtained g-C3N4 nanosheets were 1–2 nm in height and afforded chemically and colloidally stable suspensions under acidic and alkaline conditions (Fig. 8d). Notably, the exfoliated poly(triazine imide) was the first structurally well-defined, crystalline 2D g-C3N4, showing high activity toward photocatalytic water splitting, which was among the highest ever observed for pristine g-C3N4, even including mesoporous “g-C3N4” (Fig. 8e and f).179,188,189 This work highlights the vital effects of morphology and specific surface area on the photocatalytic properties of pristine g-C3N4-based photocatalysts, and opens up a way as well for the deep understanding of structure–property relationships in g-C3N4 and the factors influencing the photocatalytic activity.
 Fig. 8  Idealized poly(triazine imide) structure (lithium/chloride intercalation omitted for clarity) viewed along the c-axis (a) and the slightly tilted b-axis (b). Diagram of the exfoliation process and product labeling (c). (d) AFM images of exfoliated poly(triazine imide) nanosheets deposited on a Si/SiO2 wafer and the corresponding height image (inset). Photocatalytic HER activity of the Pt-doped precipitates (I and II) in contrast to sonicated poly(triazine imide) and bulk poly(triazine imide) (e), and of the nanosheet suspensions compared to bulk poly(triazine imide) (f), measured in a 10 vol% TEOA/water solution for 3 h illumination with visible-light (λ > 420 nm). This figure has been reproduced from ref. 195 with permission from the American Chemical Society. 
As mentioned above, in order to realize highly crystalline g-C3N4, alkali-metal salt medium has been used in the process of thermal condensation.195,208–210 However, the obtained g-C3N4 was not as good as we expected, seemingly aroused by the secondary change of the g-C3N4 structure in the molten salt system, during which alkali metals would coordinate into the C–N plane of g-C3N4 and change the spatial charge distribution. Thus, it is vital to develop a facile and economic technique for the construction of g-C3N4 photocatalysts with improved crystallinity. Zhang and co-workers211 reported a novel and effective strategy for high-yield preparation of extremely high crystalline g-C3N4 photocatalysts by two-step calcination without the assistance of any additive or salt intercalation. As expected, the obtained highly crystalline g-C3N4 photocatalysts displayed a remarkably high H2 evolution rate of 9577.6 μmol h−1 g−1 under solar light illumination, which was 15.5-fold higher than that of bulk g-C3N4, and also higher than that of most of the reported crystalline g-C3N4. Moreover, an ultrahigh apparent quantum efficiency of 9.01% at 420 nm for H2 generation was also achieved.
Microwave-assisted heating synthesis is another facile and economic method for the construction of highly crystalline g-C3N4. If the method is employed to prepare highly crystalline g-C3N4 materials, the obtained nanostructures are supposed to have large specific surface area, abundant active sites, and enhanced crystallinity, thereby benefiting further improvement in photocatalytic activity. Xue and co-workers88 reported a novel preparation method, namely “microwave-assisted heating synthesis”, for the preparation of highly crystalline g-C3N4 within minutes. The prepared g-C3N4 showed enhanced photocatalytic H2 production ability with a H2 evolution rate of 2000 μmol h−1 g−1, which was about 1.5-fold higher than that of pure g-C3N4 (1300 μmol h−1 g−1). The enhancement was attributed to the enhanced crystalline quality. Sun and co-workers89 reported a refined alternated cooling and heating treatment strategy for the fast preparation of highly crystalline few-layer g-C3N4 nanosheets within 10 min. The prepared ultrathin g-C3N4 nanosheets showed high crystallinity, narrowed band gap (∼2.62 eV), increased specific surface area (88.59 m2 g−1) and improved electron transport ability. As a result, a significant enhancement of photocatalytic properties was achieved.
In order to obtain highly crystalline g-C3N4 nanosheets, combining both supramolecular pre-assembly and exfoliation strategies is commonly considered as an effective technique. However, a perfect crystalline structure of g-C3N4 is difficult to obtain at current levels. The exploration and development of perfect crystalline g-C3N4 will be a hot research topic in future because it is beneficial for us to analyze the g-C3N4 specific structure and achieve higher photocatalytic performance. A simple calcination method seems to be a better route to weaken intralayer hydrogen bond interactions, guaranteeing highly crystalline g-C3N4 nanosheets with a relatively high H2 evolution rate of 9577 μmol h−1 g−1 (Table 3). Obviously, advanced and suitable technologies for achieving perfect crystalline g-C3N4 are worth exploring in future.
3.3. Doped g-C3N4 for HER application
When g-C3N4 is employed as photocatalysts, its inefficient utilization of visible light and high recombination of the photoexcited charge carriers lead to unsatisfactory photocatalytic performance. Heteroatom doping (B,197 F,212 I,196 C,213 P,214–216 S217) can effectively ameliorate the structural defects and dominate the electronic band structure of g-C3N4 to enlarge the visible-light absorption and regulate the redox potentials to further enhance the photocatalytic properties in the visible-light region. Wang and co-workers218 developed a new type of B-doped g-C3N4 nanosheets via a simple one-pot pyrolysis strategy. A B-doping amount of 1 at% into g-C3N4 exhibited the highest photocatalytic H2 evolution rate (ca. 1880 μmol h−1 g−1) under visible-light illumination (λ > 400 nm), which was over 12-fold higher than that of pristine bulk g-C3N4. B-Doping reduced the band gap to absorb visible-light more easily and developed higher specific surface area (49.47 m2 g−1) compared to that of bulk g-C3N4 (8.24 m2 g−1), which consequently improved the photocatalytic activity dramatically. Wang and co-workers212 reported a simple fluorination route for the preparation of a novel F-doped g-C3N4 employing ammonium fluoride as a cheap fluorine source. Fluorination not only provided a modified texture but also enabled the effective regulation of the electronic band gaps. The F-doped g-C3N4 exhibited improved photocatalytic properties, namely, the H2 evolution rate of F-doped g-C3N4 was ca. 2.7-fold higher than that of pure g-C3N4. Zhang's group prepared a highly efficient I-doped g-C3N4 photocatalyst via ball-milling g-C3N4 with iodine. The optimized I-doped g-C3N4 sample showed a high H2 evolution rate (ca. 44.5 μmol h−1) under visible-light. Teng and co-workers219 firstly annealed a mixture of urea and NH4Cl to prepare g-C3N4 nanosheets, which was then mixed with C2H5OH and annealed to obtain aromatic C-doped g-C3N4. Annealing C2H5OH resulted in the grafting of aromatic heterocycles on the g-C3N4 nanosheets. The grafted aromatic heterocycles and doped C atoms extended the π-conjugation system in g-C3N4 to decrease the band gap and facilitated the separation of photogenerated charges. The obtained aromatic C-doped g-C3N4 could effectively photocatalyze H2 production from aqueous solution, realizing apparent quantum yields of 14% and 2.2% under 420 and 550 nm monochromatic irradiation, respectively. Qiao and co-workers10 prepared porous P-doped g-C3N4 nanosheets via combining P doping and thermal exfoliation strategies for the first time (Fig. 9a). The obtained P-doped g-C3N4 nanosheets displayed improved visible-light absorption (>557 nm), increased specific surface area (122.6 m2 g−1), shortened charge-to-surface migration length (5–8 nm) and enhanced charge mobility. As a result, an ultrahigh visible-light photocatalytic H2 evolution rate (1596 μmol h−1 g−1) and apparent quantum efficiency (3.56%) at 420 nm were obtained (Fig. 9b and c). This eminent photocatalytic property was attributed to the P-doped conjugated system and the macroporous nanosheet morphology (Fig. 8e). In particular, the empty midgap states (−0.16 V vs. standard hydrogen electrode) produced by P doping can be used to explain the improved visible-light absorption (>557 nm) (Fig. 9d), and the macroporous structure boosted the mass-transfer process and enhanced light harvesting (Fig. 9f). This work presents a facile, environment-friendly and reliable strategy to prepare highly efficient porous g-C3N4 nanosheet photocatalysts, and also paves a new way for the rational design and development of advanced g-C3N4-based photocatalysts by taking full advantage of the strong synergistic effects via simultaneously tuning and optimizing the electronic, crystallographic, surface and textural structures. Cheng and co-workers217 reported S-doped g-C3N4 with a unique electronic structure that exhibited an enhanced valence bandwidth combined with an increased conduction band minimum and a decreased absorbance. Such S-doped g-C3N4 displayed a photocatalytic H2 evolution rate ca. 7.2 and 8.0-fold higher than that of g-C3N4 under λ > 300 and 420 nm, respectively.
 Fig. 9  (a) Preparation process of porous P-doped g-C3N4 nanosheets. (b) Contrast of the photocatalytic property of different g-C3N4 samples. (c) Apparent quantum efficiency of porous P-doped g-C3N4 nanosheets at 420 nm, 460 nm, 500 nm or 540 nm. (d) Electronic band structures of different g-C3N4 samples. (e) TEM image of porous P-doped g-C3N4 nanosheets with the inset showing the high magnification TEM image, the AFM image and the corresponding height profile of porous P-doped g-C3N4 nanosheets. This figure has been reproduced from ref. 10 with permission from the Royal Society of Chemistry. 
3D hierarchical self-doped g-C3N4 architectures are highly desired. This is mainly because (1) 3D hierarchical nanostructures can significantly promote the photogenerated charge separation and transfer, resulting in a much longer lifetime of the charge carriers; (2) 3D hierarchical nanostructures can produce multiple light scattering, which facilitates light harvesting; and (3) 3D nanostructures provide more exposed active catalytic sites for surface reactions, which acts as a pivotal part in shortening the charge migration distance. Inspired by this, Xu and co-workers220 prepared 3D hierarchical self-doped g-C3N4 architectures (3D CCNS) through an extremely facile hexamethylenetetramine activation approach at the bulk scale. The unique composition and structure of 3D CCNS endow it with an outstanding light absorption with a tunable band gap, high electrical conductivity, fast charge separation, and large specific surface areas with abundant reaction active sites, and thus remarkably enhanced photocatalytic H2 generation property. The optimized 3D CCNS-50 displayed an unprecedented H2 generation performance, an ultrahigh visible-light photocatalytic H2 evolution rate (27035 μmol h−1 g−1) and apparent quantum efficiency (7.8%) at 420 nm. The work also offered an extremely facile strategy toward the development of highly efficient doped-g-C3N4-based photocatalysts for large-scale production and practical applications. Interestingly, this work innovatively used hexamethylenetetramine for thermal oxidation etching of bulk g-C3N4, thus providing a promising opportunity to large-scale production of homogeneous self-modified ultrathin g-C3N4 nanosheets.
In addition to the non-metallic atom-doped g-C3N4 mentioned above, many metallic atom-doped g-C3N4 photocatalysts have also been studied. Among these metallic atoms, K atoms have recently been widely used as doping atoms to prepare g-C3N4.39,221–223 Yan and co-workers221 reported a simple KCl-template method to prepare K-doped g-C3N4. Because of the variable thickness of K-doped g-C3N4, it possessed superior photocatalytic activity and stability for photocatalytic HER under visible-light irradiation to pure g-C3N4. Zhang and co-workers223 prepared K-doped g-C3N4via a facile thermal polycondensation strategy employing KBr as the K source. The KBr content strongly affected the photocatalytic performance of the K-doped g-C3N4, and the photocatalytic H2 evolution rate of the optimized K-doped g-C3N4 nanosheets (10 wt% KBr) was as high as 1337.2 μmol h−1 g−1, which was ca. 5.6-fold higher than that of pure g-C3N4 (239.8 μmol h−1 g−1). This is due to the narrower band gap and improved light harvesting property of K-doped g-C3N4. Similarly to K, other alkali metals such as Li and Na were also examined. A comparative research of alkali metal (Li, Na, K)-doped g-C3N4 was presented by Cao and co-workers.224 They prepared three alkali metal (Li, Na, K)-doped g-C3N4 photocatalysts by a simple high-temperature treatment strategy. All of them showed improved visible-light photocatalytic properties for HER, benefited from the improved specific surface areas, reduced band gaps for enlarged visible-light absorption, and enhanced electronic structures for efficient charge transfer. Particularly, owing to the optimal regulation of micro- and electronic-structure, the Na-doped g-C3N4 showed the most effective exploitation of the photogenerated electrons during the photocatalytic HER process. In consequence, the highest photocatalytic H2 evolution rate was achieved through the Na-doped g-C3N4 (374 μmol h−1), which was ca. 3.7-fold higher than that of pristine g-C3N4 (100 μmol h−1). Similar results have also been confirmed by Meng's group.225
Other metallic atom-doped g-C3N4 photocatalysts have also attracted wide attention in HER application. Zhou and co-workers226 presented a facile one-step pyrolysis route to prepare Fe-doped g-C3N4 nanosheets using NH4Cl as the dynamic gas template and FeCl3 as the Fe source. The doping of Fe could effectively promote the exfoliation and further increase the specific surface area, but superfluous Fe might break the nanosheet structure. The specific surface area of the optimized Fe-doped g-C3N4 nanosheets was up to 236.52 m2 g−1, which was 2.5-fold higher than that of g-C3N4 nanosheets. The H2 evolution rate of the optimized Fe-doped g-C3N4 nanosheets was as high as 536 μmol h−1 g−1, which was 1.8- and 6-fold higher than that of the pure g-C3N4 nanosheets and bulk g-C3N4, respectively. Jiang and co-workers227 fabricated a series of mesoporous Cu-doped g-C3N4 photocatalysts with Cu contents from 0.1 to 5 wt% by using CuCl2 and melamine as precursors. The embedded Cu2+ transformed the optical properties, influenced the energy band structure, and improved the electron–hole separation rate and thus enhanced the photocatalytic properties. Zhang and co-workers228 synthesized Mn-doped g-C3N4 nanoribbons via a two-step calcination approach. Mn doping facilitated the formation of ˙OH radicals from H2O2 intermediate products. In particular, the obtained Mn-doped g-C3N4 exhibited excellent photocatalytic properties for HER. The yields of H2 and O2 could reach 593.35 and 59.47 μmol g−1 after 3 h illumination. Ye and co-workers229 presented a facile soft-chemical approach to prepare Zn-doped g-C3N4. Compared with other metal atoms, Zn was easier to incorporate into g-C3N4 without destroying its graphite-like structure. The H2 evolution rate of Zn-doped g-C3N4 was as high as 297.5 μmol h−1 g−1, over 10-fold higher than that of pure g-C3N4. Moreover, the maximum quantum yield was up to 3.2% at 420 nm.
In fact, heteroatom doping is regarded to introduce impurities into g-C3N4, with the doping atoms either replacing lattice atoms or residing in the in-planar cave of g-C3N4, and the hybridization between the dopant orbitals and the molecular orbitals of g-C3N4 enables tuning the electronic structure and the positions of the valence band and conduction band. Many heteroatom doped g-C3N4 photocatalysts (e.g., non-metallic and metallic doped g-C3N4) have been successfully constructed; however, they have not met our expectation because most of them only achieve a moderate photocatalytic activity. A gratifying result is that the Na-doped g-C3N4 nanotubes display a relatively high H2 evolution rate of 7150 μmol h−1 g−1 (Table 3), which is mainly ascribed to Na doping and particularly the porous nanostructure of the g-C3N4 nanotubes. Thus, combining the doping technologies and the nanostructural engineering is highly worth considering in the design and development of doped g-C3N4 photocatalysts.
3.4. Porous g-C3N4 for HER application
The morphology modulation and nanostructured designing of g-C3N4, especially forming porous g-C3N4, has been proverbially illustrated as an effective route to enhance the photocatalytic properties for HER mainly via promoting light absorption, charge transfer and separation, active site exposure, and mass diffusion during the photocatalytic process.230,231 With regard to porous g-C3N4 nanosheets, the photogenerated electrons and holes can easily transfer to the g-C3N4 surface, and subsequently participate in the redox reactions to obtain a high photocatalytic property. Zhang and co-workers232 reported a facile route to prepare porous g-C3N4 with stacking nanotubes via direct pyrolysis of melamine–ammonium oxalate stacking nanosheets. The photocatalytic H2 evolution rate of porous g-C3N4 was about 1994 μmol h−1 g−1, which was about 9.5-fold higher than that of pristine g-C3N4 (210 μmol h−1 g−1). Such an improvement was mainly ascribed to the large specific surface area (91.1 m2 g−1), enhanced visible-light absorption and efficient separation of the photogenerated electrons and holes. Huang and co-workers161 presented a novel and unparalleled template-free precursor pre-treatment strategy to prepare porous g-C3N4 nanosheets for highly efficient photocatalytic reduction and oxidation reaction (Fig. 10a). The addition of thiourea solution in the hydrothermal pre-treatment process etched the surface of melamine, thus producing the porous and thin g-C3N4 nanosheets (Fig. 10b). And the specific surface area of the optimized porous g-C3N4 nanosheets was up to 44.2 m2 g−1 (Fig. 10c). The separation of photogenerated charge carriers was prominently boosted by constructing such a porous nanosheet structure (Fig. 10d). Benefited from these advantages, porous g-C3N4 nanosheets showed markedly ameliorative visible-light photocatalytic properties for HER (991 μmol h−1 g−1), which was 3.3-fold higher than that of pristine g-C3N4 (297 μmol h−1 g−1) (Fig. 10e). This work offers a new and alternative synthesis strategy to prepare high-performance porous g-C3N4 nanosheets for photocatalytic HER application. Xu and co-workers192 prepared porous g-C3N4 nanosheets through a facile liquid exfoliation strategy. These mesoporous g-C3N4 nanosheets with thickness (ca. 5 nm) possessed high specific surface area and improved light absorption. These endowed them with enhanced photocatalytic properties for HER compared with pure g-C3N4.
 Fig. 10  (a) Diagrammatic sketch of the synthesis, (b) SEM images, (c) nitrogen adsorption–desorption isotherms and pore size distribution curves (inset), and (d) band structure diagram of the porous g-C3N4 nanosheets. (e) Photocatalytic H2 evolution rate of different g-C3N4 samples under visible-light (λ > 420 nm). This figure has been reproduced from ref. 161 with permission from the Royal Society of Chemistry. 
As we know, specific surface area plays a critical role in photocatalytic reactions by affording more active sites.233 The most important strategy for the preparation of porous g-C3N4 with a large surface area is based on template methods, such as soft template (self-assembly)24 and hard template (nanocasting).234 Majima and co-workers235 prepared g-C3N4 with a nanosheet structure using a mixture of melamine and urea as the precursors. The use of urea created a mesoporous structure, increased the surface area and defects of N vacancies acting as trap states in g-C3N4. The obtained g-C3N4 exhibited a specific surface area of ca. 299 m2 g−1, photocatalytic H2 evolution rate of ca. 3100 μmol h−1 g−1 under λ ≥ 400 nm light irradiation, and apparent quantum efficiency of ca. 74% at λ = 400 nm. The improved photocatalytic properties were presumably because the prepared g-C3N4 possessed larger specific surface area and more mesopores to enhance the electron transfer and offer more active sites compared with pure g-C3N4.
Besides, a self-assembled supramolecular precursor, a melamine–cyanaurate acid complex, has emerged as an interesting intermediate to convert into the 3D porous nanostructures of g-C3N4.115,116 It has been reported that the melamine–cyanaurate acid complex supramolecular aggregates are usually prepared via the assembly of melamine and cyanuric acid and connected by hydrogen bonding.236 Thus, the porous g-C3N4 nanosheets prepared by the melamine–cyanaurate acid complex supramolecular aggregates possessed reduced structural defects, showing the enhanced H2 evolution rates.237 Moreover, various g-C3N4 nanostructures including ordered porous microsheets and microtubular structures could also be prepared in different organic solvents and at different precipitation temperatures. Fang and co-workers238 firstly used the hydrothermal process to transfer dicyandiamide into the melamine–cyanaurate acid-like complex, and then developed uniform 3D holey g-C3N4 nanosheets by annealing the two precursors. The H2 evolution rate of the prepared 3D holey g-C3N4 nanosheets was up to 2738 μmol h−1 g−1, which was 2.5-fold higher than that of the one obtained from the conventional melamine–cyanaurate acid complex and 15 times higher than that of bulk g-C3N4, and its apparent quantum efficiency reached 10.6% under visible-light (λ = 420 ± 15 nm).
A large specific surface area can enhance mass transfer ability and provide more active sites, which can further enhance the photocatalytic activity of g-C3N4. Porosity engineering is an effective strategy to ameliorate the pore structure of g-C3N4 and enhance its specific surface area and accordingly the photocatalytic HER activity. Unfortunately, most of the reported porous g-C3N4 photocatalysts only achieve a moderate photocatalytic activity (Table 3). The unsatisfactory results are mainly because their specific surface area is still relatively small. Thus, the future work should focus on the exploration of new and advanced methods for the construction of porous g-C3N4 to meet the needs of highly efficient photocatalysts.
4. Composites of g-C3N4 as highly efficient photocatalysts for HER application
Similar to the traditional inorganic semiconductor photocatalysts (such as TiO2, ZnO, SnO2, GaN, etc.), g-C3N4 also suffers from several intrinsic shortcomings, e.g., rapid recombination rate of charge carriers, low electroconductivity and the lack of visible-light absorption above 460 nm.240 To obtain highly-active g-C3N4-based photocatalysts, three critical criteria must be met: (i) facilitating the charge migration and separation to restrain the recombination rate, (ii) enhancing visible-light utilization, and (iii) improving photochemical stability of the photocatalysts for prolonged duration. These criteria have gradually brought g-C3N4 composites to researchers’ attention. Usually, g-C3N4 composites can form heterojunctions and provide a series of improvements: (i) the visible-light absorption is enhanced, (ii) the existence of cocatalysts reduces the redox overpotential at the active sites, (iii) charge migration and separation is accelerated via a Schottky junction or p–n junction, and (iv) the system stability is enhanced by defending the active sites and functional groups on the semiconductor surface via appropriate surface passivation.61 In the following subsections, we will elaborate various g-C3N4-based composites for HER application. In addition, a performance comparison of highly efficient g-C3N4-based composite photocatalysts for HER application is shown in Table 4.

4.1. Composites of metal and g-C3N4 for HER application
The incorporation of metal nanoparticles into/onto g-C3N4 has been used as a fascinating approach to form a metal/g-C3N4 heterojunction structure via a coherent interface.241 As is said above, the metal ions can coordinate with the lone pair electrons of N atoms of the g-C3N4 framework, forming metal-doped g-C3N4. As a supplement, here we will discuss metal/g-C3N4 composites via the introduction of metal nanoparticles into/onto g-C3N4 for HER application. Under visible-light illumination, the generated energetic electrons on metal nanoparticle surfaces due to the localized plasma resonance effect are transferred to the conduction band of g-C3N4 to realize HER. Here, metal nanoparticles act as electronic conductive channels to efficiently separate the photogenerated electron–hole pairs and to further improve the visible-light photocatalytic performance of g-C3N4.
Up to now, there have been many papers with the topic words “band bending”, but most of the works are focused on solid state physics, and rarely involve “band bending” combined with “photochemistry” or “photocatalysis”.242 Band bending was first proposed by Schottky and Mott to interpret the rectifying effect of metal–semiconductor contacts.243–245 Surface band bending is the driving force of photogenerated charge separation. The deeper the degree of surface band bending is, the better the separation efficiency.4,246 As an active transition non-noble metal, Ni plays a significant role in photocatalytic systems, which has been widely used in g-C3N4-based photocatalysts. The introduction of Ni results in a change in the surface band bending, and thus enhances the separation efficiency. Ni/g-C3N4 composites were prepared through a solvothermal strategy in the presence of melamine and acetylacetone nickel.247 Ni nanoparticles with a particle size of ca. 30 nm were found to be uniformly distributed on the 2D g-C3N4 nanosheet surface with a lattice spacing of ca. 0.209 nm, which corresponded to the (111) plane of metallic Ni. The high distribution of Ni nanoparticles on the g-C3N4 nanosheet surface was beneficial to the separation of charge carriers and effectively prohibited the recombination process, which would further improve its photocatalytic activity for H2 production.247 Another related study was presented by Dong and co-workers,248 who reported a rapid light-assisted approach to prepare Ni/g-C3N4 composite photocatalysts. The Ni nanoparticles were obtained by the reduction of Ni(II) via photogenerated electrons and uniformly distributed on the electron outlet points of g-C3N4 in 30 minutes. The H2 evolution rate of the optimized Ni/g-C3N4 composites reached 4318 μmol h−1 g−1, and there is no evident loss in photocatalytic properties after 4 runs for 48 h. Moreover, the obtained Ni/g-C3N4 composites exhibited an ultrahigh H2 evolution rate (ca. 4000 μmol h−1 g−1) under natural sunlight outdoor conditions, implying that the introduction of Ni effectively restrained the recombination of the photogenerated electrons and holes of g-C3N4 and thus further enhanced the photocatalytic properties.
It is generally known that a Schottky barrier is constructed when metal nanoparticles are hybridized with g-C3N4, which is beneficial for charge carrier separation because of the rapid electron migration between the metal and g-C3N4.249 As a matter of fact, the fabrication of the Schottky barrier leads to the formation of electrons and holes in the metal and semiconductor, respectively.250,251 Thus, a Schottky barrier acts as an effective electron trapping site to restrain the electron–hole recombination for improving photocatalytic activity.252 Furthermore, metals, e.g., Au and Ag, possess the potential to mightily utilize visible-light due to their surface plasmon resonance (SPR) effect.253–257 Recently, the fabrication of metal/g-C3N4 nanocomposite photocatalysts with well-controlled and well-defined morphologies by the metal–organic supramolecular approach has been reported, which fully integrated the SPR effect and shape-dependent optical and electronic properties. Gao and co-workers258 reported a new and facile supramolecular hydrogel approach as a self-template to fabricate porous nanofiber-type Ag/g-C3N4 nanocomposites (Fig. 11a); here, Ag–melamine supramolecular hydrogels with nanofiber structures acted as both self-templated agents and promising precursors. The prepared Ag/g-C3N4 nanofibers possess high specific surface area (26.0 m2 g−1), enlarged absorption in the visible-light region (Fig. 11b and c), and enhanced photoinduced electron–hole separation capability (Fig. 11d). The obtained porous fiber-type Ag/g-C3N4 showed high photocatalytic performance under visible-light illumination (625 μmol h−1 g−1), which was nearly 6.6-fold higher than that of pristine g-C3N4 (Fig. 11e). The introduction of Ag is beneficial to form controllable porous nanofiber nanostructures, as well as enhance the specific surface areas and the visible-light absorption, prolong the lifetime of charge carries, and accelerate the separation of photogenerated electron–hole pairs. All of these results cause greatly improved photocatalytic properties.
 Fig. 11  (a) Preparation of porous nanofiber-like Ag/g-C3N4 from Ag–melamine supramolecular hydrogels. (b) Nitrogen sorption isotherm of fiber-type Ag/g-C3N4. (c) Solid state UV-vis diffusion reflectance spectra of Ag/g-C3N4 and g-C3N4. (d) Steady state photoluminescence spectra of Ag/g-C3N4 and g-C3N4 with an excitation wavelength of 375 nm. (e) Time resolved photoluminescence decay spectra of Ag/g-C3N4 and g-C3N4, monitored at 488, 488, and 577 nm, respectively. This figure has been reproduced from ref. 258 with permission from the American Chemical Society. 
As mentioned above, strong visible light absorption features and size- and shape-dependent optical and electronic properties of metal nanoparticles make an interesting case for photocatalysts by accelerating interfacial charge migration in g-C3N4–metal nanocomposites.259,260 A plasmonic photocatalyst composed of Au nanoparticles and a polar semiconductor g-C3N4 has demonstrated great potential in photocatalysis. Among various metal nanoparticles, Au nanoparticles worked as a visible-light absorber and a thermal redox active centre.261 In consideration of the advantages of Au nanoparticles, it is expected that the photocatalytic properties of g-C3N4 can be considerably improved after the introduction of Au nanoparticles. Parida and co-workers262 reported a simple deposition–precipitation approach for the construction of Au/g-C3N4 composites. The prepared Au/g-C3N4 composite showed an improved photocatalytic performance, e.g., the H2 evolution rate was up to 8870 μmol h−1 g−1, which was 23-fold higher than that of neat g-C3N4 (383 μmol h−1 g−1) (Fig. 12c), indicating that the size, shape and uniform dispersion of Au nanoparticles on the g-C3N4 surface were advantageous to improve the photocatalytic activity. This enhancement was because of the synergic mechanism between the conduction band of g-C3N4 and the plasmonic band of Au nanoparticles (Fig. 12a and b). The photocatalytic mechanism of Au/g-C3N4 composites was also illustrated (Fig. 12d) and the details were given as follows. The decorated Au nanoparticles on the g-C3N4 surface were charge-polarized, actively absorbed, and scattered the visible light through the plasmonic effect. This effect increased the visible-light absorption capability of the Au/g-C3N4 composites. When visible light irradiated polar g-C3N4 semiconductor material surfaces, conduction band electrons and valence band holes were then generated, leading to the formation of a Mott–Schottky junction at the Au/g-C3N4 interface. The Fermi level of the Au existed between the conduction band maximum and valence band minimum of polar semiconductor g-C3N4, which resulted in the formation of plasmonic photocatalysts. The excited electrons from the conduction band of g-C3N4 readily migrated to the Au NPs surface, leading to the increment of electron density and thus improving their photocatalytic performance. This work provides a comprehensive enhancement mechanism of metal nanoparticles, namely, the SPR effect and size- and shape-dependent optical and electronic properties are the root of the enhancement. It is readily possible to tune the photocatalytic properties of g-C3N4–metal photocatalysts through rational design and regulate the size and morphology of metal nanoparticles, further achieving the optimal photocatalytic properties.
 Fig. 12  (a) Estimated band gap energies and (b) photocurrent spectra of the Au/g-C3N4 composite and neat g-C3N4. (c) Photocatalytic HER activity of the g-C3N4 and all the Au/g-C3N4 composites and the neat g-C3N4 under visible-light illumination (λ ≥ 400 nm). (d) Mechanism of photocatalytic H2 generation and surface plasmon resonance of Au in Au/g-C3N4 composites. This figure has been reproduced from ref. 262 with permission from John Wiley and Sons. 
The interaction between the metal and semiconductor plays an irreplaceable role in promoting the migration of the photoinduced electrons to noble metals.263 Inspired by this, Shiraishi's group264 presented the decoration of Pt nanoparticles with a diameter of <4 nm on the g-C3N4 surface via reducing H2PtCl6·6H2O at 673 K. The strong interaction between Pt and g-C3N4 generated by high temperature treatment promoted smooth migration of the photogenerated conduction band electrons to Pt nanoparticles and efficiently formed the electron–hole charge-separated state. Thus, the prepared Pt/g-C3N4 composites can be utilized as promising photocatalysts for photocatalytic HER under visible-light. Yu and co-workers265 prepared different morphologies (cubic, octahedral and spherical) Pt nanoparticles decorated on the g-C3N4 surface by using a colloidal adsorption–deposition approach. These Pt nanoparticles with different shapes possessed similar sizes (ca. 10 nm) but different exposed facets. The visible-light-guided photocatalytic activity for the Pt/g-C3N4 composites followed the order: cubic Pt/g-C3N4 < octahedral Pt/g-C3N4 < spherical Pt/g-C3N4. This is mainly attributed to the fact that the different surface atomic structures of the different exposed facets of Pt nanoparticles result in a difference in active sites and adsorption energies in photocatalytic reactions.
The surface facets of metal nanoparticles have a tremendous impact on the molecular adsorption and activation, thus, the facet design of metal cocatalysts holds the promise for adjusting the reaction selectivity in metal/g-C3N4 photocatalysts. Xiong and co-workers266 presented a solution-phase route to in situ load facet-dependent Pd nanoparticles on a 2D g-C3N4 platform. Different capping agents were used to prepare Pd and (111) facets. For example, KBr/KI and HCHO/Na2C2O4 were employed as morphology controlling agents to stabilize the Pd(100) and (111) facets, respectively. During the preparation process, the introduction of polyvinylpyrrolidone was used to prevent the Pd nanocrystals from agglomeration and facilitate the reduction of Pd precursors with its –OH end groups (Fig. 13a). It was found that the well-loaded Pd nanoparticles possessed edge lengths of 4–6 nm on the g-C3N4 nanosheets, and Pd nanoparticles with a cubic shape exposed preferential (100) facets, but (111)-faceted Pd nanocrystals showed a tetrahedral shape (Fig. 13b–e). As shown in Fig. 13f, the addition of Pd to the g-C3N4 nanosheets significantly enhanced their photocurrents, indicating that the g-C3N4–Pd Schottky junction helped to separate the photo-generated electron–hole pairs. The photoluminescence of g-C3N4 was substantially quenched with the addition of Pd (Fig. 13g). This indicated that the recombination of electrons and holes had been restrained by distilling the electrons with Pd. The prepared g-C3N4–Pd nanocubes and g-C3N4–Pd nanotetrahedra showed an enhanced photocatalytic property, and the H2 evolution rate was up to 39.8 and 9.7 μmol h−1 g−1, respectively, which was 23.4- and 5.7-fold higher than that of the bulk g-C3N4 (1.7 μmol h−1 g−1), respectively (Fig. 13h).
 Fig. 13  (a) Diagrammatic sketch of the fabrication of Pd/g-C3N4 composites using various capping agents. TEM images of (b) g-C3N4–Pd nanocubes with Pd(100) facets and (c) g-C3N4–Pd nanotetrahedrons with Pd(111) facets. HRTEM images of (d) a g-C3N4–Pd nanocube and (e) a g-C3N4–Pd nanotetrahedron. (f) Photocurrent vs. potential responses of photoelectrodes made of Pd/g-C3N4 composites and bulk and ultrathin g-C3N4 samples, respectively, at the same g-C3N4 loading weight, measured in a 0.5 M Na2SO4 electrolyte under chopped visible-light (λ > 400 nm) irradiation. (g) PL emission spectra of Pd/g-C3N4 composites and bulk and ultrathin g-C3N4 samples (at the same amount of g-C3N4), respectively, excited at 360 nm. (h) H2 evolution rates observed for the g-C3N4–Pd nanocubes and g-C3N4–Pd nanotetrahedra. This figure has been reproduced from ref. 266 with permission from the Royal Society of Chemistry. 
The incorporation of metal into g-C3N4 can improve its conductivity and decrease the energy barrier of the interface reactions to further enhance photocatalytic HER activity. A relatively high H2 evolution rate of 8870 μmol h−1 g−1 was achieved by Au/g-C3N4 (Table 4), which was mainly attributed to the synergic mechanism between the conduction band minimum of g-C3N4 and the plasmonic band of Au nanoparticles. In particular, the size and size distribution of the metal nanoparticles have a huge impact on the photocatalytic performance of g-C3N4. Therefore, surface functionalization of g-C3N4 to ensure the strong interaction between metal nanoparticles and g-C3N4 is a good design idea. However, in consideration of high price of metals especially noble metals, a combination of multiple components with different functions (e.g., combination of a tiny amount of metal and other components) should be an effective solution.
4.2. Composites of bimetal and g-C3N4 for HER application
Compared with monometallic nanoparticles which have advantageous charge separation to lengthen the lifetime of electron–hole pairs, bimetallic nanocrystals have a brighter prospect in photocatalytic applications because of their extraordinary structural properties and synergistic effect between metals. This is a fact that various components in the bimetallic system possess a designated function in affecting catalytic properties. Thus, bimetallic/g-C3N4 composites have great potential in the field of photocatalysts. Ge and co-workers267 reported a novel AuPd bimetallic loaded g-C3N4 prepared via an in situ chemical deposition route. The well-distributed AuPd nanocrystals created intact interfaces with g-C3N4, which helped the efficient separation of electron–hole pairs. Superoxide radicals are the main oxidative species for the AuPd/g-C3N4 composites, and the existence of AuPd nanocrystals could enhance the interfacial charge transfer and restrain the recombination of electron–hole pairs. Owing to these advantages, the 0.5 wt% AuPd/g-C3N4 composite showed the highest catalytic activity with a H2 evolution rate of 326 μmol h−1 g−1, which was 3.5- and 1.6-fold higher than those of pristine Au/g-C3N4 and Pd/g-C3N4 under visible-light irradiation.
As mentioned above, when g-C3N4 contacts metal, metal serves as an electron capture agent to restrain the recombination of electron–hole pairs due to the relatively lower Fermi levels of metal, which further improves the lifetime of charge carriers and remarkably improves the photocatalytic activity.268 In comparison with single metal, bimetallic nanoparticles exhibit greater potential in photocatalytic applications due to their unique microstructure and synergistic effect of different components. It has been illustrated that the activity, selectivity and resistance to poisoning of the metal catalysts can be significantly improved due to the existence of a second metal component.269 Inspired by the merits of bimetallic systems, a PtCo/g-C3N4 system was fabricated via an in situ chemical deposition route (Fig. 14a). The PtCo nanocrystals were uniformly distributed on the g-C3N4 nanosheets. The PtCo/g-C3N4 composites promoted electron migration from g-C3N4 to PtCo nanocrystals, resulting in the accumulation of electrons on the PtCo nanocrystal surface (Fig. 14b and c). The optimal loading content of PtCo nanocrystals was measured to be 1.0 wt% for g-C3N4 nanosheets, and the corresponding H2 evolution rate was as high as 960 μmol h−1 g−1. The improved photocatalytic mechanism is explained as follows (Fig. 14d): the binary alloy showed different structures and electronic density of states when compared to the single metals, leading to adjustable degeneration of physical and chemical properties, as well as surface states of PtCo nanocrystals. The “synergistic effect” of the alloy will affect the Fermi energy level of the PtCo alloy. The Fermi energy levels of the PtCo alloy were lower than the g-C3N4 conduction band, which made the photo-excited electrons transfer from the conduction band of g-C3N4 to the PtCo alloy surface and resulted in efficient separation of electron–hole pairs. Furthermore, for the PtCo alloy, the “synergistic effect” of Pt and Co components made the surface defects increase, and changed the Fermi energy level position. Hence, the introduction of the Co element led to more ready capture of the photo-excited electrons in the PtCo alloy, and further enhanced photocatalytic performance. For the PtCo/g-C3N4 system, the photo-generated electrons in the conduction band of the g-C3N4 transferred to the PtCo co-catalyst through contacting interfaces, endowing the conduction band electrons with higher mobility and boosting the separation of electron–hole pairs. Therefore, the recombination process of the electron–hole pairs was effectively inhibited, leading to an obvious enhancement of H2 production (Fig. 14e).270 This work provides a comprehensive and detailed enhancement mechanism of bimetallic systems, which is beneficial to guide researchers to deeply understand the enhancement mechanism and develop novel cost-effective and high performance bimetal/g-C3N4 photocatalysts.
 Fig. 14  (a) Diagrammatic sketch of the preparation route for PtCo/g-C3N4 photocatalysts (bulk and nanosheets). (b) Photovoltage and (d) phase of the surface photovoltage spectroscopy spectra of pure g-C3N4 and 1 wt% PtCo/g-C3N4. (c) Diagrammatic sketch of photo-excited electron injection from the conduction band of g-C3N4 to the PtCo nanocrystals and Pt nanoparticles, and (d) diagrammatic sketch for electron charge migration and H2 evolution mechanism under visible-light irradiation. This figure has been reproduced from ref. 270 with permission from the Royal Society of Chemistry. 
Bimetal decorated g-C3N4 photocatalysts can potentially realize highly efficient photocatalytic H2 generation because of their unique architecture, electronic structure, and synergistic effect. Nadeem and co-workers271 reported Pd–Ag bimetallic and monometallic nanoparticles coated on g-C3N4 through a simple chemical reduction approach. The prepared Pd0.7–Ag0.3/g-C3N4 composite with a total metal loading of 1 wt% displayed a H2 evolution rate of 1250 μmol h−1 g−1, which was 1.5- and 5.7-fold higher than those of Pd/g-C3N4 and Ag/g-C3N4 composites, respectively. The improvement was mainly ascribed to the inherent feature of bimetallic Pd–Ag to quench photogenerated electrons via the Schottky barrier formation mechanism. Liang and co-workers272 reported three g-C3N4 samples with Au, Pt, and Pt/Au doping, respectively, which were prepared by a traditional photodeposition approach. The photocatalytic properties of the Au/Pt co-doped g-C3N4 sample were higher than those of the Au doped g-C3N4 and Pt loaded g-C3N4 and pure g-C3N4, which was probably because of the photoabsorption properties and the separation of photogenerated electrons and holes.
In brief, the development of novel bimetal/g-C3N4 photocatalysts is beneficial for improving the photocatalytic properties with the wish of further exploration and understanding the charge carrier dynamics via effective combination of theory and experiment.
4.3. Composites of semiconductor and g-C3N4 for HER application
The development of a semiconductor–g-C3N4 heterojunction with appropriate valence band and conduction band potentials is deemed to be one of the highly efficient techniques for boosting charge migration and separation considering the space charge consumption and accumulation between two semiconductors.273 In the following subsections, various semiconductor/g-C3N4 composite photocatalysts for photocatalytic HER applications are summarized and discussed.

4.3.1. Composites of g-C3N4 and g-C3N4 for HER application. 
4.3.1.1. g-C3N4/g-C3N4 isotype heterojunctions for HER application. 
The photocatalytic properties of intrinsic g-C3N4 are severely restricted due to its insufficient visible-light absorbance, slow charge transport and rapid recombination of the photo-generated charge carriers. To overcome these inherent drawbacks, forming a g-C3N4 heterojunction has become one of the most effective approaches to enhance the photocatalytic properties of g-C3N4.274,275 Commonly, a g-C3N4-based heterojunction photocatalyst is obtained via using a g-C3N4 semiconductor photocatalyst and another semiconductor. This kind of semiconductor–g-C3N4 heterojunction nanocomposites will be introduced in detail in the following section. Here, we will mainly introduce g-C3N4 isotype heterojunctions. In recent years, Chen's group128 developed a microwave-assisted molten-salt strategy to prepare a triazine/heptazine based g-C3N4 isotype heterojunction by employing easily obtainable melamine as the precursor. The prepared triazine/heptazine based g-C3N4 sample exhibited a high photocatalytic H2 evolution rate (1480 μmol h−1 g−1), which was ca. 5 times higher than that of the g-C3N4 sample (300 μmol h−1 g−1) prepared by an electric-resistance molten-salt process, 15-fold higher than that of the g-C3N4 sample (95 μmol h−1 g−1) prepared by an electric-resistance heating process and 23-fold higher than that of the g-C3N4 sample (63 μmol h−1 g−1) prepared by a microwave heating process under visible-light irradiation. The synergistic effects of microwave-assisted and molten-salt liquid polymerization provided an effective route for fast and large-scale fabrication of g-C3N4/g-C3N4 isotype heterojunctions as metal-free highly efficient HER photocatalysts by employing a simple single-component precursor. Chen and co-workers276 reported a facile protonation-assisted hydrothermal method to fabricate a g-C3N4 isotype heterojunction structure via combining two different g-C3N4 samples (one prepared by protonating treatment of g-C3N4 derived from thiourea, another prepared by protonating treatment of g-C3N4 produced from urea). Protonation, as a promising functionalization strategy, not only offered g-C3N4 with better dispersion in the solvent and higher specific surface area, which was the limitation of other reported heterojunction construction methods, but also optimized the electronic band gap and improved the ionic conductivity, in turn leading to significantly enhanced photocatalytic activity.36,277,278 The prepared g-C3N4 isotype heterojunction photocatalyst exhibited improved photocatalytic properties for H2 production under visible-light illumination. This can be ascribed to the superhigh specific surface area and efficient charge separation and migration across the isotype heterojunction interface, which have been confirmed by the systematical investigations with electron paramagnetic resonance, photoluminescence and photocurrent measurements.
Considering that the band gap energies of g-C3N4 may cause a little bit of mediation using different precursors, coupling different components of g-C3N4 with a fully matched band nanostructure to form a g-C3N4/g-C3N4 isotype heterojunction showed improved photocatalytic properties. The fabrication of isotype heterojunctions between g-C3N4 and porous g-C3N4 is a recommended choice to boost the separation of electron–hole pairs across the interface for enhancing photocatalytic properties, where the porous structure is beneficial for the diffusion of reactants and products, as well as offers abundant surface active sites. Bao and co-workers279 prepared laminated mesoporous g-C3N4/g-C3N4 nanosheet isotype heterojunction photocatalysts via a template-calcination strategy using melamine and amino cyanamide as the co-precursors for the first time (Fig. 15a). The mesoporous structure endowed the as-developed g-C3N4/g-C3N4 material with a large specific surface area. Because the conduction band position of g-C3N4 is higher than that of mesoporous g-C3N4, in the process of photocatalytic HER, the photogenerated electrons tend to migrate from the conduction band of the g-C3N4 to mesoporous g-C3N4, which is beneficial to accelerate the separation of photogenerated charge carriers and decrease their recombination rates (Fig. 15b). A significantly enhanced H2 production rate was achieved over the mesoporous g-C3N4/g-C3N4 nanosheet isotype heterojunction (115.6 μmol h−1 g−1), which was approximately 6.5-fold higher than that of bare g-C3N4 (17.8 μmol h−1 g−1), and 1.7-fold higher relative to that of mesoporous g-C3N4 (68 μmol h−1 g−1) (Fig. 15c). The stability of the mesoporous g-C3N4/g-C3N4 photocatalyst was tested under irradiation for 25 h, and no obvious loss in H2 evolution activity was observed after five cycling experiments, implying high stability (Fig. 15d). By combining the isotype heterojunction with the mesoporous structure, a significant improvement in photocatalytic properties is achieved, which inspires us to explore an effective combination of multiple enhancement strategies, greatly improving visible-light harvesting and enhancing photocatalytic performance.
 Fig. 15  (a) Fabrication process and (b) transfer mechanism of the photogenerated electron–hole in a mesoporous g-C3N4/g-C3N4 nanosheet isotype heterojunction. CB, conduction band; VB, valence band. (c) The photocatalytic H2 evolution of different g-C3N4 samples, and (d) the recyclability tests of the mesoporous g-C3N4/g-C3N4 nanosheet isotype heterojunction. This figure has been reproduced from ref. 279 with permission from Elsevier. 
4.3.1.2. g-C3N4/g-C3N4 homojunctions for HER application. 
Because heterojunction-based photocatalysts are commonly fabricated by using different types of semiconductors, it is very difficult to employ them in practical application since the formation of heterojunctions is not only governed by the position of respective energy bands but drastically affected by their crystal structures, morphologies, light absorption performances and surface/interface/bulk properties of the individual semiconductors. In this case, the formation of homojunction photocatalysts composed of the same semiconductor materials exhibits more advantages than heterojunction based photocatalysts.188,280 Classically, g-C3N4 is a representative n-type material without terminal electron donor amine groups in the structure. The replacement of electron acceptor cyano groups with terminal amine can convert n-type g-C3N4 to p-type g-C3N4. In comparison with heterojunction photocatalysts, the homojunction photocatalysts, composed of the same semiconductor materials, exhibit better application value due to their unique crystal structures and surface/interface characteristics. Moreover, the composition identity on both sides of the interface in such homojunctions could provide a continuous band bending, and thus it is more conducive to accelerate charge transfer and carrier separation.281 Inspired by this, Ye and coworkers282 prepared a defect-modified g-C3N4 (DCN) p–n homojunction by treatment of g-C3N4 with NaBH4 (Fig. 16a). Here, the cyano terminal CN groups, acting as the electron acceptors, were introduced into the DCN nanosheet edges, which endowed the p–n homojunction with n- and p-type conductivity, and as a result, p–n homojunctions were formed. This homojunction nanostructure was demonstrated to accelerate the charge migration and separation (Fig. 16d–g), and led to a 5-fold improved photocatalytic property and excellent stability (Fig. 16b and c). Martha and co-workers283 developed a novel, one step biomediated synthetic path for the preparation of exfoliated g-C3N4 p–n homojunctions from melamine by a simple thermal condensation method. With a well-exfoliated layered nanosheet structure, the g-C3N4 exhibited exceptional physical properties such as superhigh intrinsic carrier mobility and obvious transformations in the energy band structure in virtue of weak van der Waals forces between C3N4 layers. The electron acceptor (–CN) groups were easily introduced to the edges of g-C3N4, which helped the in situ construction of p–n homojunctions. The exfoliated g-C3N4 homojunction showed significantly improved photocatalytic H2 production ability with a H2 evolution rate of 1740 μmol h−1 g−1 in contrast to those prepared through other conventional routes, because of the unique structural advantages, faster electron transfer, higher light harvesting, lower recombination rate of charge carriers and highly available reaction sites during the photocatalytic reaction. This work offers a defect-induced self-functionalization approach to build such a homojunction without adding any extra materials or elements, as well as displays a promising strategy for precise band engineering of g-C3N4 and related polymer semiconductors, and a new insight into the production of novel structures of these materials for more efficient photocatalytic HER applications.
 Fig. 16  (a) Illustration of the preparation process of DCN. The possible structures for CN and DCN are shown (gray, cyan, and white balls correspond to C, N, and H atoms, respectively). (b) Photocatalytic H2 evolution rate for CN and all DCN samples under visible-light irradiation. (c) Time courses of H2 evolution for the DCN-200 photocatalyst under visible-light irradiation with pristine CN as the reference. (d) Room-temperature electron paramagnetic resonance spectra for CN, DCN-200, and DCN-350 measured with and without light irradiation. (e) Photoluminescence spectra excited at 360 nm for CN and DCN samples. (f) Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy and (g) transient photocurrents (λ > 420 nm) for CN and DCN-200 (CN represents the g-C3N4, DCN represents the defect-modified g-C3N4, DCN-200 represents the defect-modified g-C3N4 prepared at 200 °C). This figure has been reproduced from ref. 282 with permission from John Wiley and Sons. 
In principle, a supramolecular complex can be obtained using melamine and other precursors to achieve an unprecedentedly intimate g-C3N4/g-C3N4 homojunction structure under hydrothermal conditions. Dong and co-workers284 prepared a g-C3N4/g-C3N4 metal-free homojunction via calcining the supramolecular precursor self-assembly with hydrothermal melamine and dicyandiamide. The prepared g-C3N4/g-C3N4 metal-free homojunction possessed a nanomesh morphology with a large specific surface area, and the in situ formation of the homojunction created abundant interface contacts, which improved the charge transfer ability and thus improved its photocatalytic properties. Zhou and co-workers285 prepared a novel g-C3N4/g-C3N4 n–n homojunction by in situ annealing two different precursors. The as-developed n–n homojunction showed a superior photocatalytic performance to its individual g-C3N4 counterparts. The enhanced photocatalytic property was explained by the synergistic effects between the two g-C3N4 in the homogeneous nanostructures, where a better charge separation was achieved in the g-C3N4/g-C3N4 n–n homojunction. Interestingly, this study broadened the meaning of synergistic effects and demonstrated that synergistic effects can be achieved by using the same materials of the same phase which were made by different methods. Liu's group firstly prepared g-C3N4 nanosheet (CNNS) colloids from liquid exfoliation of g-C3N4 in alkali solution that was denoted as le-CNNS, and employed the chemical blowing route to obtain the bottom-up CNNS marked as cb-CNNS. These two kinds of g-C3N4 were subsequently coupled to form a le-CNNS/cb-CNNS homojunction photocatalyst. This homojunction photocatalyst showed higher photocatalytic properties than those of the two individual CNNS materials, as well as the bulk g-C3N4. Furthermore, this composite possessed high stability because of the π–π interactions and the possible hydrogen-bonding interactions between le-CNNS and cb-CNNS.
The aforementioned attempts successfully fabricated g-C3N4/g-C3N4 composite photocatalysts, offering an alternative and smart strategy to solve the inherent defects of intrinsic g-C3N4 without depending on external semiconductors for achieving highly efficient photocatalytic HER. The g-C3N4/g-C3N4 hybrids seem to have great potential to be developed easily on a large scale because of the highly abundant precursors of g-C3N4 and relatively low cost and toxicity. Although all the reported g-C3N4/g-C3N4 photocatalysts only show a moderate photocatalytic activity (Table 4), further exploration of novel g-C3N4/g-C3N4 photocatalysts with considerably improved photocatalytic properties has very important practical value.


4.3.2. Composites of metal oxide semiconductors and g-C3N4 for HER application. 
The exploration of a semiconductor heterojunction by combining two different semiconductor photocatalysts with suitable valence band and conduction band potentials is considered to be one of the most effective strategies for accelerating charge transfer and separation by taking full consideration with the space charge exhaustion and accumulation between two semiconductors. Up to now, many researchers have reported various metal oxide semiconductor/g-C3N4 heterojunctions for photocatalytic HER application. It is reported that TiO2 is a promising candidate to form heterojunctions with g-C3N4. In general, integrating TiO2 with g-C3N4 is considered to be the most feasible strategy to expand its spectral scope to visible light as well as to restrain the recombination process, thus resulting in improved performance. Qu and co-workers286 reported a photocatalyst consisting of g-C3N4 and TiO2, which was prepared through a facile approach by calcining a mixture of melamine and TiO2 precursor. The prepared TiO2/g-C3N4 composite showed improved photocatalytic properties, and its H2 evolution rate reached 1119 μmol h−1 g−1, which was 5-fold enhancement when compared to bare TiO2 or pure g-C3N4. The improved photocatalytic performance was in virtue of the advantageous structure derived from the heat treatment and the synergic and promoted interaction. Although many efforts have been devoted to designing TiO2/g-C3N4 composites, the photocatalytic properties of TiO2/g-C3N4 are still severely restricted because of its sluggish charge transfer kinetics. It is a reliable fact that the efficiency of charge transfer across the interface is the crucial factor for restraining the charge recombination. Thus, tuning and engineering the interface properties is of great importance. The design and development of new mesoporous TiO2/g-C3N4 composites is of great importance to achieve better performance. Han and co-workers287 reported mesoporous TiO2@g-C3N4 hollow core@shell heterojunction photocatalysts constructed by in situ calcining and growing cyanamide on the surface of TiO2 and using SiO2 as the template for the first time (Fig. 17a). Such an in situ growth approach is considered to be able to increase the close contact interaction between TiO2 and g-C3N4 nanosheets, and shorten the diffusion distance of photogenerated charge carriers from the interface to the surface. TiO2 hollow nanospheres and g-C3N4 nanosheets were observed to be closely attached, and two crystal lattice fringes of 0.35 and 0.32 nm were clearly observed, which matched well with the (101) planes of TiO2 and (002) planes of g-C3N4.288 The specific surface area of the mesoporous TiO2@g-C3N4 was 71.65 m2 g−1, which was higher than those of the pure SiO2 nanospheres (22.38 m2 g−1), pure TiO2 hollow nanospheres (65.72 m2 g−1), and SiO2@TiO2 nanospheres (36.38 m2 g−1). Moreover, the prepared mesoporous TiO2@g-C3N4 showed improved photocatalytic properties, namely, the H2 evolution rate was up to 197.5 μmol h−1 g−1, which was 21.35- and 21.94-fold higher than those of pure g-C3N4 and pure TiO2 hollow nanospheres (Fig. 17b). The fast charge transfer took place on the TiO2/g-C3N4 interface, which was caused by large interfacial area, as photoluminescence, electrochemical impedance spectroscopy, and photoelectric current measurement results illustrated (Fig. 17c–e). It is worth emphasizing that the heterojunction interface between TiO2 and hollow g-C3N4 offers a much faster path for charge transfer due to its unique hollow nanostructure.
 Fig. 17  (a) Diagrammatic sketch for the fabrication, (b) photocatalytic H2 evolution rate, (c) photoluminescence spectrum, and (d) Nyquist plots and (e) transient photocurrent responses of the as-prepared samples. (All the data represent mean ± SD, n = 3/treatment.) Here, HT, SiO2@TiO2 nanospheres; HTCN, mesoporous TiO2@g-C3N4 hollow spheres; C–T, TiO2/g-C3N4 prepared by using the method reported by Jiang and co-workers.291 This figure has been reproduced from ref. 287 with permission from Elsevier. 
The size, shape, and nanostructures of TiO2 have huge impacts on the photocatalytic properties of TiO2/g-C3N4 photocatalysts. A series of TiO2 nanostructures, e.g., 0D nanoparticles, 1D nanowires, 2D nanosheets, and 3D mesoporous crystals, are easily decorated on the g-C3N4 surface as an anchoring site via a seed-induced solvothermal method.289 Xie and co-workers290 reported g-C3N4 compounded with hierarchical TiO2 yolk–shell spheres through a solvothermal approach blending the prepared g-C3N4 with the precursor of the TiO2 yolk–shell spheres in DMF. The incorporation of DMF ensured the TiO2 yolk–shell spheres to be uniformly distributed on the g-C3N4 surface, thus enhancing their specific surface area. The perfect TiO2/g-C3N4 interface would be propitious for the separation of photogenerated charges. TiO2 yolk–shell nanostructures with a relatively high specific surface area could offer abundant active reaction sites and increase their light absorption. In view of this, the prepared TiO2/g-C3N4 composites possessed improved photocatalytic properties when compared to pure TiO2 and g-C3N4. Notably, TiO2 loaded on the g-C3N4 surface plays two significant roles: (i) the improvement of charge separation derived from the TiO2/g-C3N4 heterojunction promotes the efficient separation of photogenerated electron–hole pairs, and (ii) TiO2 structures can provide abundant active reaction sites and improve light harvesting properties, which contributes to the efficient utilization of the light source and thus increases the quantity of photogenerated electron–hole pairs available to participate in the photocatalytic HER.
Apart from frequently reported TiO2, ZnO is another important metal oxide with a band gap of ca. 3.2 eV and is an appropriate alternative to TiO2, which can also be effectively coupled with g-C3N4.292–295 Zhu and co-workers295 reported a significant improvement in the photocurrent of the ZnO/g-C3N4 composites under visible-light irradiation (λ > 450 nm), resulting from the effective separation of the photogenerated charge carriers originated from the synergistic effect of g-C3N4 and ZnO. The optimum synergistic effect of the ZnO/g-C3N4 composites was observed at a weight ratio of 3%, corresponding to a monolayered dispersion of g-C3N4 on the ZnO surface. The improved visible-light photocatalytic properties were attributed to the heterojunction interfaces and the core–shell nanostructure governed by the full-matched lattice and energy level between the g-C3N4 shell and the ZnO core. The excited electrons on g-C3N4 could directly move into the conduction band of ZnO, endowing the ZnO/g-C3N4 composites with evidently improved photocatalytic properties.296 The N-doped ZnO showed a much stronger visible light absorption thus achieving an improvement in the photocatalytic performance in contrast to pure ZnO.297 Krishnan and co-workers298 prepared 2D N-doped ZnO/g-C3N4 nanosheet heterojunctions and explored their photocatalytic HER properties. The optimal photocatalytic HER performance was achieved with 30 wt% of g-C3N4 nanosheets (18836 μmol h−1 g−1). The ultrahigh HER yield was mainly because of the presence of 2D–2D interfacial contact. This face-to-face contact surface was beneficial for the effective separation of the photogenerated charge carriers in space, thus facilitating H2 production. To obtain monolayered g-C3N4 with weak van der Waals forces between the adjacent layers, Vattikuti's group299 established a synthetic procedure for the construction of ternary SnO2–ZnO/g-C3N4 heterostructured hybrids, in which SnO2–ZnO quantum dots were immobilized on g-C3N4 nanosheets via a facile in situ co-pyrolysis approach. In this architecture, SnO2/ZnO quantum dots were used as spacers between the g-C3N4 layers, forming a three-dimensional conductive network that facilitated fast electron and hole transport. In addition, the as-designed heterostructures also provided a porous structure that may inhibit the recombination of the photoinduced electrons and holes. The prepared SnO2–ZnO/g-C3N4 composites possessed a high H2 evolution rate (2734.72 μmol h−1 g−1), which was 1.06- and 2.27-fold higher than those of the binary ZnO/g-C3N4 composites and pristine g-C3N4 photocatalyst.
Compared with UV-active TiO2 and ZnO semiconductors, WO3 possesses a relatively lower band gap (2.6–2.8 eV), located in the visible-light region, and thus increases its potential for HER applications under visible light.300,301 In 2014, Chen's group302 reported the separation mechanisms of the photogenerated electrons and holes for the WO3/g-C3N4 composites. The WO3/g-C3N4 composites with different components of g-C3N4 or WO3 were prepared via a ball milling and heat treatment strategy. When the main component of the WO3/g-C3N4 composites was WO3, the photogenerated electrons and holes migrated in the common band–band mode. The photocatalytic activity was not significantly improved. But when the main component of the WO3/g-C3N4 composites was g-C3N4, the transport of the photogenerated electrons and holes showed a typical Z-scheme feature, and the photocatalytic properties of the WO3/g-C3N4 composites were increased greatly. A host–guest concept has been recently rationally employed via embedding the guest WO3 nanocuboids in the host g-C3N4 to achieve materials integration with an intimate interfacial contact.303 The more positive band edges and the higher electron mobility of metal oxides are expected to synchronously enable a rapid electron injection into the valence band of g-C3N4, and thus significantly delay the fast recombination kinetics and improve the photocatalytic properties. Inspired by this, Gu and co-workers304 designed a host–guest architecture through partial intercalation of the WO3 nanocuboids (guest) within g-C3N4 (host) featuring their simultaneous exposure to light illumination. The prepared WO3/g-C3N4 composites showed significantly improved photocatalytic properties, with a high H2 evolution rate (3120 μmol h−1 g−1), which was 7.1-fold higher than that of pure g-C3N4 (Fig. 18a). The well-defined WO3/g-C3N4 interfaces were clearly found via spherical aberration-corrected STEM, and both the in-plane and interlayer structures of g-C3N4 were directly imaged at atomic scale (Fig. 18b and c). The internal electric field and the covalent W–O–N–(C)2 interaction provided the driving force and the direct pathway, respectively, for achieving direct Z-scheme electron transfer from WO3 to g-C3N4 at the interface (Fig. 18d and e). Under simulated light excitation, this atomically defined junction led to a rapid electron injection from WO3 into g-C3N4 (Fig. 18f). Fig. 18g–j confirmed that the WO3/g-C3N4 composites possessed more excited electrons with extended lifetime that were available for heterogeneous photocatalytic HER. This Z-scheme host–guest architectural design enables more efficient utilization of the excited electrons in g-C3N4 with high reducing power for photocatalytic HER, which offers a new insight into the rational design of g-C3N4-based Z-scheme photocatalysts for more efficient solar energy utilization. Katsumata and co-workers305 prepared WO3/g-C3N4 composites through a simple calcination approach. The highest photocatalytic activity was achieved for 10 wt% WO3/g-C3N4 composites at H2 evolution rate of 110 μmol h−1 g−1, over 2-fold that of pure g-C3N4. The improved photocatalytic property could be ascribed to the Z-scheme mechanism, resulting in the efficient charge separation.
 Fig. 18  (a) Comparison of average photocatalytic H2 evolution rates of the WO3/g-C3N4 composites and pure samples loaded with 1 wt% Pt co-catalyst for the first three hours under simulated solar irradiation.
