Spherical aberration-corrected STEM images of the well-defined WO3/g-C3N4 interface in the WO3/g-C3N4 composites, revealing both the (b) in-plane and (c) interlayer structure of g-C3N4 (scale bars, 2 nm). (d) Diagrammatic sketch of the atomically defined junction at the WO3/g-C3N4 interface and the internal electric field from g-C3N4 (+) to WO3 (−). (e) Energy band diagram between WO3 and g-C3N4 with inverse band bending and internal electric field at interface. (f) Schematic illustration of the function mechanism of WO3/g-C3N4 composites at multiple length scales. (g) Photoluminescence spectrum of pure g-C3N4, WO3/g-C3N4 and pristine WO3 (inset). (h) Time-resolved fluorescence decay profiles of pure g-C3N4 and WO3/g-C3N4 excited at the wavelength of 320 nm; (i) transient photocurrent response and (j) electrochemical impedance spectra of pure g-C3N4 and WO3/g-C3N4 in 0.5 M Na2SO4 aqueous solution. This figure has been reproduced from ref. 304 with permission from Elsevier. 
Besides that, Cu2O nanoparticles modified g-C3N4 photocatalysts have also recently been studied for HER application. Cu2O show a relatively lower direct band gap of 2.0 eV, increasing its potential for HER applications under visible light. The g-C3N4 with a thickness of ca. 2.5 nm was successfully decorated onto the surface of Cu2O nanospheres with a uniform diameter (ca. 200 nm). The prepared g-C3N4-coated Cu2O nanospheres possessed significantly improved photocatalytic activity for HER application in contrast to that of pure Cu2O and g-C3N4, respectively, reaching an the optimal H2 evolution rate (202.29 μmol h−1 g−1) at a g-C3N4 content of 5 wt%.306 Guo and co-workers307 reported Cu2O nanoparticles were uniformly coated on g-C3N4 surface through a one-pot in situ reduction method. The Cu2O nanoparticles with a narrow size range (3–7 nm) were well-decorated on g-C3N4 surface. With the introduction of Cu2O into g-C3N4, an electric field was formed. The conduction band of Cu2O was 1.11 eV, which was higher than g-C3N4, and ensuring a strong driving force for the electrons to be readily transformed from Cu2O to g-C3N4. In consequence, the well-matched energy levels promoted the formation of p–n heterojunction for boosting charge migration, and the photocatalytic activity was further improved. The Cu2O octahedra formed fully with (111) facets were more efficient for photocatalysis reactions than the nanospheres due to the (111) facets of Cu2O contained surface copper atoms with dangling bonds.308 Cui and co-workers309 prepared core@shell structured Cu2O@g-C3N4 octahedra photocatalyst through a combined solvothermal and chemisorption strategy. The prepared Cu2O@g-C3N4 composite possessed a high H2 evolution rate (365 μmol h−1 g−1), more than 4-fold that of the pure Cu2O crystals. The enhanced photocatalytic property could be ascribed to the effective separation and fast migration of the photogenerated charges resulted from exposed Cu2O facets.
Besides the above metal oxides, earth-abundant CoO was also used to develop g-C3N4 composites for the improved photocatalytic property. Mao and co-workers310 designed a novel g-C3N4/CoO nanocomposite by a simple thermal-annealing approach under Ar atmosphere. The prepared g-C3N4/CoO composite showed more efficient visible light absorption than pure g-C3N4, leading to a better photocatalytic property for HER. The optimum H2 evolution rate under visible-light irradiation for the g-C3N4/CoO composite was as high as 651.3 μmol h−1 g−1, which was 3-fold higher than that of pure g-C3N4. This enhancement was the result of the synergistic effect of the g-C3N4/CoO junction or interface. Liu and co-workers311 prepared CoO/g-C3N4 composites through a one-step thermal decomposition route. Interestingly, the optimal photocatalytic activity was obtained with a Co content of 10%, showing a highest H2 evolution rate of 0.46 μmol h−1 and O2 evolution rate of 0.21 μmol h−1 under visible-light illumination without adding any sacrificial reagents, which was very close to a stoichiometric ratio of 2:1. The eminent photocatalytic property of the CoO/g-C3N4 composites was mainly because of the efficient charge separation and fast decomposition.
Much attention has been paid to the crystal-plane-dependent interactions in CeO2-based photocatalysts for photocatalytic HER. Wan and co-workers312 prepared CeO2/g-C3N4 composites with tunable CeO2 crystal planes (110), (100), and (111). The CeO2(110)/g-C3N4 composites possessed much stronger interaction at interface, causing faster separation of electron–hole pairs, more Ce3+ species, and better photocatalytic property for H2 evolution. Kang and co-workers313 designed amorphous NiO/g-C3N4 as a photocatalyst, which can achieve efficient visible-light-governed overall H2 production without any sacrificial agent. The obtained NiO/g-C3N4 photocatalyst displayed the optimal photocatalytic HER property, with a H2 evolution rate of 1.41 μmol h−1 and O2 evolution rate of 0.71 μmol h−1, the stoichiometric ratio is very close to 2:1. The visible-light-guided photocatalytic property of the NiO/g-C3N4 originated from the amorphous NiO as the co-catalyst, being able to promote the photoinduced electron–hole pair separation, offer active sites and help to decompose the generated H2O2. Searching new types of 2D/2D Z-scheme heterojunctions to further enhance photocatalytic property is still a challenging task. Yu and co-workers40 reported 2D/2D g-C3N4/MnO2 composites prepared through in situ growing MnO2 nanosheets on exfoliated g-C3N4 nanosheets surface using a simple solution deposition approach. The prepared g-C3N4/MnO2 photocatalysts showed greatly improved photocatalytic property in contrast to that of the individual g-C3N4 or MnO2 component. This enhancement was due to the photoinduced electrons in MnO2 combined efficiently with the holes in g-C3N4.


4.3.3. Composites of metal sulfide semiconductor and g-C3N4 for HER application. 
Recently, the exploitation of g-C3N4 hybridized with a low band gap metal sulfide semiconductor photocatalyst has attracted more attention.314 CdS possesses a band gap of 2.4 eV, ensuring the absorption of visible light up to 520 nm. The highly matched energy levels of g-C3N4 and CdS, being beneficial for the construction of heterostructures, will ease the charge migration transfer upon light irradiation.315 Yu and co-workers316 synthesized a heterostructured g-C3N4/CdS photocatalyst with homogeneous dispersion and strong coupling interface through a simple in situ high-temperature self-transformation strategy employing melamine–CdS composite as the precursor. The g-C3N4 nanoparticles (5–30 nm) were grafted on CdS surface with densely contacted interfaces, which led to an enhanced photocatalytic activity. When the amount of g-C3N4 was 1 wt%, the obtained g-C3N4/CdS exhibited the highest H2 evolution rate (5303 mol h−1 g−1), which was 2.5-fold higher than that of pure CdS. In the g-C3N4/CdS composites, the g-C3N4 worked as an effective hole-migration cocatalyst to boost the fast migration of the photogenerated holes from the CdS surface, resulting in the highly efficient separation of the photogenerated charges in CdS. Two major issues need to be solved in CdS/g-C3N4 hybrids. CdS seriously suffers from photocorrosion that S2− in CdS tends to be self-oxidized under strong visible light radiation and their corresponding speedy photoexcited charge carriers tend to be recombined. In order to overcome these issues, protecting CdS from low efficiency and photocorrosion via coating g-C3N4 to form core/shell or yolk/shell nanostructures has proven to be a good strategy. Zhang et al.181 prepared CdS/g-C3N4 core/shell nanowires by a combined solvothermal and chemisorption strategy (Fig. 19a). g-C3N4 was easily to be exfoliated into thinner nanosheets via a simple ultrasonic method and then self-assembly-coated on CdS nanowires to form a core/shell nanostructure with intimate contact. The exfoliated g-C3N4 nanosheets were successfully decorated on the lateral surface of CdS nanowires to achieve a minimum surface energy, and the core/shell structures can be also clearly observed. The specific surface area of the obtained CdS/g-C3N4 sample was up to 13.6 m2 g−1 (Fig. 19b). The optimal photocatalytic property of the CdS/g-C3N4 sample was achieved at 2 wt% g-C3N4 content, and the H2 evolution rate was up to 4152 μmol h−1 g−1 with an apparent quantum efficiency of 4.3% at 420 nm (Fig. 19c). After running 4 times, there was no obvious loss observed in the H2 evolution rate (Fig. 19d). The eminent photocatalytic activity and stability was probably because of the synergic effect between g-C3N4 and CdS originated from their highly matched overlapping band structures, which can effectively boost the charge separation and migration corrosive holes from CdS to g-C3N4 (Fig. 19e–g). This work provides a new solution for enhancing the photocatalytic property of CdS/g-C3N4 hybrids.
 Fig. 19  (a) Diagrammatic sketch of the formation process (b) TEM and (c) HRTEM images of CdS/g-C3N4 core/shell structure. (d) Nitrogen adsorption–desorption isotherms and the corresponding pore size distributions (the inset) calculated by the BJH method for pristine CdS nanowires and different CdS/g-C3N4 samples. (e) Comparison of photocatalytic H2 evolution rate of pristine CdS nanowires and different CdS/g-C3N4 samples from mixed 0.35 M Na2S and 0.25 M Na2SO3 aqueous solutions. (f) Time course of photocatalytic H2 production for CdS/g-C3N4 sample. Diagrammatic sketch of (g) the energy-level configuration and the photogenerated charge migration process between g-C3N4 and CdS, (h) the photocatalytic process on a CdS nanowire with a thin and unclosed g-C3N4 shell, and (i) a CdS nanowire with a dense and closed g-C3N4 shell. This figure has been reproduced from ref. 181 with permission from American Chemical Society. 
CdS quantum dots can also be introduced onto g-C3N4 surface with different morphologies for HER application. In an early study, Feng and co-workers317 hybridized g-C3N4 nanosheets with CdS quantum dots with diameters of 2–5 nm by a chemical impregnation strategy. After the introduction of CdS quantum dots, the obtained CdS quantum dots/g-C3N4 showed a shift of the absorption edge of g-C3N4 to higher wavelengths in visible-light region because of the chemical interaction between g-C3N4 and CdS quantum dots. The optimal H2 evolution rate of the obtained CdS quantum dots/g-C3N4 reached 172.7 μmol h−1 g−1, which was approximately 9 times than pure g-C3N4. Notably, severe agglomeration of the CdS nanoparticles (>100 nm) was found without the g-C3N4 nanosheets as the supports. Well distributed small CdS quantum dots (∼5 nm) on g-C3N4 nanosheets can be obtained via a solvothermal approach. The obtained CdS/g-C3N4 nanocomposites showed evidently improved photocatalytic property with the H2 evolution rate of 4494 μmol h−1 g−1, which was 115-fold higher than that of pure g-C3N4. The enhancement was mainly because of the interfacial migration of the photogenerated electrons and holes, resulting in effective charge separation on the both components.318
Apart from the CdS modification, NiS/g-C3N4 composites have also received a wide attention. Depositing NiS nanoparticles on the g-C3N4 nanosheets via a hydrothermal treatment by employing Ni(CH3COO)2 and CH3CSNH2 as the raw materials, the absorption edge of g-C3N4 did not occur red shift, confirming that all elements (i.e., Ni and S) was not doped into the N pots of g-C3N4 frameworks. Thus, NiS can serve as an active cocatalyst site for the storage of photogenerated electrons from the g-C3N4. This NiS/g-C3N4 photocatalyst exhibited high H2 evolution rate (482 μmol h−1 g−1), exceeding 241-fold compared to that of pure g-C3N4.319 A similar hydrothermal treatment process was also performed for the construction of NiS/g-C3N4 composites by Yang's group.320 The ultrathin g-C3N4 nanosheets (∼ 3.4 nm) were prepared via a convenient liquid exfoliation approach and adopted as 2D matrixes for the decoration of NiS nanoparticles. In the NiS/g-C3N4 composites, the ultrathin g-C3N4 nanosheets served as a visible-light-responsive semiconductor, and the NiS nanoparticles acted as a noble-metal-free cocatalyst. The obtained NiS/g-C3N4 composites showed an improved photocatalytic performance for HER compared to g-C3N4 and exfoliated g-C3N4 nanosheets. Recently, excellent photocatalytic activities have been frequently reported when employing NiS/g-C3N4 composites as a photocatalyst for HER application. The H2 evolution rate of the NiS/g-C3N4 composites was reported to be as high as 992 μmol h−1 g−1 by Li's group,321 and 3429 μmol h−1 g−1 by M. Mohamed's group,322 respectively. The NiS/g-C3N4 composite with the highest H2 evolution rate was reported by Dong and co-workers,323 which reached 16400 μmol h−1 g−1, ca. 2500-fold higher than that of pure g-C3N4. For all we know, it is the highest H2 evolution rate of all g-C3N4-based photocatalysts reported so far. Moreover, the photocatalytic activity had no obvious loss after 40 h. In addition, the H2 evolution rate of the NiS/g-C3N4 composite reached 28300 μmol g−1 during 7 h under solar radiation. This unprecedented enhancement could be in virtue of the effective separation of the photogenerated electron–hole pairs.
2D MoS2 has recently become an ideal cocatalyst because of its low cost, robustness, and high activity.324,325 MoS2 has a similar structure to graphite, and it possesses a layered crystal structure consisting of S–Mo–S “sandwiches” hung together via van der Waals force. The fact that g-C3N4 and MoS2 possess analogous layered structures should minimize the lattice mismatch and boost the planar growth of MoS2 slabs along with the g-C3N4 surface.326 Wang and co-workers327 reported the fabrication of earth-abundant 2D/2D layered MoS2/g-C3N4 heterojunctions through an impregnation–sulfidation approach, which can raise the accessible area around the planar interface of the MoS2 and g-C3N4 layers and reduce the barriers for electron migration through the co-catalyst, thus further facilitate fast electron transfer across the interface by the electron tunneling effect. Moreover, the intrinsic band structures of g-C3N4 and MoS2 provided the possibility of the directional migration of the photogenerated electrons from g-C3N4 to MoS2. Thus, the photocatalytic HER property of g-C3N4 under visible-light was considerably enhanced via growing thin-layered MoS2 on the g-C3N4 surface. In addition, an in-depth understanding of the interface interaction and charge migration of MoS2/g-C3N4 is also very important. Li and co-workers328 extensively studied the MoS2/g-C3N4 interface region to explore the fundamental mechanism of photocatalytic improvement. The conduction band minimum and valence band maximum of the g-C3N4 monolayer were ca. 0.83 eV and 0.15 eV, respectively, which were higher than those of MoS2 nanosheets, resulting in the construction of a Type II heterojunction system. The predicted Type-II heterojunction structure ensured that the photogenerated electrons easily migrate from the monolayer g-C3N4 to the MoS2 nanosheet. The charge migration between MoS2 and g-C3N4 caused a polarized field within the MoS2/g-C3N4 interface region, which would be beneficial to the separation of photogenerated carriers. Moreover, the calculated optical absorption curves confirmed that the MoS2/g-C3N4 composites were also an excellent light-harvesting semiconductor. Thus, the MoS2/g-C3N4 composites exhibited improved photocatalytic properties. Han and co-workers329 prepared MoS2/g-C3N4 heterojunction photocatalysts via a facile in situ ion exchange strategy. Well-distributed MoS2 quantum dots with steerable size and a high loading were grown on the g-C3N4 nanosheet surface. MoS2 improved its redox activity in photocatalytic HER, and the compact heterojunction facilitated the separation of photoinduced charge carriers, which led to highly efficient photocatalytic H2 production for the prepared MoS2/g-C3N4 composites. The H2 evolution rate of the MoS2/g-C3N4 composite was up to 142 μmol h−1 g−1, which was approximately 7.4 and 9.5 times higher than those of single MoS2 and g-C3N4.
Although the combined photocatalytic mechanism for MoS2/g-C3N4 outperformed the simple individual mechanisms of MoS2 and g-C3N4, it is hard to recycle and classify a multicomponent compound from a solution. Thus, a method for the effective recycling of coupled MoS2/g-C3N4 photocatalysts is highly worth considering. Magnetic iron oxide, especially Fe3O4 and Fe2O3, have been widely used as promising oxide supports for constructing magnetically recoverable g-C3N4-based composite photocatalysts owing to their easy preparation, abundant active surface, and magnetic separability nature.330 Lu and co-workers331 synthesized a magnetically recoverable MoS2/g-C3N4 photocatalyst coupled with Fe3O4@SiO2 microspheres via an efficient and adoptable method (Fig. 20a). The microspheres were composed of a Fe3O4@SiO2 and a MoS2/g-C3N4 heterostructure, and the internal planar distances corresponding to the (002) plane of g-C3N4 and the (113) plane of MoS2 were ∼0.323 nm and ∼0.62 nm, respectively (Fig. 20b and c). The prepared Fe3O4@SiO2@MoS2/g-C3N4 composites showed an extended light response range, intimate contact interface, improved separation speed of carriers, and higher photocurrent density (Fig. 20d and e). Specifically, a H2 evolution rate of 110.72 μmol h−1 g−1 was measured with Fe3O4@SiO2@MoS2/g-C3N4 under visible-light, which was approximately 11.1- and 4.1-fold higher than those of pure g-C3N4 and Fe3O4@SiO2@MoS2. This is mainly because of the unique transfer system of the MoS2/g-C3N4 Z-scheme photocatalysts with photogenerated electrons migrating into the valence band of g-C3N4 again from the conduction band of MoS2. In addition, it also shows good recoverability and photoactivity as a promising multifunctional photocatalyst (Fig. 20f).
 Fig. 20  (a) Diagrammatic sketch, (b) TEM image and (c) HRTEM image of Fe3O4@SiO2@MoS2/g-C3N4 microspheres. (d) Nyquist impedance spectroscopy of curves, plots i–iv: Fe3O4, Fe3O4@SiO2, MoS2, g-C3N4, Fe3O4@SiO2@MoS2, and Fe3O4@SiO2@MoS2/g-C3N4, respectively. (e) Surface photocurrent curves: (i) Fe3O4, (ii) Fe3O4@SiO2, (iii) Fe3O4@SiO2@MoS2, and (iv) Fe3O4@SiO2@MoS2/g-C3N4. (f) Stable H2 generation from water under visible-light irradiation using (i) MoS2, (ii) g-C3N4, (iii) Fe3O4@SiO2@MoS2, (iv) MoS2/g-C3N4, and (v) Fe3O4@SiO2@MoS2/g-C3N4. This figure has been reproduced from ref. 331 with permission from the American Chemical Society. 
Other metal sulfide semiconductors have also been employed to modify g-C3N4. For example, Hou and co-workers332 prepared 2D/2D WS2/g-C3N4 heterojunctions via loading thin-layered WS2 on the g-C3N4 surface to form intact junctions to improve the electron tunneling effect. The prepared WS2/g-C3N4 displayed improved photocatalytic properties, and the H2 evolution rate reached the optimum when the loading amount of WS2 was about 0.3 at%. The improvement was mainly ascribed to the thin-layered WS2/g-C3N4 junctions. Zhang's group333 reported an ultrasonic dispersion strategy to prepare 2D/2D Sn2S2/g-C3N4 heterojunction photocatalysts via coupling g-C3N4 nanosheets with the ultrathin hexagonal SnS2 (4–8 nm) nanosheets. The aggregation of SnS2 nanosheets could readily be found because of the high surface energy. With the aid of ultrasonication, highly-dispersed SnS2 nanosheets were decorated horizontally on the g-C3N4 surface. The prepared SnS2/g-C3N4 showed improved photocatalytic properties and reached the highest H2 evolution rate when the loading amount of SnS2 was about 5 wt%. Zou and co-workers334 reported an integrated design and synthesis of ZnS/g-C3N4 heterostructure with affluent zinc vacancy defects on the ZnS surface to emphasize the synergistic promotion on charge separation. The prepared ZnS/g-C3N4 heterojunction displayed a high photocatalytic H2 evolution rate (713.68 μmol h−1 g−1), over 30-fold higher than that of pure g-C3N4. This improved photocatalytic property could be because of the ZnS/g-C3N4 intimate interfacial contact, which increased the light-absorbing capacity and charge separation efficiency of the ZnS/g-C3N4 heterojunction.


4.3.4. Composites of metal phosphide semiconductors and g-C3N4 for HER application. 
Nickel phosphide semiconductors consisting of inexpensive and earth-abundant elements have received growing interest due to their unique optical and electrical properties, which endow them with great potential in HER application.335 Very recently, Ni2P nanocrystals composited as co-catalysts with g-C3N4 semiconductor photocatalysts for H2 production have attracted increasing attention. The first example of Ni2P/g-C3N4 composites for HER application was reported by Driess and co-cworkers.336 In the Ni2P/g-C3N4 composites, the Ni2P acted as an eminent co-catalyst for highly efficient photocatalytic HER under visible-light. This integrated system showed a higher catalytic property than the physical mixture of Ni2P and g-C3N4 or Ni2P on g-C3N4 under identical conditions. An and co-workers337 prepared a Ni2P/g-C3N4 lamellar composite photocatalyst through a one-pot hydrothermal method by in situ anchoring Ni2P nanocrystals onto the exfoliated g-C3N4 nanosheets using red phosphorus as a “green”, cheap and easy-to-handle precursor. The obtained Ni2P/g-C3N4 composite displayed an improved photocatalytic property for HER with a H2 evolution rate of 362.4 μmol h−1 g−1, which was ca. 22-fold higher than that of pure g-C3N4. A similar work was also reported by Lin and co-workers.338 Ni2P was loaded onto g-C3N4via a facile annealing of a mixture of NiCl2, NaH2PO2 and g-C3N4 in an Ar atmosphere. The charge migration from the photo-excited g-C3N4 to Ni2P was enhanced. As a result, the H2 evolution rate over the Ni2P/g-C3N4 was up to 82.5 μmol h−1 g−1, which was higher than that of the Pt-loaded g-C3N4. The improvement was mainly attributed to efficient charge migration from the photo-excited g-C3N4 to Ni2P, which restrained the recombination of electron–hole pairs in g-C3N4. Chen's group339 used a solution-phase approach to prepare highly monodisperse 0D Ni2P nanoparticles, which were then loaded on 2D porous g-C3N4 nanosheets through a facile self-assembly strategy to fabricate the intimate 0D–2D heterojunction interface. When the Ni2P amount was 3.5 wt%, the Ni2P/g-C3N4 composite exhibited a high H2 evolution rate of 474.7 μmol h−1 g−1 and an apparent quantum yield of 3.2% at 435 nm, which surpassed most of the reported noble-metal-free co-catalyst-modified g-C3N4 photocatalysts. The outstanding photocatalytic performance was ascribed to the important role of Ni2P as an excellent reduction co-catalyst to restrain the recombination of charge carriers from g-C3N4 and offer good electrical conductivity as well.
Ni12P5 nanoparticles as non-precious earth-abundant photocatalysts have been extensively applied as promising cocatalysts for significantly accelerating the photocatalytic H2 production over g-C3N4, which can effectively enhance their photocatalytic performances.340 Li and co-workers341 prepared Ni12P5 modified g-C3N4 nanosheet (Ni12P5/g-C3N4) composites via a simple grinding method. Ni12P5 nanoparticles were uniformly decorated on the g-C3N4 nanosheet surface. The maximum photocatalytic H2 evolution rate of Ni12P5/g-C3N4 composites was up to 126.61 μmol h−1 g−1, exceeding 269.4-fold that of pure g-C3N4. The Ni12P5 nanoparticles decorated on the g-C3N4 surface could serve as important active sites to promote separation of the photoexcited electrons and holes, and thus boost the H2 generation. Han and co-workers342 synthesized Ni12P5 colloidal nanoparticles, and coupled them with 2D porous g-C3N4 nanosheets via a facile solution-phase self-assembly strategy under continuous ultrasonication. The Ni12P5 nanoparticles with high purity and good crystallinity were uniformly embedded into the porous g-C3N4 nanosheets, thus boosting the separation and migration of the photogenerated charge carriers. The obtained Ni12P5/g-C3N4 composite displayed an excellent H2 evolution activity of 535.7 μmol h−1 g−1, which was higher than that previously reported for the Ni12P5/g-C3N4 composites prepared by a mechanical blending approach.341 Among all transition metal phosphides, nickel phosphides, e.g., Ni3P, Ni12P5, and Ni2P, have received growing interest for constructing NixPy/g-C3N4 composite photocatalysts due to their facile fabrication approaches and various crystal structures; thus, a detailed discussion about the influence of the different phases of NixPy on photocatalytic performance is of great importance. Majima and co-workers343 prepared three different phases of NixPy (Ni2P, Ni12P5, and Ni3P) as cocatalysts to be hybridized with g-C3N4. The NixPy nanoparticles with an average particle size of ca. 150–250 nm were uniformly loaded on the g-C3N4 surface (Fig. 21a–f). Commonly, there are three important factors in favor of enhancement of photocatalytic activity, including improved light absorption, effective charge carrier separation, and fast charge migration to the interface, all of them can be realized by the introduction of NixPy to g-C3N4 (Fig. 21g–i). Consequently, the prepared NixPy/g-C3N4 composites, especially Ni2P/g-C3N4 composites, showed excellent photocatalytic activity and stability, and the highest HER activity was achieved by Ni2P/g-C3N4 (168 μmol h−1 g−1) (Fig. 21j and k). The introduction of NixPy accelerated the migration and separation of the photoexcited electrons of g-C3N4, leading to an enhanced photocatalytic property in HER (Fig. 21l). In the NixPy system, Ni and P have a partial positive charge (δ+) and a partial negative charge (δ−), respectively, which implies that the electron density of P is higher than that of Ni.344 The P sites in NixPy play an important role as the H+ acceptor and the delivery site for H, which is beneficial for the subsequent H2 production. Among these three different NixPy compounds, Ni2P possesses the highest ratio of P atoms in contrast to Ni12P5 and Ni3P, indicating that Ni2P could provide more H+ acceptor centers and active sites,345 and thus the Ni2P/g-C3N4 composites have shown the highest photocatalytic activity.
 Fig. 21  TEM (a, c and e) and HRTEM (b, d and f) images of Ni2P/g-C3N4, Ni12P5/g-C3N4, and Ni3P/g-C3N4; UV-vis diffuse reflectance spectra (g) and photoluminescence (h) of g-C3N4 and NixPy/g-C3N4 (5 wt% NixPy); time profiles of normalized transient absorption for g-C3N4 and NixPy/g-C3N4 (5 wt% NixPy) with 420 nm laser irradiation (i); photocatalytic H2 generation rates for g-C3N4, Pt/g-C3N4, and NixPy/g-C3N4 (5 wt% NixPy) (j); recycle of H2 generation on Ni2P/g-C3N4 (5 wt% NixPy) (k) and schematic diagram of photocatalytic H2 generation for the NixPy/g-C3N4 hybrid (l). This figure has been reproduced from ref. 343 with permission from the American Chemical Society. 
In addition to NixPy, other earth-abundant metal phosphides have also been utilized to prepare g-C3N4-based photocatalysts. Li and co-workers346 prepared CoP/g-C3N4 hybrids via in situ 2D/2D assembly between ultrathin CoP and g-C3N4 nanosheets. The design of a 2D/2D CoP/g-C3N4 interface enlarged the Schottky effect and shortened the transmission distance of the photo-generated charges. As a result, a remarkable improvement of the photocatalytic activity and stability could be achieved. Xu and co-workers347 prepared a novel CoP/g-C3N4 photocatalyst by low-cost precursors. It was found that the unique P(δ−)–Co(δ+)–N(δ−) surface bonding states in the as-prepared photocatalyst resulted in superior photocatalytic H2 generation activity (1924 μmol h−1 g−1) to the noble metal Pt-decorated g-C3N4 photocatalyst (646 μmol h−1 g−1). The quantum efficiency of 12.4% at 420 nm was also higher than that reported for the other transition metal cocatalyst-loaded g-C3N4 photocatalysts. Xie and co-workers348 prepared NiCoP/g-C3N4 composites via a facile one-pot approach. NiCoP nanoparticles (∼10 nm) were well coated on the g-C3N4 surface, forming inorganic/polymer junctions. Importantly, NiCoP/g-C3N4 composites exhibited an ultrahigh H2 evolution rate of 1643 μmol h−1 g−1, which was about 21-fold higher than that of pure g-C3N4. The outstanding photocatalytic property was probably because of the combined effects of the enhanced separation efficiency and effective charge transfer efficiency.


4.3.5. Composites of complex compound semiconductors and g-C3N4 for HER application. 
As we know, a complex compound consists of at least 3 elements. The incorporation of a complex compound into light-absorbing semiconductor photocatalysts is believed to be a promising technique for improving photoredox reactions, especially for the HER because of the efficient charge separation.349–351 Generally speaking, the complex compounds are easily decorated on the g-C3N4 surface by physical and chemical adsorption. The transfer of electrons is viable from the photoexcited semiconductors to the molecular cocatalysts to delay the recombination of photogenerated electron–hole pairs.
An Aurivillius-based semiconductor photocatalyst has attracted much interest as an ideal candidate for the engineering of g-C3N4-based photocatalysts.352,353 The g-C3N4/Bi2WO6 and g-C3N4/Bi2MoO6 heterojunctions, following a typical heterojunction charge transfer mechanism, have shown relatively weak potential for H2 generation. DFT calculation indicated that the charge migration at the interface of the heterostructure resulted in a built-in potential, which benefited the separation of photogenerated charge carriers. The g-C3N4/Bi2WO6(010) heterostructure with more negative adhesion energy (−1.10 eV Å−2) was predicted to have a better adsorptive ability and could form more easily when compared to the g-C3N4/Bi2MoO6(010) interface (−1.16 eV Å−2). Therefore, the g-C3N4 interaction with Bi2MoO6 was stronger than that with Bi2WO6, which resulted in a better photocatalytic performance.354 Inspired by this, Fan and co-workers355 prepared g-C3N4/Bi2MoO6 heterojunctions via an in situ solvothermal method. The exfoliated g-C3N4 nanosheets provided large specific surface area and shortened the diffusion distance of photogenerated charges, enhancing the photocatalytic property as well as profiting the sufficient interaction with Bi2MoO6. Moreover, the intimate contact of Bi2MoO6 and g-C3N4 nanosheets facilitated the migration and separation of the photogenerated electron–hole pairs. Remarkably, 20%-g-C3N4/Bi2MoO6 composite exhibited the optimal photocatalytic property with a H2 evolution rate of 563.4 μmol h−1 g−1, which was ca. 3.84- and 32.19-fold higher than those of g-C3N4 and Bi2MoO6. Shi and co-workers356 prepared g-C3N4/InVO4 heterojunction-type photocatalysts through in situ growth of InVO4 nanoparticles onto the g-C3N4 nanosheet surface by a simple hydrothermal route. The InVO4 nanoparticles (∼20 nm) coated uniformly on the g-C3N4 nanosheet surface. The intimate interface boosted the charge migration and restrained the recombination rate of the photogenerated electron–hole pairs, which effectively enhanced the photocatalytic property. The prepared g-C3N4/InVO4 photocatalyst exhibited excellent photocatalytic property, namely, its H2 evolution rate was up to 212 μmol h−1 g−1 and apparent quantum efficiency was as high as 4.9% at 420 nm.
BiOX (X = Cl, Br and I) possesses a layered structure and indirect transition band gaps which can provide an enormous region to polarize the atoms and orbitals, and the internal static electric fields are perpendicular to the [Bi2O2] slabs and halogen anionic slabs in the BiOX structure.357,358 By now several significant studies on the construction of BiOX/g-C3N4 for HER application have been presented. Among these BiOX compounds, BiOBr with a moderate band gap (∼2.75 eV) shows strong visible-light response and high chemical stability. Zan and co-workers359 synthesized BiOBr–g-C3N4 composite photocatalysts via a one-step chemical bath approach at low temperature. The obtained BiOBr–g-C3N4 composites exhibited improved photocatalytic performance compared to pure g-C3N4 and BiOBr. The interaction between BiOBr and g-C3N4 was embodied by the facets coupling between BiOBr-(001) and g-C3N4-(002), and the photoinduced charge migration between these two facets led to efficient charge separation. BiOCl is a white colored semiconductor with a band gap energy of ∼3.4 eV and a high recombination rate of photo-generated carriers. Therefore, it is necessary for BiOCl to find a suitable narrow-band-gap semiconductor as a photosensitizer. Shen and co-workers360 reported novel g-C3N4/BiOCl composite photocatalysts with controllable mass ratios of g-C3N4 to BiOCl, which were fabricated via direct hydrolysis of NaBiO3 and g-C3N4, through which BiOCl was loaded onto g-C3N4. The prepared g-C3N4/BiOCl photocatalysts displayed higher photocatalytic properties than pure BiOCl and g-C3N4. The enhancement was in virtue of the effective separation of electron–hole pairs in the g-C3N4/BiOCl interface.361 BiOI is a fascinating p-type semiconductor with the smallest band gap (1.7–1.8 eV), showing the highest absorption in the visible-light region compared to BiOBr and BiOCl.362 Yao and co-workers363 prepared a series of Z-scheme BiOI/g-C3N4 photocatalysts via a simple deposition approach. The optimal 7.4-BiOI/g-C3N4 composite was confirmed to be an ideal photocatalyst for H2 production and showed better photocatalytic properties than pure BiOI and g-C3N4 under visible-light (λ > 400 nm). The improved photocatalytic activity was ascribed to the efficient separation of the photogenerated charge carriers.
CoTiO3, a typical ABO3 perovskite oxide, is a semiconductor with a narrow band gap (Eg = 2.25 eV), appropriate electronic band structure, large absorption coefficient, high carrier mobility, and high stability. Moreover, CoTiO3 possesses appropriate band edges (ECB = 0.1 eV, EVB = 2.35 eV), which match well with g-C3N4 (ECB = −1.3 eV, EVB = 1.4 eV) to form a direct Z-scheme photocatalytic system.364 A direct Z-scheme photocatalyst consisting of 1D CoTiO3 rods and 2D g-C3N4 nanosheets was successfully prepared via calcining the mixture of preformed CoTiO3 and urea (Fig. 22a). g-C3N4 was uniformly decorated on the CoTiO3 rod surface, forming an intimate two-phase contacting layer, which was beneficial to rapid electron transfer (Fig. 22b). Moreover, the crystal lattice spacing (ca. 0.27 nm) along with the interface was well-matched with the (104) facet of the high-crystalline CoTiO3, indicating that intimate junctions were obtained between the (104) plane of CoTiO3 and g-C3N4 (Fig. 22c). The molecular interaction between perovskite CoTiO3 and tri-s-triazine g-C3N4 was confirmed to be some chemical bonds, e.g., Co–O–N or Ti–O–N (Fig. 22d). The 0.15% CoTiO3/g-C3N4 composite exhibited the optimal photocatalytic property with a H2 evolution rate of ca. 858 μmol h−1 g−1 and quantum efficiency of 38.4% at 365 nm, and 3.23% at 420 ± 20 nm (Fig. 22e). These enhancements were mainly because of the synergistic effect of CoTiO3 and g-C3N4 related to the fabrication of heterojunctions on the solid–solid contact interface. It could be explained very well by Z-scheme mechanisms. The electrons generated from the conduction band of CoTiO3 could transfer to the valence band of g-C3N4 through a solid–solid heterointerface, subsequently recombine with the local holes, consequently boosting the separation of the photogenerated electrons from the conduction band of g-C3N4. Meanwhile, the photogenerated holes on the CoTiO3 surface could be rapidly shifted and consumed by sacrificial ethanol, which would boost the charge separation and thus enhance photocatalytic properties (Fig. 22f).365 This study focuses mainly on the direct solid-state Z-scheme CoTiO3/g-C3N4, which offers very useful guidance role for further development of novel heterojunction perovskite/g-C3N4 photocatalytic systems.
 Fig. 22  (a) Diagrammatic sketch for the preparation of CoTiO3/g-C3N4 composites. (b) SEM and (c) HRTEM images of the CoTiO3/g-C3N4 composites. (d) Schematic illustration of molecular interaction between perovskite CoTiO3 and tri-s-triazine g-C3N4via the formation of chemical bonds. (e) Photocatalytic activity for H2 evolution with different ratios of CoTiO3/g-C3N4 photocatalysts. (f) Schematic illustration of traditional heterojunction-type (i) and direct Z-scheme (ii) mechanisms for charge carrier separation. This figure has been reproduced from ref. 365 with permission from the American Chemical Society. 
In addition to CoTiO3, other ABO3 perovskite oxide materials such as SrTiO3 with a band gap of 3.2 eV can also well match g-C3N4 to form SrTiO3/g-C3N4 composites. Irvine and co-workers366 prepared SrTiO3/g-C3N4 composites through a simple synthetic method that allowed close interfacial connections between SrTiO3 crystalline and g-C3N4. The prepared SrTiO3/g-C3N4 composite exhibited a high H2 evolution rate (440 μmol h−1 g−1) under visible-light irradiation, which was higher than that of the traditional anion doped SrTiO3 or physical blends of g-C3N4 and SrTiO3. The enhancement could be because of the compact interfacial interaction between g-C3N4 and SrTiO3 where the photo-generated electron and holes could be rapidly separated.
As a typical ternary metal sulfide, cubic CaIn2S4 material has been employed to construct a 2D heterojunction photocatalyst with g-C3N4 because of its appropriate band gap, good activity, and outstanding stability.367,368 The formed 2D heterostructure of the nanocomposite photocatalyst could not only improve the interfacial contact area between CaIn2S4 and g-C3N4 but also facilitate separation of charge carriers. In consequence, the prepared CaIn2S4/g-C3N4 composite exhibited improved photocatalytic H2 generation activity, e.g., its H2 evolution rate was 102 μmol h−1 g−1, 3-fold higher than that of pristine CaIn2S4. The improved photocatalytic property of the CaIn2S4/g-C3N4 composite mainly resulted from the improved charge separation efficiency.41 Many other complex compound modified g-C3N4 materials have also been reported recently, for example, BiPO4/g-C3N4,369 Bi2O2CO3/g-C3N4,370 Bi5Nb3O15/g-C3N4,371 Bi5O7I/g-C3N4,372 Bi4O5I2/g-C3N4,373 Bi2Fe4O9/g-C3N4,374 SmVO4/g-C3N4,375 ZnFe2O4/g-C3N4,376 MnTiO3/g-C3N4,377 BiFeO3/g-C3N4,378 CeVO4/g-C3N4,379 Ag3PO4/g-C3N4,380 Ag2WO4/g-C3N4,381 Ag2CrO4/g-C3N4,382 and most of them showed enhanced photocatalytic activity.


4.3.6. Composites of other semiconductor and g-C3N4 for HER application. 
In addition to all the composites mentioned above, AgX-based (X = Cl, Br and I) photocatalysts have also been reported as a promising candidate to construct AgX/g-C3N4 heterojunction photocatalysts. Li and co-workers383 fabricated a series of visible-light-driven AgX/g-C3N4 (X = Br, I) composites via a simple water bath approach. Small-sized AgX nanoparticles (∼10 nm) were uniformly distributed on the g-C3N4 surface to form AgX/g-C3N4 heterostructures. After the incorporation of AgX nanoparticles onto g-C3N4, the photocurrents of the AgBr/g-C3N4 and AgI/g-C3N4 composites were observed to be increased to 21- and 8-fold compared with pure g-C3N4, respectively. As a result, the obtained AgX/g-C3N4 composites showed higher photocatalytic property than the pure g-C3N4 and AgX nanoparticles. The improvement was ascribed to the surface plasmon resonance effect produced from Ag and the AgX/g-C3N4 heterojunction structure for boosting the charge migration and separation.384
The improved photocatalytic activity has been achieved by combining Ag with AgX,385,386 and the obtained Ag/AgX composites are promising candidates for the construction of Ag/AgX/g-C3N4 heterojunction photocatalysts. Li and co-workers156 prepared a highly efficient Ag@AgCl/g-C3N4 plasmonic photocatalyst via an in situ ion exchange strategy between the exfoliated g-C3N4 nanosheets with porous 2D morphology and AgNO3. The obtained Ag@AgCl/g-C3N4 composites showed an improved photocatalytic property compared to g-C3N4 and Ag@AgCl. This result indicated that a combination of the synergistic effect of appropriate size plasmonic Ag@AgCl and strong coupling effect between the Ag@AgCl nanoparticles and the exfoliated porous g-C3N4 nanosheets showed an excellent visible-light response and rapid separation of the photogenerated electron–hole pairs, thus evidently enhancing the photocatalytic activity. Guo and co-workers387 prepared a series of Ag@AgBr grafted g-C3N4 (Ag@AgBr/g-C3N4) plasmonic photocatalysts through a deposition–precipitation approach. Improved photocatalytic properties were achieved for Ag@AgBr/g-C3N4 compared to pure g-C3N4 and Ag@AgBr because of the high visible-light adsorption efficiency and Z-scheme structures, which kept the photogenerated electrons and holes with high reduction and oxidation ability.
As we know, the coupling of g-C3N4 with semiconductors enables an efficient improvement in the separation of charge carriers by the mutual transfer of carriers in the composites through a heterojunction-transfer mechanism or a Z-scheme mechanism.388,389 Thus, ternary g-C3N4-based heterojunction photocatalysts will possess more potential applications due to their synergistic effects. Xiang and co-workers390 fabricated a series of ternary CdS/g-C3N4/CuS composite photocatalysts through a convenient low-temperature solid-state route. The prepared ternary CdS/g-C3N4/CuS composites showed improved visible-light-governed photocatalytic properties with a H2 evolution rate of 1151.2 mol h−1 g−1 and apparent quantum efficiency of 16.5% at 420 nm. The improved photocatalytic properties were attributed to the heterojunctions and the synergistic effect of CuS, CdS, and g-C3N4 in the acceleration of the charge separation and mobility.
Song and co-workers391 prepared CdS/Cu7S4/g-C3N4 ternary heterostructures through ion exchange between CdS and Cu+ combined with ultrasonication-assisted self-assembly of CdS/Cu7S4 and g-C3N4 (Fig. 23a). The addition of g-C3N4 increased the light utilization of the CdS/Cu7S4/g-C3N4 heterostructure in the visible and near-infrared light region, which further improved the photocatalytic activity (Fig. 23b). The CdS/Cu7S4/g-C3N4 composite showed the largest specific surface area (ca. 37 m2 g−1) because of the addition of g-C3N4, which can offer more active sites and facilitate the charge carrier transport (Fig. 23c). By regulating the content of Cu7S4, the H2 evolution rate on CdS/Cu7S4 nanocomposites can be effectively tuned (Fig. 23d). The H2 evolution rate was markedly enhanced with the introduction of Cu7S4 as a co-catalyst, reaching a value as high as 1930 μmol g−1 h−1. More importantly, the H2 evolution rate and photochemical stability of the CdS/Cu7S4 composites were greatly enhanced after coupling with the g-C3N4 nanosheets, and the maximum H2 evolution rate reached 3570 μmol g−1 h−1 over the CdS/Cu7S4/g-C3N4 ternary heterostructure (Fig. 23e and g). In addition, the CdS/Cu7S4/g-C3N4 ternary heterostructure showed the highest photocurrent density, suggesting that the construction of the CdS/Cu7S4/g-C3N4 heterojunctions can effectively strengthen the electron–hole separation and further enhance the photocatalytic activity (Fig. 23f). The major reason for the high carrier density of the CdS/Cu7S4/g-C3N4 ternary heterostructure was that the existence of Cu7S4 and g-C3N4 in the CdS/Cu7S4/g-C3N4 could availably improve the charge migration and dramatically restrain the electron–hole recombination (Fig. 23h). The photogenerated electrons produced from the conduction band of g-C3N4 could be directly migrated to the conduction band of CdS, and then to the conduction band of Cu7S4. The separation of electron–hole pairs could be boosted because of the formed p–n junction between CdS and Cu7S4. Furthermore, after g-C3N4 was introduced to the system, it could bring more heterojunction spots and utilize more solar light, which greatly increased the charge carrier lifetime to protect CdS and Cu7S4 from possible photocorrosion (Fig. 23i). All results encourage us for the rational design and exploration of multiple component g-C3N4-based heterostructures with widened light absorption, which may pave new avenues for further accelerating the photocatalytic research studies.
 Fig. 23  (a) Diagrammatic sketch for the construction of CdS/Cu7S4/g-C3N4 ternary heterostructures. (b) UV-vis-NIR diffuse reflectance spectra and (c) N2 adsorption–desorption isotherms of CdS, CdS/Cu7S4 and CdS/Cu7S4/g-C3N4 heterostructures. (d) Photocatalytic H2 generation activity of CdS/Cu7S4 with different molar ratios of Cd to Cu (with and without g-C3N4). (e) Photocatalytic HER activity of the different catalysts under visible-light irradiation (>420 nm). (f) Photocurrent responses for CdS (black), CdS/Cu7S4 (red) and CdS/Cu7S4/g-C3N4 heterostructures (blue). (g) Cycling hydrogen evolution property of CdS/Cu7S4 (red) and CdS/Cu7S4/g-C3N4 heterostructure (blue) for 15 h. (h) Mott–Schottky plots of CdS, CdS/Cu7S4 and CdS/Cu7S4/g-C3N4 heterostructures, and (i) schematic illustration of the possible visible-light photocatalytic charge transfer and H2 evolution processes in the CdS/Cu7S4/g-C3N4 ternary heterostructure. This figure has been reproduced from ref. 391 with permission from the American Chemical Society. 
Yang and co-workers392 reported a robust and highly efficient visible-light-guided photocatalyst, namely mesoporous TiO2/WO3/g-C3N4 ternary hybrid nanofibers, prepared by a foaming-assisted electrospinning strategy followed by a solution dipping strategy. The prepared TiO2/WO3/g-C3N4 hybrid fibers, without the noble-metal assistance, exhibited an ultrahigh H2 evolution rate (5732 μmol h−1 g−1), which was considerably improved compared to those of pure TiO2 (86 μmol h−1 g−1), pure g-C3N4 (1550 μmol h−1 g−1), TiO2/WO3 (438 μmol h−1 g−1), TiO2/g-C3N4 (3386 μmol h−1 g−1) and P25 (350 μmol h−1 g−1). The significantly improved photocatalytic property could be because of the WO3/g-C3N4 Z-scheme system and the built-in electric field caused by the heterojunctions formed between the g-C3N4 and TiO2 interfaces, resulting in the improved space separation and transfer of the photo-induced charge carriers. Wu and co-workers393 fabricated a series of novel ZnO/ZnS/g-C3N4 ternary composites with double Z-scheme heterojunctions through a facile two-step chemical conversion route. The spherical ZnS nanoparticles were well-loaded onto the ZnO nanoflower surface. After that, the ZnO/ZnS composites were further hybridized with g-C3N4 nanosheets by the static electric attraction. The prepared ZnO/ZnS/g-C3N4 displayed the optimum photocatalytic H2 evolution rate of 301 μmol h−1 g−1 under solar light illumination. The higher photocatalytic property of the ZnO/ZnS/g-C3N4 composite was probably because of the multi-step charge migration and effective electron–hole separation in the double Z-scheme system.
Many other semiconductor/g-C3N4 nanocomposite heterojunction photocatalysts have also been reported recently. For example, g-C3N4/Al2O3,394 g-C3N4/Ag2SO4,395 g-C3N4/Bi4O5I2,373 g-C3N4/Bi5O7I,372 DyVO4/g-C3N4,396 g-C3N4/Ag2VO2PO4,397 Mn0.8Cd0.2S/g-C3N4,398 CdLa2S4/g-C3N4,399 LaFeO3/g-C3N4,400 g-C3N4/Cu3B2O6,401 NiS/MoS2/g-C3N4,402 Co3O4/Fe3O4/g-C3N4,403 TiO2/In2O3@g-C3N4,404 g-C3N4/Al2O3/ZnO,405 WO3/g-C3N4/CoOx,406 g-C3N4/Fe3O4/Ag2CrO4,407 g-C3N4/ZnO/AgCl,408 g-C3N4/Fe3O4/AgI,409 g-C3N4/Ca2Nb2TaO10,410 g-C3N4/Cu3P,411 Ag2S/g-C3N4,412 Ag/AgVO3/C3N4,413 g-C3N4/Ag/MoS2,414 ZnO/g-C3N4/MoS2,415 BiOCl/g-C3N4/Cu2O/Fe3O4,416 g-C3N4/Fe3O4/AgI/Bi2S3,417etc. Some of them have been investigated for photocatalytic applications.
The development of a semiconductor–g-C3N4 heterojunction with appropriate valence band and conduction band potentials is considered to be one of the most commonly used strategies for boosting charge migration and separation to further enhance the photocatalytic properties. Semiconductor–g-C3N4 hybrids show an outstanding photocatalytic HER performance (e.g., 16400 μmol h−1 g−1 for the NiS/g-C3N4 and 18836 μmol h−1 g−1 for the N-doped ZnO/g-C3N4 nanosheets) (Table 4) and relatively low cost, but unfortunately, semiconductors particularly those containing heavy metal elements potentially cause environmental pollutions and biological hazards. In a comprehensive consideration of the catalytic efficiency and cost, an effective combination of multiple components with different functions is supposed to greatly improve catalytic efficiency and simultaneously reduce the cost.

4.4. Composites of metal organic frameworks and g-C3N4 for HER application
Metal organic framework (MOF) is a new type of crystalline organic–inorganic complex with uniform pore structures, high stability and tunable metrics, and one kind of potential low-bandgap materials.418–420 Unlike many other materials, MOFs benefit from a high surface area, controllable pore size and tunable light harvesting capacity.421–424 Up to now, researchers have paid much attention to the integration of MOFs with g-C3N4 because of their structural and species diversities, controllable cavities and porosity, and high specific surface areas.184,425 Yuan and co-workers426 prepared a novel highly active composite photocatalyst UiO-66/g-C3N4 by hybridizing Zr-containing MOFs (UiO-66) and g-C3N4via a thermal annealing process. Through coupling UiO-66 with g-C3N4, the photocatalytic HER property was greatly improved. The optimal H2 evolution rate of the UiO-66/g-C3N4 was up to 14110 μmol h−1 g−1, 17-fold that of pure g-C3N4 (800 μmol h−1 g−1). The improvement was because of the efficient interfacial charge migration from photoexcited g-C3N4 to UiO-66. Besides, the high content of g-C3N4 endowed the UiO-66/g-C3N4 composites with the “quasi-polymeric nature,” which was different from the reported heterostructures.
The carbon nanostructure derived from zeolitic imidazolate framework (ZIF-8) can form an intimate contact with g-C3N4 and possess large specific surface area, which serves as an electron acceptor for promoting the separation of photogenerated charge carriers, and as an effective cocatalyst to improve photocatalytic properties. A thermal condensation process was used for the preparation of C-ZIF/g-C3N4 photocatalysts, in which a ZIF-8 and melamine were employed as the starting materials (Fig. 24a). Prolonged ultrasonication could not detach the ZIF-8 nanoparticles from the g-C3N4 nanosheets, suggesting the construction of an intimate interface, which benefited the charge separation in photocatalysis (Fig. 24b). The photoluminescence emission intensity for the C-ZIF/g-C3N4 photocatalysts remarkably decreased with increasing C-ZIF contents, implying an enhanced charge carrier separation efficiency in the C-ZIF/g-C3N4 composites (Fig. 24c), and the radiative lifetime for charge carriers was effectively elongated by compositing a small amount of C-ZIF onto g-C3N4 (Fig. 24d); all of these were beneficial for photocatalytic HER. All C-ZIF/g-C3N4 composites without the Pt co-catalyst exhibited much enhanced H2 production activity and good stability. Among them, the 1 wt% C-ZIF/g-C3N4 composite displayed the highest H2 evolution rate (326 μmol h−1 g−1), which was ca. 36.2-fold higher than that of pure g-C3N4, and even 2.8-fold higher than that of the Pt loaded g-C3N4 (Fig. 24e and f). This enhancement was mainly put down to the improved charge separation and migration ability (Fig. 24g–j). Under visible light irradiation, g-C3N4 absorbed photons to produce electrons from its valence band to the conduction band. The electrons in the conduction band of g-C3N4 fleetly migrate to C-ZIF to perform photocatalytic HER due to the role of electron acceptor and cocatalyst of C-ZIF. This work offers an effective approach for the exploration of metal-free, all carbon-based g-C3N4 photocatalysts for photocatalytic HER.184
 Fig. 24  (a) Diagrammatic sketch of the preparation of C-ZIF/g-C3N4 composites. (b) TEM images of 1 wt% C-ZIF/g-C3N4 composites, the red dashed circles indicated the C-ZIF nanoparticles grown on g-C3N4 nanosheets. (c) Photoluminescence spectra of pure g-C3N4 and C-ZIF/g-C3N4 composites with different C-ZIF contents. (d) Time-resolved fluorescence decay spectra of pure g-C3N4 and the 1 wt% C-ZIF/g-C3N4 composite. (e) Photocatalytic H2 evolution rate of pure g-C3N4, 3 wt% Pt/g-C3N4, and C-ZIF/g-C3N4 composites with different C-ZIF contents. (f) Photocatalytic HER as a function of reaction time in 10 vol% TEOA aqueous solution without loading the Pt co-catalyst under visible-light irradiation (λ > 400 nm). (g) The polarization curves of various catalysts in 0.5 M H2SO4 at a sweep rate of 5 mV s−1. (h) Photoluminescence spectra of g-C3N4, Pt/g-C3N4, and 1 wt% C-ZIF/g-C3N4 composites under 375 nm excitation at room temperature. (i) Schematic illustration showing the mechanism of photocatalytic hydrogen evolution for C-ZIF/g-C3N4 composites under visible-light irradiation (λ > 400 nm), and (j) Nyquist plots obtained at an AC voltage with an amplitude of 5 mV over the frequency range from 105 to 10−1 Hz for g-C3N4 and 1 wt% C-ZIF/g-C3N4 electrodes in 0.5 mol L−1 Na2SO4 aqueous solution under visible-light irradiation (λ > 400 nm). This figure has been reproduced from ref. 184 with permission from the Royal Society of Chemistry. 
MIL is a highly porous and crystalline titanium dicarboxylate with high thermal stability and photochemical property, owning large surface area and accessible pore diameters. This material not only possesses high density of the immobilized Ti sites within porous MOFs, but also can be used to synthesize isostructural MOFs with improved photocatalytic properties by the incorporation of organic ligands.427 Han and co-workers428 presented a facile grinding strategy for the production of a g-C3N4/MIL-53(Fe) composite, which displayed great potential as a highly efficient photocatalyst for HER under simulated visible-light illumination. The introduction of MIL-53(Fe) into g-C3N4 could evidently improve photocatalytic activity, which was probably because of the charge spatial separation between g-C3N4 and MIL-53(Fe). In consequence, the highest H2 evolution rate of 905 μmol h−1 g−1 was obtained for the g-C3N4/MIL-53(Fe)-0.5 composite, 335- and 47-fold higher than those of pure MIL-53(Fe) (2.7 μmol h−1 g−1) and g-C3N4 (19.3 μmol h−1 g−1), respectively.
Usually, MOFs possess a high surface area, controllable pore size and tunable light harvesting capacity, and thus seem to be an ideal material to combine with g-C3N4 to achieve highly efficient photocatalytic HER with enhanced visible-light utilization efficiency. However, up to now, only a few works have been reported and relatively low photocatalytic activities have been achieved (Table 4). Thus, there's still a lot of room for designing and synthesizing novel MOFs/g-C3N4 hybrids with greatly improved photocatalytic performance. Based on this fact, it is believed that the MOFs/g-C3N4 hybrids have great potential for large-scale production in the near future.
4.5. Composites of carbon and g-C3N4 for HER application
Carbon materials with π-conjugated structures, e.g., graphene, carbon nanotubes, carbon dots, ordered mesoporous carbon, activated carbon, etc., have been used as promising substrates and reductive cocatalysts to integrate with photoactive nanostructures to restrain the reorganization of electron–hole pairs generated by light due to their unique structural, optical and electronic features.429 Different kinds of carbon/g-C3N4 composites are elaborated in the following subsection.

4.5.1. Composites of graphene and g-C3N4 for HER application. 
Graphene, a honeycomb nanostructured 2D macromolecular nanosheet of carbon atoms, has achieved great attraction because of the excellent mechanical, thermal, optical, and electrical properties, especially optical and electrical features, which restrain the reorganization of light-generated electron–hole pairs and enhance photocatalytic properties.430,431 Thus, graphene/g-C3N4 composites have received growing attention in photocatalytic HER application. The first example of graphene/g-C3N4 composites as photocatalysts for HER application was reported by Jaroniec and co-workers,182 who prepared graphene/g-C3N4 composite photocatalysts via an impregnation chemical reduction approach involving polycondensation of melamine with graphene oxide (GO) and N2H4·H2O at 550 °C under N2 protection. After graphene nanosheets were produced, g-C3N4 was loaded on the surface to form layered composites. The graphene nanosheets here served as electronic conductive pathways and could be used for efficient separation of the photogenerated current carrier, thus further improved the photocatalytic properties of g-C3N4 for HER. The optimal photocatalytic activity was achieved by adding 1.0 wt% graphene with a much higher H2 evolution rate (451 μmol h−1 g−1) compared to pure g-C3N4 (147 μmol h−1 g−1).
An enhanced visible light absorption was achieved after loading GO on the g-C3N4 surface, and the photoluminescence spectra indicated that g-C3N4/GO possessed higher photo-induced charge separation efficiency than pure g-C3N4. Therefore, the g-C3N4/GO composites could obtain a prominent improvement of photocatalytic activity with visible-light irradiation.432 In addition, g-C3N4/reduced graphene oxide (rGO) composites were readily regulated through simply changing the rGO dosage, and a suitable rGO portion in the composites could improve the oxidation ability due to the existence of a significant positively-shifted valence band edge potential.433 The g-C3N4 band structure could also be regulated, e.g., shifted from “n-type” to “p-type” via the introduction of rGO (≤1 wt%).434 However, there is an important factor which needs to be known, namely, what are the roles of the O atoms in GO and rGO in the photocatalytic reactions? To answer this question, Huang and co-workers435 systematically explored the interplay between g-C3N4 and rGO using large-scale ab initio calculations. The sp2-bonded C and N atoms formed the graphitic planes which were stacked in a staggered fashion to generate a laminar structured g-C3N4. In contrast to single g-C3N4 monolayer, g-C3N4/rGO composites possessed significantly decreased band gap, and more dispersive levels near the Fermi level at a higher O concentration, which indicated that the effective mass of the carrier became smaller. Most importantly, a Type-II, staggered band alignment could be acquired at the g-C3N4/rGO interface, and the active sites of negatively charged O atoms in the rGO resulted in high photocatalytic HER activity. Thus, the O atoms were believed to play considerably significant roles in the g-C3N4/rGO composites.
Usually, the preparation strategy of graphene/g-C3N4 composites involved a strong ultrasonic exfoliation process for g-C3N4 and graphene, a protonation process for g-C3N4 by acid pretreatment, a heating reaction for the electrostatic self-assembly of protonated g-C3N4 and GO, and a reduction reaction of GO to form graphene/g-C3N4 composites.437,438 The entire process was complex, possessed low yields and high energy costs, and the size of the protonated g-C3N4 was still too large. Fan's group439 fabricated porous protonated graphitic carbon nitride/rGO (pPCN/rGO) composites by an acidic cutting and hydrothermal method. The holey structure not only accelerated photo-induced charge transfer, but also endowed the pPCN with more exposure of the active sites. Compared to the pure pPCN and rGO, the prepared pPCN-5 wt%/rGO composite possessed significantly improved photocatalytic performance with H2 evolution rate up to 557 μmol h−1 g−1 when exposed to visible-light. Based on this study, a novel H2SO4-assisted dissolution–precipitation strategy was also applied to prepare pPCN/rGO composites. Bulk g-C3N4 was firstly dissolved in concentrated H2SO4 without destroying its structure, which not only provided positive polarity on the surface but promoted the exfoliation of g-C3N4 from bulk to ultrathin g-C3N4 nanosheets directly. Then, the dissolved protonated g-C3N4 was composited with rGO dispersion liquid with molecular level contact by electrostatic self-assembly (Fig. 25a). The thin pPCN nanosheets grown on the large layered RGO nanosheets formed layered pPCN/RGO composites with an intimate 2D/2D interfacial contact (Fig. 25b). The PCN/2 wt% rGO composite showed the highest ratio of surface to volume, which was 1.28 and 5.37 times higher than those of pPCN and CN, respectively (Fig. 25c). The prepared PCN/rGO composites showed smaller charge migratory resistance and more effective charge separation than those of pPCN and CN (Fig. 25d–g). The pPCN/2 wt% rGO composite possessed the highest H2 evolution rate (715 μmol h−1 g−1), which was approximately 2.6 and 13-fold higher than those of pPCN (278 μmol h−1 g−1) and CN (55.6 μmol h−1 g−1), respectively (Fig. 25h). Combined with the large specific surface area and improved light absorption properties, the pPCN/rGO heterojunction showed a remarkable improvement in photocatalytic HER performance (Fig. 25i).436 As is known to us, the dissolution of g-C3N4 still remains a challenge, and this work offers a novel dissolution–precipitation strategy for the construction of g-C3N4-based heterojunctions, which can achieve exfoliation and simultaneous protonation. In addition, this strategy is highly efficient with low energy consumption and easy operation.
 Fig. 25  (a) Diagrammatic sketch for the synthesis route of pPCN and pPCN/rGO samples through a dissolution-electrostatic self-assembly precipitation method. (b) TEM images of the pPCN/rGO nanocomposite. (c) Nitrogen adsorption–desorption isotherms of the as-prepared samples. (d) Photoluminescence spectra, (e) time resolved fluorescence decay spectra, (f) transient photocurrent responses and (g) Nyquist plots for CN, pPCN and pPCN/2 wt% rGO samples. (h) Photocatalytic H2 generation performance of the different samples and stable H2 evolution by the pPCN/2 wt% rGO under irradiation with visible-light (λ > 420 nm). (i) Proposed photocatalytic H2 generation mechanisms over the pPCN/rGO heterojunction under visible-light illumination. This figure has been reproduced from ref. 436 with permission from Elsevier. 


4.5.2. Composites of carbon dots and g-C3N4 for HER application. 
Carbon nanodots (CDs), a new kind of carbon materials with a diameter of <10 nm, have obtained growing attention because of their outstanding optical and electronic performances.440 When CDs were combined with g-C3N4, CDs could serve as effective electron-transfer materials to transfer the photo-generated electrons in g-C3N4 and reduce H2O to H2 due to their suitable LUMO position, which was lower than g-C3N4 but higher than the potential position of the normal hydrogen electrode according to theoretical calculations.441 The first example of CDs/g-C3N4 composites as photocatalysts for HER application was reported by Kang and co-workers.442 CDs/g-C3N4 could photocatalyze H2O to H2 and O2 when irradiated with visible-light. In the CDs/g-C3N4 composite, g-C3N4 acted for photocatalysis, and CDs functioned for chemical catalysis and increased the light absorbance, quantum efficiency and solar energy conversion efficiency. As a result, the as-developed CDs/g-C3N4 composite photocatalyst exhibited high quantum efficiency (16%, 6.29% and 4.42% for λ = 420 ± 20 nm, 580 ± 15 nm and 600 ± 10 nm, respectively) and overall solar energy conversion efficiency (>2.0%). Moreover, the prepared CDs/g-C3N4 composite photocatalyst maintained a high rate of H2 and O2 generation (for λ > 420 nm) with moderate stability after 200 cycles and used over 200 days. Yu and co-workers443 reported a facile hydrothermal method to prepare CDs/g-C3N4 photocatalysts with CDs uniformly and steadily loaded on the g-C3N4 surface in the presence of L-ascorbic acid and g-C3N4. The prepared CDs/g-C3N4 photocatalysts exhibited better photocatalytic properties towards H2 generation under UV light illumination than pure g-C3N4, which was probably attributed to the fast interfacial charge migration.
Carbon quantum dots (CQDs) with steerable size and band gap, affluent hydrophilic surface groups and durable stability can serve as an active component in CQDs/g-C3N4 composite photocatalysts. Tang and co-workers444 reported an example of NIR-light-driven metal-free photocatalytic H2 generation via integrating g-C3N4 nanosheets with CQDs into a heterostructure (CQDs/g-C3N4) via a facile one-step hydrothermal strategy. An appropriate amount of electron withdrawing CQDs anchored onto g-C3N4 nanosheets can effectively facilitate energy and charge migration by forming electronic coupling on the basis of the cascade energy alignments and the built-in electric field between the intimately contacted g-C3N4 and CQDs with similar graphitic structures. In consequence, the H2 evolution rates of CQDs/g-C3N4 were greatly enhanced in both UV-vis and visible regions, achieving a NIR induced H2 production with a rate of 6.76 μmol h−1 g−1 without any co-catalysts under 808 nm irradiation. Zhang and co-workers445 prepared CQDs/g-C3N4 composites through a facile solvothermal reaction in ethanol. The prepared CQDs/g-C3N4 composite exhibited an improved photocatalytic property with a H2 evolution rate of 2322 μmol h−1 g−1, which was thrice the value of the pure g-C3N4 nanosheets (756 μmol h−1 g−1). The CQDs were beneficial to trap electrons and boost the separation of the photo-induced electron–hole pairs of g-C3N4 nanosheets. In fact, a Type-II van der Waals heterojunction could be formed between CQDs and g-C3N4, in which CQDs also acted as a spectral sensitizer, which was confirmed by theoretical calculation.441 Fang and co-workers446 prepared CQDs/g-C3N4 composites via a similar method. The ultrathin 2D structured g-C3N4 nanosheets possessed a series of hollow spherical structures, and CQDs were well dispersed and uniformly anchored into the g-C3N4 nanosheet network via π–π stacking interactions. The prepared CQDs/g-C3N4 composites showed an enhanced photocatalytic property with a H2 evolution rate of 88.1 μmol h−1 g−1, which was 5.14-fold higher than that of the g-C3N4 nanosheets (9 μmol h−1 g−1) (Fig. 26a and b). This enhancement was because of the fact that the incorporation of CQDs into the g-C3N4 nanosheet texture could effectively facilitate the photo-induced charge transfer and separation, leading to the obviously enhanced photocatalytic HER (Fig. 26c and d). Here, a tentative and comprehensive mechanism for enhancing photocatalytic performance was first proposed (Fig. 26e). Firstly, under visible-light irradiation, the photogenerated electrons excited from g-C3N4 nanosheets could be shuttled freely to the conducting texture of the CQD (served as electron acceptor), thus efficiently slowing the recombination and prolonging the electron–hole pairs lifetime. Secondly, the π–π conjugation CQDs were used as a photosensitizer to sensitize g-C3N4 nanosheets and donate the electrons to the conduction band of g-C3N4 nanosheets, leading to an extended visible-light response region for photocatalytic HER. Thirdly, CQDs with upconversion photoluminescence properties could absorb a longer wavelength visible-light and then emit a shorter wavelength light, resulting in more charge carriers formed by the excited g-C3N4 nanosheets. This advanced enhancement mechanism about CQDs/g-C3N4 composites provides us with a new understanding about the role of CQDs and g-C3N4. A theoretical calculation result can be good assistant evidence to explain why or how, and thus, an effective integration of theoretical calculation and experimental results is highly worth considering in future work.
 Fig. 26  (a) Photocatalytic HER activity of different g-C3N4-based photocatalysts. (b) H2 evolution rate under visible-light irradiation (λ > 420 nm) for the CQDs/g-C3N4 composites with different CQD contents. (c) Periodic ON/OFF photocurrent response in 0.25 M Na2S/0.35 M Na2SO3 electrolyte under visible light irradiation (λ > 420 nm) at 0.5 V vs. Ag/AgCl electrode. (d) Nyquist plots in the dark and (e) schematic illustration for the photocatalytic process of CQDs/g-C3N4 photocatalysts under visible-light irradiation (λ > 420 nm). This figure has been reproduced from ref. 446 with permission from Elsevier. 


4.5.3. Composites of multi-walled carbon nanotubes and g-C3N4 for HER application. 
Multi-walled carbon nanotubes (MWCNTs) are nanoscale-sized tubes consisting of seamless cylindrical graphitic sheets with strong mechanical properties, low density, and remarkable thermal stability,447,448 and sp2 hybrid carbon atoms provide perfect conditions for electron transfer, which results in a high conductivity.449 The introduction of MWCNTs may change the g-C3N4 band gap and facilitate visible-light harvesting, thus further improving photocatalytic performance. Inspired by this idea, Wang and co-workers450 fabricated various MWCNTs/g-C3N4 composites via direct thermal polymerization of cyanamide with appropriate amounts of MWCNTs, which provided good thermal stability for the obtained composites. The g-C3N4 was uniformly coated on the MWCNT surface. The prepared MWCNTs/g-C3N4 composites showed an enhanced photocatalytic property when compared to pure MWCNTs and g-C3N4. Ge and co-workers27 constructed various MWCNTs/g-C3N4 composites via a facile heating approach. The optimal H2 evolution rate of 75.8 μmol h−1 g−1 was achieved with a MWCNT content of 2.0 wt%, which was ca. 3.7 times that of pure g-C3N4 (20.3 μmol h−1 g−1). The MWCNTs possessed higher electron capture capability and could promote electron migration from g-C3N4 to their surface, thus resulting in an improved photocatalytic performance. Ogale and co-workers451 reported a dramatic 100% improvement for the optimum 0.5 wt% MWCNTs/g-C3N4 composite. Their study revealed that besides the charge migration effects, the morphological change in MWCNTs/g-C3N4 composites also played a significant role in the photocatalytic properties. Chen and co-workers452 reported hybrid MWCNTs/g-C3N4 composites using cyanamide as a precursor. The optimal MWCNT content was determined to be 0.2 wt% in the MWCNTs/g-C3N4 composites with H2 evolution rate at 394 μmol h−1 g−1, which was ca. 2.4-fold higher than that of pure g-C3N4 (164 μmol h−1 g−1). The improvement was mainly due to the MWCNTs/g-C3N4 intimate interactions that enhanced the charge transfer effect. The introduction of MWCNTs was beneficial to promote electron–hole separation on the excited g-C3N4via extending the charge carrier lifetimes.


4.5.4. Composites of other carbon forms and g-C3N4 for HER application. 
Carbon black is a form of paracrystalline carbon with a high ratio of surface area to volume. The particle size of carbon black can be tuned from 5 nm to hundreds of nanometers, indicating that it is easy to disperse carbon black with a small particle size on the g-C3N4 surface for a uniform distribution and large contact area. Yan and co-workers453 reported conductive carbon black/g-C3N4 composites with carbon black nanoparticles uniformly dispersed on g-C3N4 surface using a simple one-step molten salt route. The carbon black nanoparticles acted as conductive pathways to effectively facilitate the separation and transfer of the photo-induced charge carriers, and thus enhancing the photocatalytic properties under visible light irradiation. The carbon black/g-C3N4 composite showed an improved photocatalytic property with ca. 4.2-fold higher H2 evolution rate (689 μmol h−1 g−1) than the pristine g-C3N4 intercalation compound (163 μmol h−1 g−1).
Among all carbon materials, the physicochemical properties of 1D carbon fibers (CFs) and MWCNTs are alike, but the former can be easily synthesized from a few of inexpensive polymers via an electrospinning technique, being more consistent with the requirement of low cost compared to the MWCNTs. Huang and co-workers454 reported a CFs/g-C3N4 composite photocatalyst fabricated via a facile two-step strategy including electrospinning and a subsequent calcination route. The introduced CFs formed intimate interactions with g-C3N4 to effectively increase the photocatalytic HER activity with the optimal value of 1080 μmol h−1 g−1 when the composite photocatalyst was exposed to visible light (λ ≥ 420 nm), which was ca. 4.6-fold the value of pure g-C3N4.
Although considerable efforts have been made to functionalize g-C3N4 with various carbon materials to enhance its electronic and photoelectrochemical performance, only a few works are reported to assemble g-C3N4 into unique architectures toward highly efficient photocatalytic applications.455 The core–shell architecture of carbon@g-C3N4 is a classical example, which offers a large accessible specific surface area, various electron transport channels, and short mass diffusion distances. All of these features are beneficial to charge separation and migration, and thus accelerate the photoelectrochemical process. Fan and co-workers456 fabricated a special core–shell architectured carbon spheres/g-C3N4via a one-step electrostatic self-assembly route (Fig. 27a). The chemical protonation of g-C3N4 solids with strong oxidizing acids (such as HNO3) was an efficient strategy to prepare highly stable and flexible g-C3N4 colloids, which could be coated onto the surface of carbon spheres with a thickness of ∼3 nm and a diameter of ∼200 nm via electrostatic adsorption (Fig. 27b and c). The obtained carbon@g-C3N4 showed high mechanical stability with broadened light absorption and enhanced conductivity for charge transport. Thus, the carbon@g-C3N4 photocatalysts possessed a markedly enhanced photocatalytic property with a high H2 evolution rate up to 2588.4 μmol h−1 g−1, which was ca. 7.93-fold that of pure g-C3N4 (326.4 μmol h−1 g−1) (Fig. 27d and e). This outstanding photocatalytic performance could be mainly attributed to the narrower gap sp2 carbon cluster embedded in carbon spheres, the promoted charge-carrier transport capability, prolonged electron lifetime, and lower recombination of electron–hole pairs. In the photocatalytic process, the photo-induced electrons were injected into the g-C3N4 conduction band, and reacted with H+ ions of the adsorbed molecular water. In addition, the photo-induced electrons were generated from the valence band to the conduction band of g-C3N4 by the excitation of the high-energy photons. The photogenerated electrons formed in the space-charge regions were transferred to the carbon spheres, and the holes remained on g-C3N4 to participate in the redox reaction (Fig. 27f and g). The high flexibility and compatibility of the g-C3N4 colloidal solution open up a new avenue to load polymeric semiconductors on desired functionalized carbon materials.
 Fig. 27  (a) Diagrammatic sketches of (i) the depolymerization–repolymerization process of g-C3N4, and (ii) the fabrication process of the carbon@g-C3N4 core–shell photocatalysts. (b) TEM and (c) HR-TEM images of carbon@g-C3N4 core–shell photocatalysts. (d) Stability test of H2 generation of pristine g-C3N4 and carbon@g-C3N4 photocatalysts. (e) H2 production over pristine g-C3N4, carbon@g-C3N4, and carbon sphere photocatalysts. (f) Electron–hole pairs in the carbon spheres are generated under visible-light illumination. Based on the relevant position of the band, the electrons inject into g-C3N4, and (g) carbon spheres as an electron sink to scavenge away the electrons, thus hindering the recombination of electron–hole pairs. This figure has been reproduced from ref. 456 with permission from the American Chemical Society. 
Yu and co-workers457 reported novel amorphous carbon/g-C3N4 composites fabricated by simple one-step pyrolysis of glucose and urea precursors. Through accurately regulating the weight ratio of precursors, the amorphous carbon/g-C3N4 composites displayed an enhanced photocatalytic property. The optimal photocatalytic H2 evolution rate was as high as 212.8 μmol h−1 g−1, which was ca. 10-fold the value of pure g-C3N4 (20.9 μmol h−1 g−1). The amorphous carbon/g-C3N4 intimate interface played a crucial role in lengthening the lifetime of charge carriers and boosting the charge transfer, which was beneficial for improving the photocatalytic property. In addition, the significantly improved photocatalytic property was probably because of the enhanced visible-light utilization and expanded specific surface area.
In brief, interface engineering is of great importance to optimize the charge transfer and separation across the heterojunction formed between the carbon materials and g-C3N4 to restrain the electron–hole recombination. However, there is always an issue involving the optimum loading of carbon to be incorporated in the g-C3N4-based hybrid systems in order to reduce the negative effect resulting from excessive carbon. Consequently, optimizing the g-C3N4-based hybrid nanostructures with the modification of an appropriate amount of carbon is very important. That is to say, researchers should design individual material components to the heterointerface construction and optimization process for considerably enhanced photocatalytic HER activity.

4.6. Composites of conductive polymers and g-C3N4 for HER application
A conductive polymer (CP) is deemed to be one of the most important organic semiconductors with strong visible-light absorption and perfect π-conjugated electronic systems.458 A combination of the aromatic-based conjugated system with the π-electronic structures of the CP favors coupling g-C3N4 with CPs to obtain heterojunctions and further improve photocatalytic activity. Up to now, some extensively reported CPs, e.g., polyaniline (PANI), polypyrrole (PPy), graphitized polyacrylonitrile (g-PAN), poly(3-hexylthiophene) (P3HT), and poly(3,4-ethylenedioxythiophene) (PEDOT), have been frequently combined with g-C3N4 as photocatalysts.
PANI is one of the most widely studied CPs, owning many excellent advantages such as low toxicity, high chemical stability, outstanding electron and hole conducting properties, excellent corrosion resistance and low cost.459–461 Thus, it is speculated that the combination of the g-C3N4 photocatalyst with PANI should be ideal for improving visible-light photocatalytic activity. Ge and co-workers462 prepared PANI/g-C3N4 composite photocatalysts through “in situ” oxidation polymerization of aniline monomer with g-C3N4 powder. The PANI/g-C3N4 composite displayed evidently enhanced photocatalytic performance compared with pure g-C3N4. The enhancement was because of the effective electron–hole separation. A hierarchical nanocomposite of PANI nanorods decorated on g-C3N4 nanosheets was successfully prepared through dilute polymerization under −20 °C. The prepared PANI/g-C3N4 hierarchical nanocomposite showed higher photocatalytic performance than pure g-C3N4 and PANI under visible-light illumination. The enhancement was because of the effective separation of the photogenerated charge, the large specific surface area and the energy band structure.463 Inspired by this, Zhu and co-workers464 prepared PANI/g-C3N4 composite hydrogels with 3D hierarchical nanostructure through in situ oxidation polymerization. The 3D hierarchical structure was robust and stable, ensuring the composite hydrogels easy to separate and recycle. Moreover, it possessed high photocatalytic performance because of the excellent interfacial charge separation and photoelectrochemical properties.
PPy is a typical CP with a band gap of 2.2–2.4 eV,465 high conductivity, stability in the oxidized state and attractive redox properties.
