The incorporation of monodisperse PPy nanoparticles onto g-C3N4 nanosheets can form surface junctions to increase the number of photo-generated electrons on g-C3N4. As early as 2013, Chen and co-workers466 reported dramatically improved photocatalytic properties of g-C3N4 for photocatalytic HER from pure water by decorating highly dispersed conductive PPy nanoparticles. For the g-C3N4 loaded with 1.5 wt% PPy nanoparticles, the H2 evolution rate can increase up to 50-fold. This enhancement was attributed to the surface junction, which increased the number of photoinduced electrons and facilitated electron migration to the interface. Fu and co-workers467 prepared PPy/g-C3N4 composites using an in situ polymerization method. Because of the high conductivity, the PPy served as a transport channel for electrons to move onto the g-C3N4 surface, thus restraining the recombination of the photogenerated carriers of g-C3N4 and improving the photocatalytic properties.
P3HT is a CP with a band gap of 1.9–2.1 eV, a higher charge carrier mobility, dissolubility, processability and long-term stability.468 The incorporation of monodisperse P3HT nanoparticles onto g-C3N4 nanosheets can also form surface junctions to increase the number of photo-generated electrons on g-C3N4. Yan and co-workers469 fabricated a polymer composite of g-C3N4 and P3HT for photocatalytic HER from an aqueous solution containing Na2S and using Na2SO3 as the electron donors. The prepared P3HT/g-C3N4 composite showed an enhanced activity with a H2 evolution rate of ca. 11200 μmol h−1, 300-fold higher than that of pure g-C3N4. The efficient charge migration between g-C3N4 and P3HT resulted in improved photocatalytic properties. Zhou and co-workers470 prepared P3HT/g-C3N4 photocatalysts by assembling p-type P3HT particles on n-type g-C3N4 nanoplates through a ball milling approach. The prepared P3HT/g-C3N4 photocatalysts displayed improved photocatalytic properties. The formation of the P3HT/g-C3N4 heterojunction interface improved the separation efficiency of the photogenerated electron–hole pairs and finally led to the improvement of photocatalytic properties.
Polyacrylonitrile (PAN) is a cheap polymeric material, owning unique conjugated structures after heat treatment. When PAN is heated above 600 °C, “graphitization” takes place and graphitized PAN (g-PAN) is formed, which has a layered graphite-like nanosheet structure. The graphite-like network structure of g-PAN is propitious to the rapid charge migration, and a combination of g-PAN with layered g-C3N4 nanosheets offers attractive photocatalytic properties. Chen and co-workers471 reported novel composites consisting of g-PAN nanosheets loaded on layered g-C3N4 nanosheets prepared by a simple one-step pyrolysis of PAN and melamine. The g-PAN nanosheets acted as effective electron migration pathways to boost charge carrier separation in the PAN/g-C3N4 composites. As a result, the 5.0 wt% g-PAN/g-C3N4 composite demonstrated the optimal H2 evolution rate of 370 μmol h−1 g−1, over 3.8-fold that of pristine g-C3N4. Designing and developing hollow g-C3N4-based heterojunction nanostructures is propitious to mass transport in photocatalysts, the enlargement of specific surface area, and the charge carrier separation.472 Usually, hollow g-C3N4 nanostructures can be fabricated via the hard-templating and self-assembled supermolecular methods. However, these approaches suffer from poor structural and morphological controllability. Liu and co-workers473 reported tapered hollow PAN-derived carbon (C-PAN)/g-C3N4 composite nanotubes prepared through a convenient sulfur-mediated self-templating approach by direct pyrolysis of PAN, melamine, and sulfur (Fig. 28a). Its structure and morphology can be easily tuned only by adjusting the calcination temperature, and adding sulfur in the melamine precursor system is beneficial for the formation of hollow structures. The valence band potential of g-C3N4 was more positive than that of the C-PAN, in accordance with the results of Mott–Schottky plots (Fig. 28b and c), suggesting that the photogenerated electrons could migrate from g-C3N4 to C-PAN, promoting the separation of photogenerated charge carriers (Fig. 28d). The average lifetimes for the g-C3N4 and 5 wt% C-PAN/g-C3N4 composite nanotubes were computed to be 7.06 and 8.08 ns, respectively, suggesting that the average lifetime of the C-PAN/g-C3N4 composite nanotubes was lengthened (Fig. 28e). All C-PAN/g-C3N4 composite nanotubes exhibited improved photocatalytic HER activity, among which the 5 wt% CPAN/g-C3N4 composite nanotubes reached the optimum H2 evolution rate (1775 μmol h−1 g−1), 16.7-fold higher than that of pure g-C3N4, and even 4.7-fold higher than that of the C-PAN/g-C3N4 composite nanosheets prepared without sulfur (Fig. 28f). After 15 h of successive illumination, the 5 wt% C-PAN/g-C3N4 composite nanotubes still showed excellent stability and without evident loss in the photocatalytic properties (Fig. 28g). This work offers a new and facile route to develop hollow g-C3N4-based photocatalysts with superior photocatalytic activity.
 Fig. 28  (a) Diagrammatic sketch for the construction of C-PAN/g-C3N4 composite nanotubes. (b) Mott–Schottky plots of (1) g-C3N4, (2) 3 wt% C-PAN/g-C3N4, (3) 5 wt% C-PAN/g-C3N4, and (4) 10 wt% C-PAN/g-C3N4 composite nanotubes. (c) Diagrammatic sketch of the electronic band structure of the g-C3N4 and C-PAN composites. (d) Photoluminescenc spectra of (1) g-C3N4, (2) 3 wt% C-PAN/g-C3N4, (3) 5 wt% C-PAN/g-C3N4, and (4) 10 wt% C-PAN/g-C3N4 composite nanotubes. (e) Time-resolved fluorescence decay spectra of (1) pure g-C3N4 and (2) 5 wt% C-PAN/g-C3N4 composite nanotubes. (f) Photocatalytic H2 evolution rate of (1) pure g-C3N4, (2) 5 wt% C-PAN/g-C3N4 prepared without sulfur in the precursor, (3) sulfur-mediated g-C3N4, and (4) 3 wt% C-PAN/g-C3N4, (5) 5 wt% C-PAN/g-C3N4, and (6) 10 wt% C-PAN/g-C3N4 composite nanotubes, and (g) photocatalytic H2 generation as a function of reaction time for (1) pure g-C3N4 and (2) 5 wt% C-PAN/g-C3N4 composite nanotubes in 10 vol% TEOA aqueous solution under visible-light illumination (λ > 400 nm). This figure has been reproduced from ref. 473 with permission from the American Chemical Society. 
PEDOT is considered to be a promising conducting polymer in optoelectronic devices due to its high electrical conductivity and thermal stability, and good transparency in the visible-light range.474 The introduction of PEDOT can increase the number of photo-generated electrons, and thus improving photocatalytic properties. A new-style g-C3N4-based composite photocatalyst was obtained via co-loading PEDOT as a hole transport pathway and Pt as an electron trap on g-C3N4. The obtained g-C3N4–PEDOT–Pt composite exhibited greatly improved photocatalytic activity for visible-light-guided photocatalytic HER with a H2 evolution rate of ca. 327 μmol h−1 g−1 due to the spatial separation of the reduction and oxidation reaction sites.475
Commonly, CP serves as an electron migration channel for improving the charge transport ability, and also effectively prolonging the lifetime of charge carriers. To date, only a few CP/g-C3N4 hybrids have been investigated for photocatalytic HER under visible light, and relatively low activities have been reported (Table 4). However, it is still believed that there's a large room for performance improvement by rationally designing and engineering novel CP/g-C3N4 hybrids with tailored nanostructures.
4.7. Composites of sensitizers and g-C3N4 for HER application
As reported above, several strategies, such as forming thinner, highly crystalline, doped, and porous g-C3N4 to boost the separation of photogenerated charge carriers and incorporation of metals, semiconductors, carbons, conductive polymers to form heterojunctions, have been employed to improve photocatalytic performance. Despite the above-mentioned efforts, g-C3N4-based photocatalysts with a significantly enlarged visible-light absorption still remains a challenge. To further widen the range of visible-light absorption, sensitization is another attractive strategy for highly efficient capture of solar energy. As early as 2012, Eosin Y (EY), as a typical xanthene dye, has been used as a sensitizer and matrix to prepare EY-sensitized mesoporous g-C3N4,183 and the photoresponse of the mesoporous g-C3N4 can be effectively enlarged up to ca. 600 nm. The mesoporous g-C3N4 with large specific surface area and porous structure can effectively promote EY molecules assembled on the g-C3N4 surface, thus further improving the photocatalytic properties due to the enhanced light harvesting. In consequence, this sensitized photocatalyst demonstrated excellent photocatalytic properties for HER under visible-light irradiation, with the absorption range expanded up to ca. 600 nm. The apparent quantum efficiency was as high as 19.4% under 550 nm irradiation.
Erythrosin B (EB) is another typical xanthene dye, which has been proven to be an excellent sensitizer for the construction of EB-sensitized g-C3N4.476,477 Xu and co-workers476 firstly prepared well-dispersed Pt nanoparticles (∼3 nm) coated on g-C3N4 nanosheets by a chemical reduction method. The prepared Pt/g-C3N4 sample exhibited a high H2 evolution rate (451 μmol h−1 g−1). Surprisingly, through simply introducing an inexpensive dye EB in the photoreaction suspension, an considerably improved H2 evolution rate (6525 μmol h−1 g−1) was achieved; this is due to the efficient electron migration from the excited EB to the conduction band of g-C3N4. EB-sensitized MoSx–g-C3N4 was fabricated through an in situ photoreduction approach. The obtained EB-sensitized MoSx–g-C3N4 showed higher photocatalytic activity and stability than the EB-sensitized g-C3N4 and EB-sensitized MoSx. The highest H2 evolution rate reached 257.25 μmol h−1g−1, and the highest apparent quantum yield was up to 8.3% at 545 nm. The enhancement was attributed to the fact that the MoSx acted as a promosing cocatalyst which facilitated the efficient electron migration between the photoexcited EB, g-C3N4 and MoSx.477
Zinc phthalocyanine (ZnPc) is a type of chromophore well known for its intrinsic absorption in the UV/blue and the red/near IR spectral regions.478 Moreover, ZnPc possesses excellent photochemical and thermal stability and appropriate redox properties, which make them ideal sensitizers for the sensitization of semiconductors, e.g., g-C3N4. When ZnPc was coupled with g-C3N4, the photocatalytic properties and apparent quantum yield of the ZnPc/g-C3N4 were greatly enhanced.479 Moreover, the outstanding photochemical/thermal stabilities and appropriate redox properties of ZnPc also make ZnPc attractive as a dye for the sensitization of relatively wide-band-gap g-C3N4. Peng's group480 developed Zn-tri-PcNc/g-C3N4 composites using the highly asymmetric Zn-tri-PcNc (a zinc ZnPc derivatives) (Fig. 29a) as a sensitizer of g-C3N4. Dynamic time-resolved photoluminescence spectra analyses indicatd that the photogenerated electrons of the excited Zn-tri-PcNc can be efficiently transferred to g-C3N4 (Fig. 29b). Moreover, it was found that chenodeoxycholic acid as a co-adsorbent with Zn-tri-PcNc could improve the electron injection efficiency and restrain the charge recombination (Fig. 29c and d), and further led to significantly promoted photocatalytic H2 production (Fig. 29e and f). Importantly, the Zn-tri-PcNc/g-C3N4 with chenodeoxycholic acid as the co-adsorbent showed a H2 evolution rate of 12520 μmol h−1 g−1 with a turnover number of ∼5008 h−1 under visible-light irradiation (Fig. 29f); moreover, a considerably high apparent quantum yield (1.85%) at 700 nm was achieved (Fig. 29g). The improved photoactivity for H2 production originated from the excitation of Zn-tri-PcNc, enabling the photogenerated electrons to be injected into the conduction band of g-C3N4 and further trapped by the co-catalyst Pt for H2 production. Simultaneously, the oxidized Zn-tri-PcNc were regenerated by accepting electrons from the sacrificial reagent and then re-excited by light irradiation (Fig. 29h). Based on this sensitizer, they also used indole-based D–π–A organic dye (LI-4) and Zn-tri-PcNc as the co-sensitizers to prepare co-sensitized g-C3N4. The prepared co-sensitized g-C3N4 showed extremely high H2 production activity (24760 μmol h−1 g−1) under visible/near-infrared light irradiation. Moreover, the prepared co-sensitized g-C3N4 catalysts also exhibited a wide visible/near infrared light responsive range (400–800 nm) with pretty high apparent quantum yields of 16.3, 7.7, and 1.75% under 420, 500, and 700 nm monochromatic light irradiation, respectively.481 The proposed photocatalytic HER mechanism over Zn-tri-PcNc/g-C3N4 was given as follows. In brief, the excitation of Zn-tri-PcNc was adsorbed on g-C3N4, and the photogenerated electrons were subsequently injected into the conduction band of g-C3N4 and further trapped by the loaded co-catalyst Pt for H2 generation. Meanwhile, the oxidized Zn-tri-PcNc would be regenerated via accepting electron from the sacrificial reagent and then re-excited via visible light illumination. This study displays the promising application of phthalocyanine derivatives/g-C3N4 in photocatalytic HER for more efficiently utilizing the solar light with wavelength up to 600 nm or even 700 nm, which is unprecedented. Such outstanding photocatalytic properties and enlarged visible-light absorption ensure Zn-tri-PcNc/g-C3N4 has great potential for large scale industrial application.
 Fig. 29  (a) Structures of zinc phthalocyanine derivative (Zn-tri-PcNc). (b) Diffuse reflectance absorption spectroscopy spectra of g-C3N4 and Zn-tri-PcNc/g-C3N4 (above) and UV-vis spectra of 10 μM Zn-tri-PcNc solution and the desorbed solution of Zn-tri-PcNc/g-C3N4 (below). (c) Effects of chenodeoxycholic acid on the time-resolved fluorescence decay of the Zn-tri-PcNc/g-C3N4 film (above) or Zn-tri–PcNc solution with/without adding g-C3N4 (below). Excitation and detection wavelengths are 377 and 711 nm, respectively. (d) Effects of coabsorbent chenodeoxycholic acid on the transient photocurrent behaviors under optimal photoreaction conditions and λ ≥ 500 nm light irradiation. (e) Effects of Zn-tri-PcNc and chenodeoxycholic acid amount on the photoactivity for H2 production over Zn-tri-PcNc/g-C3N4. (f) The photocatalytic activity of Zn-tri-PcNc/g-C3N4 under the optimal photoreaction conditions and λ ≥ 500 nm light irradiation. (g) Comparison of diffuse reflectance absorption spectra and apparent quantum yield values of g-C3N4 and Zn-tri-PcNc/g-C3N4 under optimal photoreaction conditions and different monochromatic light irradiation and (h) proposed photocatalytic HER mechanism of the Zn-tri-PcNc/g-C3N4 system. This figure has been reproduced from ref. 480 with permission from the American Chemical Society. 
Similar to ZnPc, magnesium phthalocyanine (MgPc) can also be used as a sensitizer for the sensitization of semiconductor g-C3N4 due to its high chemical stability, absorption of light around ca. 670 nm, and structural similarity to g-C3N4. Both MgPc and g-C3N4 contain planar layers with π electron conjugation, and are mainly composed of C and N, and thus establish strong interactions with each other. In addition, the oxidation potential of the photogenerated hole of g-C3N4 is insufficient to oxidize the hydroxyl group to hydroxyl radicals, making g-C3N4 a particularly suitable photocatalyst for hosting organic dye molecules to ensure stable sensitization under full solar spectrum.188 Domen and co-workers482 prepared a MgPc/Pt/g-C3N4 sample (MgPc as the sensitizer and Pt as the cocatalyst) by a simple process. The prepared MgPc/Pt/g-C3N4 sample showed stable photocatalytic H2 evolution activity with sacrificial reagents (triethanolamine), even under irradiation at wavelengths >600 nm. To further enhance the photocatalytic properties, smooth charge migration of the excited electrons from the dye to the photocatalyst and eventually to the cocatalyst was crucial.
Fluorescein (FL), a xanthene dye, has appropriate excitation and emission wavelengths in the visible region, and a high fluorescence quantum yield in aqueous solution. Moreover, the electron transfer rate from the LUMO of FL to g-C3N4 is higher than that from other dyes.483 Thus, FL has great potential to work as a photosensitizer to enhance the photocatalytic properties of g-C3N4. Zeng and co-workers484 reported a Ag/g-C3N4 composite with a Ag loading of 3 wt% possessing the optimum photocatalytic activity, 3.6-fold higher than that of pure g-C3N4. Surprisingly, when FL was employed as a photosensitizer, the H2 evolution rate shot up to 2014.20 μmol h−1 g−1, over 4.8-fold that of the 3 wt% Ag/g-C3N4 composites. The enhanced photocatalytic activity was in virtue of the synergistic effect between the components as well as the appropriate loading amount of Ag.
It is well known that dye sensitization is an attractive technique to increase the visible-light utilization of g-C3N4 and improve the photocatalytic H2 generation property under visible-light irradiation. Most of the previous works are focused on how to promote the photocatalytic HER activity of g-C3N4 sensitized with dyes under visible light illumination, but the interaction dynamics between g-C3N4 and sensitizer dye is rarely reported. Yu and co-workers485 chose four normally used xanthene dyes, e.g., fluorescein, dibromofluorescein, eosin Y, and erythrosine B (Fig. 30a) to prepare dye-sensitized g-C3N4 composites. The lowest unoccupied molecular orbital energy of all selected xanthene dyes was higher than that of the g-C3N4 conduction band energy, and thus, the electron could easily migrate from the lowest unoccupied molecular orbital of the photoexcited xanthene dye to the conduction band of g-C3N4. It was also found that fluorescence decays of their corresponding dye–SiO2 composites were obviously slower than those of their corresponding dye-sensitized-C3N4 composites (Fig. 30c and e), but almost identical to those of their saturated dye aqueous solutions (Fig. 30b) and the maximal adsorption amount of dye–SiO2 composites was quite similar to that of dye-sensitized-g-C3N4 (Fig. 30d), suggesting the electron transfer from the lowest unoccupied molecular orbital of the photoexcited xanthene dye to the conduction band of g-C3N4. Moreover, the electron on the highest occupied molecular orbital of each selected xanthene dye can transfer to the valence band of photoexcited g-C3N4 on the basis of the prediction from their energy level location, which can prolong the lifetime of the electron on the conduction band of the photoexcited g-C3N4 thus improving photocatalytic performance (Fig. 30f). This study is vital for researchers to deeply understand the fundamental aspects in xanthene dye-sensitized-g-C3N4 photocatalytic systems.
 Fig. 30  (a) Molecular structures of fluorescein, dibromofluorescein, eosin Y, erythrosine B, and g-C3N4. (b) Fluorescence decays of their corresponding saturated dye aqueous solutions, and (c) their corresponding dye-sensitized g-C3N4 composites. (d) UV-vis diffuse reflectance spectra of fluorescein–SiO2, dibromofluorescein–SiO2, eosin Y–SiO2, and erythrosine B–SiO2 composites. (e) Fluorescence decays of their corresponding dye–SiO2 composites, and (f) fluorescence decays of g-C3N4 in g-C3N4 alone, fluorescein–g-C3N4, dibromofluorescein–g-C3N4, eosin Y–g-C3N4, and erythrosine B–g-C3N4 composites. Excitation wavelength, 395 nm; detection wavelength, 470 nm. This figure has been reproduced from ref. 485 with permission from the American Chemical Society. 
Sensitizer/g-C3N4 composites have revealed the highest H2 evolution rate (e.g., 24760 μmol h−1 g−1 reported for the LI-4/g-C3N4/Zn-tri-PcNc) among all reported g-C3N4-based photocatalysts (Table 4). Moreover, enlarged visible-light absorption ensures that they have great potential for large scale industrial application. However, it seems to be detrimental to soil and water sources if the sensitizer is used in a large quantity. In addition, the synthesis of the sensitizer is generally complicated and time consuming, resulting in high cost. In the process of developing sensitizer/g-C3N4 composite photocatalysts, not only should we consider the photocatalytic activity, stability and visible-light utilization efficiency, but also take into account the production and environmental costs. Due to the great potential for practical photocatalytic HER application, future work should focus on the design and development of new-style sensitizers with low cost and toxicity and simple synthesis as well.
4.8. Composites of other materials and g-C3N4 for HER application
As one of the allotropes of phosphorus (P), 2D black phosphorus (BP) has obtained growing attention in photoelectric applications since the first report on field-effect transistors in 2014.486 This new-style 2D material possesses different direct band gaps in monolayer, few-layer and bulk forms, from ca. 2.1 eV for monolayer BP to ca. 0.3 eV for bulk BP. The direct-band-gap features enable BP to act as an efficient photocatalyst with broadband solar absorption. Inspired by this, Majima and co-workers487 reported binary BP/g-C3N4 nanohybrids composed of BP nanoflakes and g-C3N4 nanosheets, which were first utilized as a metal-free photocatalyst. The BP nanoflakes were fixed on the g-C3N4 nanosheet surface (Fig. 31a), which made the absorption for g-C3N4 to exhibit strong absorption in the visible or even near-infrared region (Fig. 31b). The BP/g-C3N4 nanohybrids reached the optimum H2 evolution rate of 1.93 μmol for 3 h under visible-light illumination (λ > 420 nm) without any cocatalyst, and still generated 0.46 μmol H2 for 3 h (λ > 780 nm) (Fig. 31c and d). The decay lifetime of the BP/g-C3N4 was longer than that of BP, indicating that the P–N coordinate bond at the interface acted as the trap site of electrons for HER (Fig. 31e–g). The improved photocatalytic property was owing to the strong BP/g-C3N4 interfacial interaction and efficient charge migration at the interface, inhibiting the recombination of photogenerated charges (Fig. 31h). A type I heterojunction existed in their interface when BP was loaded on the g-C3N4 surface. In this system, under visible light irradiation, g-C3N4 was excited to produce electrons and holes. Then, BP acted as an electron acceptor from adjacent g-C3N4, which was beneficial for restraining the recombination of the photogenerated charges. These results display the advantages of BP/g-C3N4 nanohybrids as a metal-free photocatalyst with broad absorption in the visible and NIR region in developing a new energy conversion system. Similarly, another allotrope of P, red phosphorus (RP), has also been investigated as a promising semiconductor for the fabrication of g-C3N4 composites.488 The decoration of g-C3N4 onto the RP surface resulted in a considerably enhanced photocatalytic property, and the corresponding H2 evolution rate was as high as 310 μmol h−1 g−1, 10-fold higher than that of RP. The improvement was because of the effective separation of the photogenerated electrons and holes over the RP/g-C3N4 heterojunction.
 Fig. 31  (a) TEM image of BP/g-C3N4 nanohybrid. (b) UV-vis diffuse reflectance spectra of g-C3N4, BP, and BP/g-C3N4. (c) Photocatalytic H2 evolution rate on different catalysts (λ > 420 nm). (d) Photocatalytic H2 evolution rate on the BP/g-C3N4 (λ > 780 nm). (e) Time-resolved diffuse reflectance spectroscopic plots for BP and (f) BP/g-C3N4 after irradiation with a 780 nm laser flash. (g) Time profiles of normalized transient absorption at 950 nm, and (h) proposed diagrammatic sketch for the visible and near infrared light activated photocatalytic HER employing BP/g-C3N4 photocatalysts. This figure has been reproduced from ref. 487 with permission from the American Chemical Society. 
Some transition metal hydroxides were also examined as economical co-catalysts of g-C3N4 for photocatalytic HER from water. Yu and co-workers489 prepared Ni(OH)2-modified-g-C3N4 composite photocatalysts through a facile precipitation strategy employing g-C3N4 as the support and Ni(NO3)2 as the precursor. Ni(OH)2 was found to be an efficient co-catalyst for the photocatalytic HER. With the optimal Ni(OH)2 loading of 0.5 mol%, the Ni(OH)2/g-C3N4 composite revealed a H2 evolution rate of 152 μmol h−1 g−1 and an apparent quantum efficiency of 1.1% at 420 nm. Zhou and co-workers490 reported a facile method for the preparation of Cu(OH)2/g-C3N4 composite photocatalysts. The obtained Cu(OH)2/g-C3N4 sample showed evidently improved photocatalytic properties, and the optimal Cu(OH)2 loading content was examined to be 0.34 mol%, showing a H2 evolution rate of 48.7 μmol h−1 g−1, which was more than 16.5-fold that of pure g-C3N4. The enhancement was ascribed to the existence of Cu(OH)2 clusters on porous g-C3N4 surface, which effectively promoted the visible-light absorption and separation of the photogenerated electron–hole pairs. Lu and co-workers491 prepared non-noble metal oxide Co(OH)2 nanoparticles uniformly decorated on g-C3N4 by an in situ chemical deposition approach as a co-catalyst for HER application. After being co-sensitized by Eosin Y and Rose Bengal dyes, this photocatalyst showed a relatively low onset potential (0.26 V) for HER. The g-C3N4 offered a large specific surface area and nanoporous structure for the restricted growth of Co(OH)2 nanoparticles, as well as largely boosted Eosin Y and Rose Bengal molecules assembled on its surface, which facilitated the charge migration from the dye to the co-catalyst, and thus improved the photocatalytic activity.
Over the last few years, ternary or even multicomponent g-C3N4-based heterojunction photocatalysts have also been widely studied for HER application. Neppolian and co-workers492 fabricated rGO decorated g-C3N4–TiO2 ternary hybrid layered photocatalysts by an ultrasound-assisted wet impregnation strategy with different mass ratios of g-C3N4 to TiO2. The optical band gap of the g-C3N4–TiO2/rGO composite was observed to be red shifted to 2.56 eV from 2.70 eV for bare g-C3N4. Moreover, the g-C3N4–TiO2/rGO composite showed an improved photocatalytic property with a H2 evolution rate of 13143 μmol h−1 g−1, which was ca. 78- and 2.5-fold higher than those of g-C3N4 and TiO2, respectively. The enhancement could be attributed to the improved visible-light absorption and an effective separation of the photogenerated electron–hole pairs at the g-C3N4–TiO2/rGO interface. Li and co-workers493 prepared a g-C3N4/acetylene black/Ni(OH)2 ternary composite photocatalyst with dual robust electron co-catalysts employing a simple two-step method: ultrasonic dispersion treatment and a subsequent precipitation process. The g-C3N4/0.5%-acetylene black/1.0%-Ni(OH)2 composite showed the highest H2 evolution rate (240 μmol h−1 g−1) under visible-light irradiation, which was 320-, 100- and 3.31-fold higher than those of pure g-C3N4, g-C3N4–0.5% acetylene black and g-C3N4–1.0% Ni(OH)2, respectively. It was found that the synergistic effect between the robust acetylene black and Ni(OH)2 as dual electron co-catalysts on the g-C3N4 surface could effectively boost the separation of the photo-generated electron–hole pairs and improve the H2 production activity.
Xie and co-workers404 prepared a TiO2/In2O3@g-C3N4 ternary hybrid via a facile solvothermal strategy. The H2 evolution rate of the prepared TiO2/In2O3@g-C3N4 ternary composite was found to be ca. 48-fold that of pure g-C3N4. The enhancement was mainly ascribed to the interfacial migration of the photogenerated electrons and holes between TiO2, In2O3 and g-C3N4, resulting in effective charge separation on these semiconductors. Selvam and co-workers494 reported a novel ternary CdS/rGO/g-C3N4 composite photocatalyst through a hydrothermal process. A remarkably improved H2 evolution rate (676.5 μmol h−1 g−1) was obtained for the Z-scheme CdS/rGO/g-C3N4 composite, which was 8.6 times that of pure g-C3N4.
Besides the above ternary nanocomposites, substantial efforts have also been made to search for other candidates through hybridizing with g-C3N4 to form multicomponent heterojunction systems. For example, graphene/g-C3N4/P3HT,495 Ag/Ag2O/g-C3N4,496 Ag/AgVO3/g-C3N4,413 g-C3N4/MWCNT/NiS,497 g-C3N4/N-doped graphene/MoS2,498 g-C3N4/N-doped graphene/NiFe,499 g-C3N4/rGO/S8,500 Au/g-C3N4/Fe2O3,501 Ag/Fe3O4/g-C3N4,502 g-C3N4/ZnO/AgCl,408 g-C3N4/RGO/Bi2WO6,18 ZnCr2O4@ZnO/g-C3N4,503 BiPO4/TiO2/g-C3N4,504 (C16H33(CH3)3N)4W10O32/g-C3N4/rGO,505 RuO2/C3N4@rGO,506 g-C3N4/SiO2–Au,507 GQDs/PANI/g-C3N4,508 PANI/g-C3N4/TiO2,509 g-C3N4/C@Bi2MoO6,510 g-C3N4/Bi2O2CO3/CoFe2O4,370 and many more. All of them have been investigated as a highly efficient visible-light photocatalyst with enhanced charge migration and separation for photocatalytic HER. It is believed that an effective combination of multiple components with different functions will greatly improve visible-light harvesting and facilitate the separation of the photogenerated electron–hole pairs, and accordingly high HER performance.
5. Conclusions and outlook
Polymeric g-C3N4 as a semiconductor is the “holy grail” as the next generation visible-light-induced photocatalysts for HER application because of its facile synthesis, attractive electronic band structure, high physicochemical stability, and “earth-abundant” feature. The research hotspots in the field of g-C3N4-based photocatalysts are focused on the design and development of g-C3N4-based photocatalysts with remarkably improved photocatalytic properties and visible-light utilization. Usually, the improvement of the photocatalytic properties and visible-light utilization of g-C3N4-based photocatalysts can be achieved by three important criteria: (i) enlarging the light absorption toward the visible-light region or even near-infrared region, (ii) impeding the recombination rate of the photoinduced charge carriers, and (iii) increasing the amount of adsorbed reactant species on the photocatalyst surface. Inspired by these principles, strategies such as forming thinner, highly crystalline, doped, and porous g-C3N4 have been used to enhance the photocatalytic properties of g-C3N4 photocatalysts. Apart from the rapid development of these promoted techniques on intrinsic g-C3N4, the formation of g-C3N4 composite heterojunctions can also ameliorate the photocatalytic performance of g-C3N4-based photocatalysts.
Although many groundbreaking results have been reported so far, the studies in this hot area are still in an initial stage and further developments are evidently required. Remarkably, the reported g-C3N4-based photocatalysts still suffer from relatively low photocatalytic efficiency and visible-light utilization, which are far from the needs of practical application. Therefore, it is vital for the rational design and development of novel g-C3N4-based photocatalysts to further cater to the practical demands. Here, some new challenges and insights are discussed in the following aspects:
(i) A more comprehensive understanding of the photocatalytic reaction mechanism is necessary for further amelioration and improvement in the photocatalytic performance, which is advantageous for guiding us to rationally design and develop highly efficient g-C3N4-based photocatalysts. Besides the widely accepted charge carrier transfer mechanism, the thermodynamics and kinetics of surface catalytic reactions also need more comprehensive investigations. Some new characterization techniques and theoretical calculations may serve as extremely useful tools toward this purpose, which may significantly deepen our understanding on the structure–activity relationship of the g-C3N4-based photocatalysts.
(ii) Modifications of the surface/textural properties and bandgap configuration of intrinsic g-C3N4 still require further exploration to achieve the optimized optical and photocatalytic performances. The specific surface area of all reported g-C3N4 nanosheets is still much smaller than the theoretical value (2500 m2 g−1) of ideal monolayer g-C3N4, and a perfectly crystalline structure of g-C3N4 is hard to obtain. Monolayered g-C3N4 nanosheets and perfectly crystalline g-C3N4 are expected to be two hot research topics in future because they are beneficial for us to analyze their specific structure and achieve the optimal photocatalytic performance.
(iii) In addition to specific surface area, a 3D hierarchical architecture composed of large mesopores connected with macropores is also very important for g-C3N4 to achieve high photocatalytic activity. Macropores can serve as an efficient light-transfer path to introduce incident photon flux into the connected inner mesopores, enabling light to penetrate deeply inside the photocatalyst. As a result, light absorption, reflection and scattering within a hierarchical nanostructure would greatly increase the light-activated surface area. The interconnected macropores and large mesopores not only facilitate mass transport, but also have a multiple-reflection effect on incident light, resulting in enhanced multilight scattering and light harvesting under visible light irradiation, and accordingly considerably improved photocatalytic performance. Therefore, some synthesis strategies which can create a 3D hierarchical nanostructure for g-C3N4-based photocatalysts are highly desired.
(iv) g-C3N4 composite photocatalysts still require further exploration to achieve better photocatalytic performance and visible-light utilization, lower fabrication cost and little-to-no pollution to the environment. Therefore, in the process of developing g-C3N4 composite photocatalysts, not only should we consider the photocatalytic activity, stability and visible-light utilization efficiency, but also take into account the production and environmental costs (a detailed discussion is given below).
(v) There is a very interesting phenomenon, that is, almost all studies about g-C3N4-based photocatalysts for HER are focused on the design and development of novel g-C3N4-based photocatalysts with high efficiency and stability. In these studies, the enhancement mechanism is nearly identical, namely, efficient separation of the photogenerated electron–hole pairs. Although this is correct, it is not in-depth and specific enough. Some physical and theoretically calculated technologies are encouraged to be used in this field. The exploration of the essence of g-C3N4-based photocatalysts through theoretical computation and modeling could further guide us to rationally design highly efficient HER photocatalysts, and thus is also particularly important.
(vi) To further enhance the photocatalytic efficiency of highly efficient g-C3N4-based photocatalysts for large scale photocatalytic HER application, photoreactor engineering is particularly important and highly desired. When a novel photoreactor (e.g., fluidized bed photoreactor) is used for a photocatalytic process, it can provide improved mass transfer, outstanding radiation distribution, and enhanced photocatalyst surface area-to-volume ratio. Through maximising mass transfer and visible light utilization, such novel fluidized photoreactors can significantly improve the photocatalytic performance of g-C3N4-based photocatalysts by eliminating the limitation in the photocatalytic efficiency in traditional photoreactors.514 Up to now, there have been only a few publications that explore novel photoreactors for g-C3N4-based photocatalysts. Therefore, there is a great necessity for engineering photoreactors to realize large scale industrial photocatalytic HER application.
(vii) Another urgent and important issue needed to be addressed is the inconsistency of photocatalytic performance tests, such as the different usage amounts of photocatalysts, cocatalysts and sacrificial reagents, and the different types of cocatalysts, sacrificial reagents and light sources, etc. (see Tables 3 and 4), which makes it virtually impossible to compare fairly the photocatalytic performance of various g-C3N4-based photocatalysts. Thus, standardization of photocatalytic performance tests is also highly worth considering.
In a comprehensive consideration of the catalytic efficiency and fabrication cost, here, we summarize and compare a series of representative g-C3N4-based photocatalysts to obtain some new views and insights. Among the representative g-C3N4-based photocatalysts (Table 5), sensitizer/g-C3N4 has shown the highest H2 evolution rate (e.g., 24760 μmol h−1 g−1 reported for the LI-4/g-C3N4/Zn-tri-PcNc).481 Moreover, enlarged visible-light absorption ensures that they have great potential for large scale industrial application. However, it seems to be detrimental to soil and water sources if the sensitizer is used in a large quantity. In addition, the synthesis of the sensitizer is generally complicated and time consuming, resulting in high cost. Semiconductors may be a better choice because of their wide variety, outstanding HER performance (e.g., 16400 μmol h−1 g−1 for NiS/g-C3N4323 and 18836 μmol h−1 g−1 for N-doped ZnO/g-C3N4 nanosheets298) and relatively low cost, but similarly, semiconductors particularly those containing heavy metal elements potentially cause environmental pollutions and biological hazards. Although nanostructured intrinsic g-C3N4 has revealed a moderate H2 evolution rate (e.g., 9577 μmol h−1 g−1 reported for the highly crystalline g-C3N4),211 which is about one-third of the highest one (e.g., 27035 μmol h−1 g−1),220 the relatively harsh synthetic conditions severely limit its application. Very recently, an enlightening g-C3N4-based photocatalyst was reported by Neppolian's group,492 who combined low-cost, nontoxic, photoactive and photostable TiO2 and graphene with a large surface area and high electron mobility to prepare rGO/g-C3N4/TiO2, which possessed very high H2 evolution rate (e.g., 23143 μmol h−1 g−1). Therefore, it is believed that an effective combination of multiple components with different functions will greatly improve visible-light harvesting and facilitate the separation of the photogenerated electron–hole pairs, and accordingly high HER performance. In addition, surface functionalization of g-C3N4 is also highly worth considering because it can regulate the surface charge with specific surface groups to strengthen the anchoring ability of g-C3N4, with a view to reduce nanoparticle aggregation and thus achieving higher catalytic activity and stability. Surely, 3D hierarchical self-doped g-C3N4 architectures also achieve an ultrahigh H2 evolution rate (27035 μmol h−1 g−1) and apparent quantum efficiency (7.8%) at 420 nm. This is mainly because: (1) 3D hierarchical nanostructures can significantly promote the photogenerated charge separation and transfer, resulting in a much longer lifetime of the charge carriers; (2) 3D hierarchical nanostructures can produce multiple light scattering, which facilitates light harvesting; and (3) 3D nanostructures provide more exposed active catalytic sites for surface reactions, which acts as a pivotal part in shortening the charge migration distance. Moreover, extremely facile hexamethylenetetramine activation strategy also ensures it have a great potential for a large scale industrial application. Thus, 3D hierarchical g-C3N4 nanostructures are highly desired for catering to the actual production demand in the future work.

With an eye to the future, a great number of opportunities and challenges are presented for this booming and hot field. We believe that this review article will be able to provide a strategic guide to reasonably design and develop novel g-C3N4-based photocatalysts with high activity, stability and visible-light utilization efficiency, environmental friendliness and low-cost for visible-light photocatalytic HER application. It is believed that enlarging the knowledge on fundamental aspects through more physicochemical theoretical research studies combined with experimental results will energetically bring new discoveries in materials science and technologies. Therefore, with more research findings in g-C3N4-based photocatalysts for HER application obtained from various fields, the bottleneck and issues will be fully addressed. There is no doubt that the emerging g-C3N4-based photocatalysts will be regarded as the “holy grail” for achieving highly efficient photocatalytic H2 generation under visible-light.

Guangfu Liao
	Guangfu Liao received his MSc in Polymer Materials and Engineering from Hubei University in 2017. He then joined Prof. Haiyang Gao's group at Sun Yat-sen University to pursue PhD in Materials Science. His primary research is focused on Ag-based catalysts, nanocomposites, biomaterials, and coordination polymerization. He has more than 6 years of research experience in the fields of catalysis, environment and biomaterials. He has published over 20 SCI papers, and also received some awards including the Graduate Student National Scholarship, Hubei University First-class Academic Scholarship, Hubei University Outstanding Graduate & Excellent Graduation Thesis, and Sun Yat-sen University Second-class Academic Scholarship.

Yan Gong
	Yan Gong was born in Anhui, China, in 1996. She received her BSc in Chemistry and Chemical Engineering from Fuzhou University in 2017. In 2018, she worked as a visiting scholar at Prof. Rodney. S. Ruoff's group at the IBS Center for Multidimensional Carbon Materials. Currently, she is working in Prof. Da Zhan’ research group at Xiamen University to pursue her master's degree in Materials Science. Her primary research is focused on the development of high performance graphite-like C3N4 nanosheets for visible-light-driven photocatalysis applications.

Li Zhang
	Li Zhang earned his bachelor's degree in Materials Science and Engineering and Master's degree in Polymer Chemistry and Physics from Hubei University in 2014 and 2017, respectively. Then he worked as a research assistant at Hong Kong Polytechnic University from 2017 to 2018, and now he is a PhD student at City University of Hong Kong. His research interests are devoted to designing and fabricating various organic/inorganic hybrid nanomaterials, and investigating their applications especially in photo-induced nanomedicine.

Haiyang Gao
	Dr Haiyang Gao received his PhD in Polymer Chemistry and Physics from Sun Yat-sen University in 2005 under the supervision of Prof. Qing Wu. He then worked as a lecturer in the same university. After working as a postdoctoral research fellow in 2009 at the University of California, Santa Barbara, he moved back to Sun Yat-sen University, and is currently a full professor at the School of Materials Science and Engineering. His research interests mainly include organometallic catalysts, catalytic olefin polymerization and polyolefins. He has authored more than 100 peer-reviewed papers and 15 patents.

Guan-Jun Yang
	Dr Guan-Jun Yang earned his PhD in Materials Science from Xi’an Jiaotong University (XJTU) in 2005. He joined XJTU as a faculty in 2002, and now is a professor and director of the Center for Thermal Spray and Hybrid Materials, State Key Laboratory for Mechanical Behavior of Materials. He has published over 200 peer-reviewed papers. He also serves as an editorial board member of several scientific journals including Journal of Materials Science and Technology. His research interests include nanomaterials and coatings/films for clean energy and environment.

Baizeng Fang
	Dr Baizeng Fang earned his PhD in Materials Science from the University of Science and Technology, Beijing, in 1997. He then worked as a JSPS research fellow (Japan), Lise Meitner scientist (Austria), and Research Professor (Korea), and is a Senior Scientist at the University of British Columbia (Canada). He has published over 100 SCI papers in high-profile journals including reviews in Accounts of Chemical Research, Chemical Reviews, Chemical Society Reviews, Energy & Environmental Science and Progress in Materials Science. His research interests include nanostructured materials for artificial photosynthesis and electrochemical energy storage/conversion. He also serves as an Associate Editor for RSC Advances.

Broader context
Exploitation of clean and renewable energy is a crucial strategy to satisfy the growing global energy demands and solve the environmental problems caused by the over-consumption of fossil fuels. Photocatalytic hydrogen production from water splitting by solar energy has been widely recognized as a promising avenue for sustainable development. Semiconductor polymeric graphitic carbon nitride (g-C3N4) photocatalysts have attracted dramatically growing interest in the research area of the visible-light-induced hydrogen evolution reaction (HER) because of their facile synthesis, easy functionalization, attractive electronic band structure, high physicochemical stability and particularly unique visible-light responsiveness, which are believed to be the “holy grail” for photocatalytic HER. To effectively reduce the recombination rate of charge carriers and simultaneously enhance visible-light absorption above 460 nm, a series of nanostructured g-C3N4 photocatalysts (e.g., thinner, highly crystalline, doped, and porous g-C3N4) and highly efficient g-C3N4 composite photocatalysts (e.g., metal/g-C3N4, semiconductor/g-C3N4, metal organic framework/g-C3N4, carbon/g-C3N4, etc.) have been explored. They can effectively modify the electronic band structure of g-C3N4, widen the light absorption range and facilitate the separation of photo-generated electron–hole pairs, leading to considerably reduced recombination rate of charge carriers, enlarging visible light harvesting and utilization, and thus significantly improving HER activity under visible-light irradiation.

Supramolecular pre-assembly strategy	Produced g-C3N4 with an ordered texture and controllable morphology	(i) Low efficiency, time- and cost-consuming, and sophisticated
(ii) Produced g-C3N4 with a relatively low crystallinity
Microwave-assisted heating strategy	(i) Facile, time-saving, and highly efficient	Produced g-C3N4 without an ordered texture, controllable morphology and porous structure
(ii) Produced g-C3N4 with a relatively high crystallinity, thinner layer, and decreased structural imperfections
Template-assisted strategy	Produced g-C3N4 with porous structure and high crystallinity	Time- and cost-consuming and complicated preparation process
Molten salt strategy	Produced g-C3N4 with high crystallinity	Time- and cost-consuming and complicated preparation process
Ionic liquid strategy	Produced g-C3N4 with porous structure, thinner layer, and high crystallinity	Time- and cost-consuming, complicated preparation process, and low efficiency
Chemical exfoliation strategy	(i) Facile, time-saving, and highly efficient	(i) The danger of strong acid and not eco-friendly
(ii) Produced g-C3N4 with a relatively high crystallinity, and thinner layer	(ii) Produced g-C3N4 without an ordered texture, controllable morphology and porous structure
Hydrothermal pretreatment strategy	(i) Facile, eco-friendly, and highly efficient	Complicated preparation process
(ii) Produced g-C3N4 with a relatively high crystallinity, and thinner layer
Fast flash frozen strategy	(i) Fast, high effect, and eco-friendly	Complicated preparation process and expansive equipment
(ii) Produced g-C3N4 with a relatively high crystallinity

(e) H2 production over pristine g-C3N4, carbon@g-C3N4, and carbon sphere photocatalysts. (f) Electron–hole pairs in the carbon spheres are generated under visible-light illumination. Based on the relevant position of the band, the electrons inject into g-C3N4, and (g) carbon spheres as an electron sink to scavenge away the electrons, thus hindering the recombination of electron–hole pairs. This figure has been reproduced from ref. 456 with permission from the American Chemical Society.

(b) Mott–Schottky plots of (1) g-C3N4, (2) 3 wt% C-PAN/g-C3N4, (3) 5 wt% C-PAN/g-C3N4, and (4) 10 wt% C-PAN/g-C3N4 composite nanotubes. (c) Diagrammatic sketch of the electronic band structure of the g-C3N4 and C-PAN composites.
